Part I
Single Cell Dynamics
From the squid giant axon to the human ventricular myocyte — how ion channels generate the electrical signals that drive every heartbeat.
Chapter 1

The Hodgkin–Huxley Revolution

In 1952, Alan Hodgkin and Andrew Huxley did something no one thought possible: they wrote down a mathematical model of the nerve impulse that was so precise it predicted experiments that hadn't been done yet. Every cardiac cell model used today — from the simplest to the most complex — is a direct descendant of their work.

1.1   The Membrane as a Circuit

A cell membrane is, at its core, a thin lipid bilayer — roughly 7 nm of oily molecules separating two conducting salt solutions. This structure has a remarkable electrical consequence: the membrane acts as a capacitor. Like two metal plates separated by an insulator, the membrane stores charge — positive ions lining up on one side, negative on the other.

But the membrane is not a perfect insulator. Embedded within it are ion channels — protein pores that allow specific ions (Na$^+$, K$^+$, Ca$^{2+}$, Cl$^-$) to flow through when open. Each channel type acts like a variable resistor: its conductance changes with voltage, time, and chemical signals. At any instant, the total current flowing across the membrane is the sum of the capacitive current (charging the membrane) and the ionic current (flowing through channels):

(1.1) $$I_m = C_m \frac{dV_m}{dt} + I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, t)$$

Here $C_m \approx 1\;\mu\text{F/cm}^2$ is the specific membrane capacitance (remarkably constant across all cell types), $V_m$ is the transmembrane potential, and $I_{\text{ion}}$ is the total ionic current — the quantity that Hodgkin and Huxley set out to understand.

Figure 1.1. The Hodgkin–Huxley equivalent circuit. The membrane capacitance $C_m$ sits in parallel with voltage-dependent conductances for Na$^+$, K$^+$, and a leak current. Each ionic branch has a variable conductance $g_x$ in series with a Nernst battery $E_x$.

The key insight of Figure 1.1 is that each ionic species contributes its own current $I_x = g_x(V_m - E_x)$, where $E_x$ is the reversal potential — the voltage at which the electrochemical gradient for that ion vanishes. For sodium, $E_{\text{Na}} \approx +65$ mV; for potassium, $E_{\text{K}} \approx -88$ mV. The enormous 150 mV difference between these two batteries is the energy source that powers every action potential.

Think of each ion channel as a door with a bouncer who checks the voltage. At rest ($V_m \approx -85$ mV), the Na$^+$ doors are nearly all shut and the K$^+$ doors are mostly open. The cell sits quietly at a voltage close to $E_{\text{K}}$. But if something pushes $V_m$ above about $-55$ mV, the Na$^+$ doors suddenly swing open — and the positive feedback loop that creates an action potential begins.

1.2   Gating Variables: m, h, and n

Hodgkin and Huxley's genius was not the circuit diagram — that was known. It was their decision to treat each conductance as a product of independent "gates," each opening and closing with its own voltage-dependent kinetics. For the squid axon, they found:

$$g_{\text{Na}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Na}} \cdot m^3 \cdot h, \qquad g_{\text{K}} = \bar{g}_{\text{K}} \cdot n^4$$

Here $\bar{g}_{\text{Na}}$ and $\bar{g}_{\text{K}}$ are the maximal conductances — what you'd measure if every channel were fully open. The gating variables $m$, $h$, and $n$ each range between 0 (all gates closed) and 1 (all gates open). They obey first-order kinetics:

(1.2) $$\frac{dx}{dt} = \alpha_x(V_m)(1 - x) - \beta_x(V_m)\,x, \qquad x \in \{m, h, n\}$$

where $\alpha_x$ and $\beta_x$ are voltage-dependent rate constants that Hodgkin and Huxley measured experimentally from the squid giant axon. This equation says something beautifully simple: each gate transitions between a closed state (population $1-x$) and an open state (population $x$), with forward rate $\alpha_x$ and backward rate $\beta_x$.

It is often more revealing to rewrite equation (1.2) in relaxation form:

(1.3) $$\frac{dx}{dt} = \frac{x_\infty(V_m) - x}{\tau_x(V_m)}$$

where $x_\infty = \alpha_x / (\alpha_x + \beta_x)$ is the steady-state value (where the gate would end up if voltage were held constant forever) and $\tau_x = 1/(\alpha_x + \beta_x)$ is the time constant (how fast it gets there). This form makes the physics transparent: the gate exponentially approaches its target $x_\infty(V_m)$ with time constant $\tau_x(V_m)$.

The relaxation form (1.3) is not just prettier notation — it is the foundation of the Rush–Larsen method, the standard numerical integrator for gating variables in cardiac simulations. We will meet it again in Chapter 8 when we discuss operator splitting.

Figure 1.2. Steady-state gating curves for the HH model. At rest, $m_\infty \approx 0$ (Na$^+$ activation off), $h_\infty \approx 0.6$ (Na$^+$ inactivation partially available), and $n_\infty \approx 0.3$ (K$^+$ partially open). The Na$^+$ activation curve $m_\infty$ is notably steeper than $n_\infty$, explaining why sodium channels open faster than potassium channels — the key to the action potential upstroke.

What do these three gates mean physically? The variable $m$ is the sodium activation gate: it controls how quickly Na$^+$ channels open when the membrane depolarizes. The variable $h$ is the sodium inactivation gate: it shuts Na$^+$ channels back down, even if the voltage stays high. The variable $n$ is the potassium activation gate: it opens K$^+$ channels slowly during depolarization to repolarize the cell.

The powers ($m^3$ and $n^4$) reflect the cooperativity of channel gating — Hodgkin and Huxley interpreted them as the number of independent "particles" that must simultaneously be in the right position for the channel to conduct. Modern structural biology has confirmed this: voltage-gated channels have four voltage-sensing domains, and the sodium channel's fast gate involves three of them.

1.3   The Sodium Hypothesis and Positive Feedback

The action potential is a self-amplifying event, and understanding why it fires requires tracing a feedback loop. Suppose a small stimulus pushes $V_m$ slightly above the threshold of about $-55$ mV. Here is what happens:

Step 1: The small depolarization causes $m_\infty$ to increase (the $m_\infty$ curve in Figure 1.2 is steeply rising near threshold). Gates start opening: $m$ rises toward $m_\infty$.

Step 2: As $m$ increases, the sodium conductance $g_{\text{Na}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Na}} m^3 h$ grows rapidly. Na$^+$ ions rush into the cell (driven by the large electrochemical gradient $V_m - E_{\text{Na}} \approx -55 - 65 = -120$ mV).

Step 3: The inward Na$^+$ current further depolarizes the membrane, which causes even more $m$-gates to open — and the process accelerates explosively.

This is positive feedback: depolarization opens Na$^+$ gates, which cause more depolarization, which opens more gates. It is the biological equivalent of a spark igniting gasoline. Once started, the upstroke is essentially unstoppable — $V_m$ shoots from $-85$ mV toward $E_{\text{Na}} \approx +65$ mV in less than 1 ms. This is the "all-or-nothing" nature of the action potential.

What stops this runaway? Two mechanisms, operating on different timescales:

Fast shutdown — inactivation ($h$): While $m$ opens in under 0.5 ms, the inactivation gate $h$ closes with a time constant of 1–5 ms. As $h \to 0$, the product $m^3 h \to 0$ even though $m$ is large. The Na$^+$ current self-terminates. This is the fundamental asymmetry: $m$ is fast, $h$ is slower.

Slow recovery — potassium ($n$): The K$^+$ gate $n$ opens even more slowly ($\tau_n \approx$ 5–10 ms). As $n^4$ grows, the outward K$^+$ current $I_K = \bar{g}_K n^4 (V_m - E_K)$ overwhelms the now-extinct Na$^+$ current and pulls $V_m$ back toward $E_K$.

1.4   Activation vs. Inactivation: The Refractory Period

After an action potential, the cell enters a refractory period during which it cannot fire again. This is essential for ordered wave propagation — without it, waves could travel backward and the heart would fibrillate. The refractory period has a clear gating-variable explanation:

Immediately after the action potential, $h \approx 0$ (Na$^+$ channels are inactivated) and $n$ is still elevated (K$^+$ channels are open). Even if a new stimulus arrives, there are no Na$^+$ channels available to open — the $h$ gate needs time to recover ($h$ must return toward $h_\infty(V_{\text{rest}}) \approx 0.6$). This recovery takes 5–10 ms in nerve, and 200–300 ms in ventricular cardiac cells.

Figure 1.3. The nerve action potential (squid axon). The upstroke is driven by Na$^+$ influx ($m^3h$ activating), the repolarization by Na$^+$ inactivation ($h \to 0$) and delayed K$^+$ activation ($n^4$ increasing). The refractory period corresponds to the time needed for $h$ to recover.

In Engine V5.4, every ionic model inherits from the IonicModel abstract base class (cardiac_sim/ionic/base.py), which requires: compute_Iion(V, ionic_states) to compute total ionic current, step(V, ionic_states, dt) to advance the full model, and separate methods compute_gate_steady_states() and compute_gate_time_constants() so that the Rush–Larsen solver can update gates externally. V is always stored separately from ionic states. The Hodgkin–Huxley structure — membrane current as sum of conductances times driving forces, gating variables following first-order kinetics — is exactly the pattern this interface encodes. Understanding HH means understanding the API contract of every model in the engine.

The complete Hodgkin–Huxley system is four coupled ordinary differential equations: one for $V_m$ (the circuit equation) and three for the gates ($m$, $h$, $n$). It was the first biophysically detailed cell model and remains the conceptual template for all that followed. But for the heart, we need something quite different — as we'll see in the next chapter.

Chapter 2

From Neurons to Heart Cells

The nerve action potential is a brief spark — over in 2 milliseconds. The cardiac action potential is a sustained flame, lasting 200–400 ms. This chapter explains why the heart demands such a profoundly different electrical signature, and what new biophysics makes it possible.

2.1   Why the Heart Needs a Longer Action Potential

A nerve fiber has one job: transmit a binary signal from point A to point B as fast as possible. It fires and recovers quickly so it can fire again. But a cardiac myocyte has a very different job: it must contract, and contraction takes time. The heart needs the electrical signal to outlast the mechanical event so that every cell stays activated long enough to squeeze in synchrony.

If the cardiac AP were as short as a nerve spike, the cell would repolarize before it finished contracting. Worse, a new electrical impulse could arrive and re-activate parts of the heart while other parts were still contracting — leading to chaotic, uncoordinated contraction. The long AP duration serves as a built-in safety mechanism: by keeping the cell depolarized for hundreds of milliseconds, it prevents premature re-excitation.

Think of it this way: the nerve AP is like a camera flash — bright, instantaneous, done. The cardiac AP is like holding a light switch on for a deliberate period. The heart needs this "held" state because mechanical contraction is slow compared to electrical activation. The electrical plateau is the heart's way of saying "stay contracted until I tell you to relax."

2.2   The L-type Calcium Current and the Plateau

So how does the heart stretch a 2 ms spike into a 300 ms plateau? The answer is a current that neurons barely use: the L-type calcium current, $I_{\text{CaL}}$. This inward current activates during depolarization (like Na$^+$), but it inactivates much more slowly — on a timescale of 50–200 ms rather than 1–2 ms.

During the plateau phase (Phase 2 of the cardiac AP), the membrane sits at roughly 0 to +20 mV because two opposing forces are nearly balanced:

$$\underbrace{I_{\text{CaL}}}_{\text{inward, depolarizing}} \approx -\underbrace{I_{\text{Kr}} + I_{\text{Ks}}}_{\text{outward, repolarizing}}$$

The L-type Ca$^{2+}$ current holds the voltage up, while slowly activating delayed-rectifier K$^+$ currents ($I_{\text{Kr}}$ and $I_{\text{Ks}}$) try to pull it down. This tug-of-war creates a nearly flat plateau that can last over 200 ms. Eventually, as $I_{\text{CaL}}$ inactivates (both via voltage and via rising intracellular Ca$^{2+}$), the K$^+$ currents win and repolarization accelerates.

The plateau is a fragile equilibrium. A small change in any current during Phase 2 can dramatically alter AP duration. This is why drug-induced QT prolongation (blocking $I_{\text{Kr}}$) is so dangerous — it tips the balance toward inward current, lengthens the plateau, and can trigger the lethal arrhythmia torsades de pointes. Understanding this balance is central to computational drug safety testing.

The L-type calcium current also serves a second critical role: it triggers the release of a much larger amount of calcium from the sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR) — the cell's internal calcium store. This process, called calcium-induced calcium release (CICR), amplifies the Ca$^{2+}$ signal roughly 10-fold and drives actual muscle contraction. We will see this calcium handling machinery in detail when we study the TTP06 model in Chapter 5.

2.3   Minimal Cardiac Models: FitzHugh–Nagumo to Fenton–Karma

Before diving into the full complexity of modern ionic models, it is instructive to see how the cardiac AP can be captured with just 2–3 variables. These minimal models sacrifice biophysical detail for mathematical tractability, but they capture the essential shape.

The FitzHugh–Nagumo Model (2 variables)

In 1961, Richard FitzHugh realized that the HH equations could be distilled to their essence. The key insight: the fast variable (Na$^+$ activation) and the slow variable (recovery) are what matter most. He proposed:

(2.1) $$\frac{dv}{dt} = v - \tfrac{v^3}{3} - w + I_{\text{ext}}, \qquad \frac{dw}{dt} = \epsilon(v + a - bw)$$

Here $v$ is a rescaled voltage, $w$ is a slow recovery variable (combining $h$ and $n$ into a single "how recovered is the cell" variable), and $\epsilon \ll 1$ ensures that $w$ moves much slower than $v$. The cubic nonlinearity $v - v^3/3$ creates the bistability needed for excitation: $v$ has two stable branches (resting and excited) and the system jumps between them.

FitzHugh–Nagumo captures excitability, threshold behavior, and refractoriness — but it produces a triangular AP, not the flat plateau of a cardiac cell.

The Fenton–Karma Model (3 variables)

In 1998, Flavio Fenton and Alain Karma introduced a three-variable model specifically designed for cardiac tissue. The key addition: a slow inward current that produces the plateau.

(2.2) $$\frac{dV}{dt} = -\bigl(I_{\text{fi}}(V,v) + I_{\text{so}}(V) + I_{\text{si}}(V,w)\bigr)$$

The three currents map directly to cardiac physiology: $I_{\text{fi}}$ is a fast inward current (like Na$^+$ creating the upstroke), $I_{\text{so}}$ is a slow outward current (like K$^+$ causing repolarization), and $I_{\text{si}}$ is a slow inward current (like Ca$^{2+}$ sustaining the plateau). With just three variables ($V$, $v$, $w$), the Fenton–Karma model reproduces realistic AP shapes, spiral waves, and even alternans — all phenomena seen in real cardiac tissue.

Minimal models like Fenton–Karma are not toys. In Engine V5.4, they serve as essential validation benchmarks: if the monodomain solver produces the correct spiral-wave dynamics with a 3-variable model, you can have confidence in the diffusion numerics before scaling up to TTP06 (18 variables) or ORd (40 variables). They are also used for parameter sweeps and machine-learning studies where millions of simulations are needed.

The progression from HH (4 variables, nerve) → FitzHugh–Nagumo (2 variables, generic excitable) → Fenton–Karma (3 variables, cardiac) → full ionic models (18–40+ variables, human ventricle) is the story of matching model complexity to the scientific question being asked. In the next chapter, we zoom in on the full cardiac action potential and dissect each phase.

Chapter 3

Anatomy of the Cardiac Action Potential

The ventricular action potential is traditionally divided into five phases (0 through 4). Each phase has a dominant current, a clear biophysical story, and direct consequences for the heart's electrical and mechanical function. Understanding these phases is the Rosetta Stone for reading any cardiac cell model. Chapter 5 provides the complete mathematical formulations for all 12 membrane currents in the TTP06 model; here we build the qualitative intuition first.
Figure 3.1. The five phases of the human ventricular action potential with dominant currents. Phase 0: Na$^+$ upstroke. Phase 1: transient outward K$^+$ (notch). Phase 2: Ca$^{2+}$-K$^+$ plateau. Phase 3: K$^+$-driven repolarization. Phase 4: resting potential maintained by $I_{\text{K1}}$.

3.1   Phase 0: The Sodium Upstroke

Phase 0 is the fastest event in cardiac electrophysiology. When an arriving wavefront brings $V_m$ above threshold ($\approx -65$ mV), sodium channels undergo the same explosive positive feedback we saw in the HH model: $m$-gates open rapidly, Na$^+$ floods inward, and $V_m$ rockets from $-85$ mV to about $+40$ mV in roughly 1–2 ms.

The maximum rate of rise, $dV/dt_{\text{max}}$, is a clinical measure of sodium channel availability. In healthy ventricular tissue, $dV/dt_{\text{max}} \approx 200{-}300$ V/s. This is also what determines conduction velocity — the faster the upstroke, the larger the local current source that can depolarize neighboring cells, and the faster the wave propagates. In the monodomain equation (Chapter 7), this translates directly to the source term.

3.2   Phase 1: Early Repolarization ($I_{\text{to}}$)

Immediately after the peak, a brief notch appears — a small dip in voltage before the plateau. This is caused by the transient outward potassium current $I_{\text{to}}$, which activates rapidly during depolarization and inactivates within tens of milliseconds.

Phase 1 may look like a minor detail, but it has major consequences. The depth of the notch varies across the ventricular wall: epicardial cells (outer surface) have large $I_{\text{to}}$ and a deep notch, while endocardial cells (inner surface) have little $I_{\text{to}}$ and almost no notch. This transmural gradient in AP morphology creates voltage differences across the wall that are visible on the ECG — the J-wave and T-wave both depend on it.

3.3   Phase 2: The Plateau — A Delicate Balance

The plateau is the heart's signature. For 150–250 ms, the membrane voltage hovers between $-10$ and $+20$ mV, sustained by the near-cancellation of two opposing current groups:

Inward (depolarizing): The L-type calcium current $I_{\text{CaL}}$ is the dominant plateau current. Ca$^{2+}$ ions enter through slowly inactivating channels, holding the voltage up. A smaller late sodium current ($I_{\text{NaL}}$) also contributes, especially in disease states.

Outward (repolarizing): Two delayed-rectifier potassium currents slowly activate: $I_{\text{Kr}}$ (rapid component, carried by hERG channels) and $I_{\text{Ks}}$ (slow component). The inward-rectifier $I_{\text{K1}}$ is largely blocked at plateau voltages due to its voltage dependence — it will become important again in Phase 3–4.

The plateau balance explains why the cardiac AP duration (APD) is so sensitive to perturbations. Block 30% of $I_{\text{Kr}}$ (as many common drugs do), and the plateau suddenly lacks enough repolarizing current. APD extends, the QT interval on the ECG lengthens, and the risk of arrhythmia rises. This is why in silico drug testing with models like TTP06 and ORd has become a regulatory focus (the CiPA initiative).

3.4   Phase 3: Repolarization — The Potassium Takeover

As $I_{\text{CaL}}$ gradually inactivates (driven both by voltage-dependent inactivation and by rising intracellular Ca$^{2+}$ concentration), the balance tips irreversibly toward outward current. The K$^+$ currents $I_{\text{Kr}}$ and $I_{\text{Ks}}$, which have been slowly building, now dominate. As the voltage falls, $I_{\text{K1}}$ — the strong inward rectifier that was blocked at plateau voltages — begins to unblock and adds its large outward conductance to the repolarization.

Phase 3 repolarization is regenerative: as $V_m$ falls, more $I_{\text{K1}}$ unblocks, which pulls $V_m$ down further, which unblocks more $I_{\text{K1}}$. This positive feedback (now outward) ensures that repolarization, once started, proceeds rapidly to completion. The membrane returns to its resting potential of about $-85$ mV.

3.5   Phase 4: Rest and the Role of $I_{\text{K1}}$

At rest, the membrane potential sits near $E_K \approx -88$ mV because $I_{\text{K1}}$ dominates the conductance. The "inward rectifier" name refers to a peculiar property: $I_{\text{K1}}$ passes large outward current at voltages just below $E_K$, but essentially zero current at plateau voltages. This voltage-dependent block (caused by intracellular magnesium and polyamines plugging the channel pore) is what allows the plateau to exist — without it, K$^+$ would short-circuit the plateau and the AP would collapse.

Think of $I_{\text{K1}}$ as a two-state switch. At rest, it is "on" — a strong anchor holding $V_m$ near $E_K$. During the plateau, it switches "off" — stepping aside to let the delicate Ca$^{2+}$/K$^+$ balance play out. Then in Phase 3, it switches back "on" to slam the door shut and restore the resting potential. No other current has this dual role.

With the five phases clearly in mind, we now turn to the single most important ion in cardiac physiology: calcium. The next chapter is devoted entirely to the intracellular calcium machinery — from the nanoscale architecture of the dyadic cleft to the pathological remodeling that drives heart failure and arrhythmia.

Chapter 4

Intracellular Calcium: The Master Regulator

Calcium is the singular ion that links electrical excitation to mechanical contraction. But this link is not simple: it involves nanoscale signaling domains, stochastic release events, multi-protein regulatory complexes, and feedback loops that span timescales from microseconds to minutes. This chapter provides a modern, in-depth account of cardiac calcium handling — from the architecture of the dyadic cleft to the pathological remodeling seen in heart failure.

4.1   The Dyadic Cleft — Where Electrical Meets Mechanical

Excitation-contraction (EC) coupling occurs at dyads — nanoscopic junctions formed where transverse tubules (T-tubules) invaginate to within 10–20 nm of the junctional sarcoplasmic reticulum (jSR). This tiny gap — the dyadic cleft — is the reaction chamber where the electrical signal is transduced into a calcium signal. During an action potential, calcium concentration within the cleft surges from a resting $\sim$100 nM to $\sim$10 $\mu$M, a 100-fold increase confined to a space smaller than a virus particle.

The cleft is held together by junctophilin-2 (JPH2), a structural protein that physically anchors the T-tubule membrane to the jSR membrane. JPH2 maintains the critical 10–20 nm separation required for efficient local control coupling. As we will see in Section 4.7, the loss of JPH2 in heart failure is one of the earliest and most consequential structural failures.

Calcium Release Units and Nanoscale Organization

The functional unit of calcium release is the calcium release unit (CRU), also called a couplon. A CRU consists of a cluster of type-2 ryanodine receptors (RyR2) on the jSR membrane, positioned directly beneath a group of L-type calcium channels (Ca$_\text{V}$1.2) on the T-tubule membrane. A typical human ventricular myocyte contains approximately 10,000–20,000 CRUs.

Recent super-resolution imaging — using dSTORM (direct stochastic optical reconstruction microscopy), DNA-PAINT, and most recently MINFLUX microscopy — has revealed the nanoscale architecture of these clusters in unprecedented detail. Key findings include:

RyR2 cluster heterogeneity: A typical CRU contains 4–20 RyR2 tetramers (16–80 individual subunits), but cluster sizes vary widely. RyR2 channels function cohesively as a CRU only when their nearest-neighbor distance (NND) is less than $\sim$100–150 nm. Beyond this threshold, coupling between channels is lost and the release event becomes unreliable.

Sparse channel activation: Despite clusters containing dozens of channels, only 2–4 RyR2 channels typically open simultaneously during a single elementary release event under basal conditions. Beta-adrenergic stimulation increases this to 5–8 channels, providing a mechanism for graded calcium release amplification.

L-type channel organization: Super-resolution studies using STED and DNA-PAINT (2024) revealed that Ca$_\text{V}$1.2 and Ca$_\text{V}$1.3 channels form loose clusters of $\sim$9 molecules within $\sim$120 nm domains, arranged in an irregular pattern with significant intermolecular spacing — not densely packed. This loose arrangement reflects the functional need for positioning (close to RyR2) rather than crowding.

Figure 4.1. Architecture of the dyadic cleft. L-type Ca$^{2+}$ channels on the T-tubule membrane face RyR2 clusters on the jSR, separated by $\sim$15 nm. Junctophilin-2 (JPH2) anchors the two membranes. Triadin (T) and junctin (J) link RyR2 to luminal CASQ2 polymers. SERCA2a pumps Ca$^{2+}$ back into the SR; NCX extrudes Ca$^{2+}$ across the sarcolemma. Only 1–2% of cytoplasmic Ca$^{2+}$ is free.

4.2   Calcium-Induced Calcium Release — Local Control Theory

Calcium Sparks: The Elementary Release Event

A calcium spark is the optical signature of the concerted opening of a small group (typically 2–10) of RyR2 channels within a single CRU. Discovered by Cheng, Lederer, and Bhatt in 1993 using confocal line-scanning and the fluorescent indicator fluo-3, sparks are now understood as the fundamental building blocks of the whole-cell calcium transient.

Each spark has well-defined characteristics: a peak amplitude of 0.5–2 $\mu$M above baseline, a spatial half-width of 1–3 $\mu$M, a rise time of 2–5 ms, and a full-width-at-half-maximum (FWHM) duration of 15–50 ms. During a normal heartbeat, the action potential triggers $\sim$10,000 sparks nearly simultaneously across the myocyte; their spatial and temporal summation produces the global calcium transient that drives contraction.

Think of CRUs as thousands of tiny fireworks launchers spread throughout the cell. When the action potential arrives, it does not light them all at once with a single fuse. Instead, each launcher has its own independent trigger — an L-type channel — that fires with a certain probability ($\sim$30–40% per beat). The global calcium transient is the sum of thousands of probabilistic, independent sparks. This is why calcium release is graded: more L-type current recruits more sparks, producing a larger transient — without the all-or-nothing behavior you might expect from positive feedback.

Figure 4.2. The spark-to-wave transition. Left: Under normal conditions, individual sparks (green) are spatially isolated — positive feedback is confined within each CRU. Right: With SR overload or leaky RyR2, sparks recruit neighboring CRUs, generating a propagating calcium wave that activates NCX and triggers DADs.

Local Control vs. Common Pool

The local control theory, established in the 1990s and continuously refined through 2025, resolves a paradox that troubled the field for decades: how can CICR be both high-gain (a tiny calcium trigger from L-type channels produces a large SR release) and graded (release is proportional to trigger size, not all-or-nothing)?

The answer is spatial isolation. Each RyR2 cluster senses local calcium concentration within its dyadic cleft ($\sim$10 $\mu$M during activation), not the global cytosolic concentration ($\sim$1 $\mu$M). Positive feedback — calcium from one RyR2 opening its neighbors — is confined within the CRU because the cleft's tiny volume ($\sim$10$^{-18}$ L) means even picoampere currents produce micromolar concentrations locally, while rapid diffusion dilutes the signal before it reaches neighboring CRUs $\sim$2 $\mu$m away.

This contrasts with the older common pool model, which assumed a single well-mixed cytoplasmic calcium compartment. Common pool models predict all-or-nothing release — once any RyR2 opens, the resulting calcium elevation should trigger all other RyR2s. Experimentally, this is not what happens: sparks occur at discrete locations without propagating, and the total release scales smoothly with L-type current magnitude. Local control explains both observations.

SR Load: The Central Regulator of Release

The SR calcium content ($[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$, typically 1–2 mM total, of which only $\sim$20–50 $\mu$M is free — the rest bound to calsequestrin) is the single most powerful determinant of release properties. Spark frequency increases exponentially with SR load: doubling the load can increase spontaneous release 5–10-fold. Spark amplitude increases more modestly, while the probability that a given L-type channel opening will trigger a spark (the coupling fidelity) rises steeply with load.

This load-dependence creates an inherent negative feedback: release depletes the store, which reduces the probability of further release. In healthy cells, this feedback stabilizes the system. In disease, when SR load is chronically elevated or RyR2 sensitivity is pathologically increased, the feedback can be overwhelmed — sparks merge into waves, and arrhythmias follow.

The Spark-to-Wave Transition

Under pathological conditions — elevated SR load, increased diastolic calcium, enhanced RyR2 sensitivity — isolated sparks can recruit their neighbors, generating calcium waves that propagate through the myocyte at $\sim$100 $\mu$m/s. The wave front advances by saltatory conduction: a spark at one CRU raises local calcium enough to trigger sparks at neighboring CRUs $\sim$2 $\mu$m away. The critical radius for wave propagation is $\sim$2.7 $\mu$m — below this, the calcium elevation dissipates before reaching the next CRU.

Calcium waves are the primary trigger for delayed afterdepolarizations (DADs). As the wave sweeps through the cell, the elevated cytoplasmic calcium activates the sodium-calcium exchanger (NCX) in forward mode, generating a net inward current that depolarizes the membrane. If this depolarization reaches threshold during diastole, a triggered action potential fires — the cellular mechanism behind many clinical arrhythmias.

4.3   The Ryanodine Receptor — Gatekeeper of the SR

RyR2 is the largest known ion channel ($\sim$2.2 MDa as a homotetramer) and the primary gate controlling calcium release from the SR. Its regulation involves a remarkable convergence of signals: cytoplasmic calcium (activation), luminal calcium (load sensing), phosphorylation by multiple kinases, oxidative modifications, and protein–protein interactions.

Phosphorylation by PKA and CaMKII

Two kinases dominate RyR2 regulation, each targeting a distinct serine residue with different physiological and pathological consequences:

PKA at Serine 2808: During beta-adrenergic stimulation (exercise, stress), PKA phosphorylates RyR2 at S2808, increasing the channel's sensitivity to calcium activation. The channel opens more frequently and requires less trigger calcium. This is the acute "fight-or-flight" enhancement of calcium release. Phosphatase 1 (PP1) rapidly reverses the phosphorylation when adrenergic tone subsides.

CaMKII at Serine 2814: CaMKII, activated by calcium/calmodulin, phosphorylates RyR2 at S2814 with more potent effects on spark frequency than PKA. Critically, CaMKII also increases the channel's sensitivity to luminal calcium, amplifying the load-dependent release mechanism. This creates a positive feedback loop: more calcium → more CaMKII activation → more RyR2 phosphorylation → more calcium release.

Recent work (2023–2025) has established that CaMKII phosphorylation at S2814 — not PKA phosphorylation at S2808 — is the primary driver of pro-arrhythmic calcium leak in chronic heart failure. A 2025 study in Nature Cardiovascular Research found an unexpected twist: preventing RyR2 phosphorylation reduced calcium leak but paradoxically reactivated the sodium current $I_{\text{Na}}$, suggesting a previously unknown crosstalk between RyR2 status and sarcolemmal channel regulation. This has implications for anti-arrhythmic drug design.

Luminal Calcium Sensing: The CASQ2–Triadin–Junctin Complex

RyR2 possesses an intrinsic ability to sense the calcium concentration inside the SR, independent of cytoplasmic calcium. This luminal sensing is mediated by a multi-protein complex anchored to the SR luminal face of RyR2:

Calsequestrin 2 (CASQ2) is the primary SR calcium storage protein, binding $\sim$40–50 calcium ions per molecule across a broad affinity range ($K_d \approx$ 0.1–10 mM). At low luminal $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]$ ($\lt$1 mM), CASQ2 exists as a monomer and directly inhibits RyR2 via triadin and junctin — a molecular "brake" that prevents release from a depleted store. As luminal calcium rises through intermediate concentrations (3–5 mM), CASQ2 undergoes progressive oligomerization: monomer → dimer → polymer. The polymeric form dissociates from the triadin–junctin bridge, relieving RyR2 inhibition and permitting calcium release.

Triadin (primarily the 95 kDa junctional isoform) and junctin are integral SR membrane proteins that serve as molecular scaffolds linking CASQ2 to RyR2. Their ablation causes complete loss of functional CRUs, severe arrhythmias, and cardiomyopathy — underscoring their essential structural role. Mutations in triadin and junctin are now recognized causes of catecholaminergic polymorphic ventricular tachycardia (CPVT).

The CASQ2 sensing mechanism works like a pressure gauge. When the SR "tank" is nearly empty (low luminal calcium), CASQ2 monomers bind to the RyR2 "valve" and hold it shut — preventing futile release from a depleted store. As the tank fills (SERCA pumps calcium back in), CASQ2 polymers form and detach from the valve, allowing it to open on the next trigger. This self-regulating mechanism ensures that release magnitude tracks store content — a form of automatic gain control.

The "Leaky RyR" Hypothesis and Heart Failure

Andrew Marks and colleagues demonstrated that RyR2 dysfunction is central to heart failure pathophysiology through a mechanism now termed the leaky RyR hypothesis. In failing hearts, chronic neurohormonal activation leads to persistent hyperphosphorylation of RyR2 at both S2808 (PKA) and S2814 (CaMKII). This, combined with oxidative modification (S-nitrosylation, sulfhydryl oxidation), increases the channel's diastolic open probability — creating a sustained calcium leak from the SR even when the cell should be at rest.

The consequences cascade: elevated diastolic $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$ ($\sim$200–300 nM vs. normal 50–100 nM) impairs relaxation, activates calcium-dependent proteases (calpains), and drives maladaptive hypertrophic signaling via calcineurin/NFAT. Simultaneously, the increased forward-mode NCX activity needed to extrude the leaked calcium brings excess sodium into the cell, raising $[\text{Na}^+]_i$ and further compromising SERCA efficiency. The leaked SR calcium is also unavailable for systolic release, weakening contraction.

Store-Overload-Induced Calcium Release (SOICR)

When SR calcium load exceeds a critical threshold ($[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}} \gt$ $\sim$1.5 mM free), spontaneous calcium release occurs without any electrical trigger — a phenomenon called SOICR. At such high loads, CASQ2 inhibition is completely relieved, and the tiny amount of calcium that inevitably leaks through any channel is amplified by CICR into full-scale spontaneous waves.

SOICR is the cellular mechanism behind digitalis toxicity (Na/K-ATPase inhibition → sodium overload → reverse NCX → calcium overload → SOICR → arrhythmia), and it is a primary trigger for CPVT — a genetic arrhythmia syndrome caused by RyR2 or CASQ2 mutations that lower the load threshold for spontaneous release. Carvedilol, a nonselective beta-blocker, uniquely suppresses SOICR by directly reducing RyR2 open duration, independent of its beta-blocking properties — making it particularly effective for CPVT and heart failure arrhythmias.

4.4   SERCA2a and Phospholamban — The Pump and Its Brake

The sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium-ATPase 2a (SERCA2a) is the workhorse of diastolic calcium removal, responsible for 70–90% of cytoplasmic calcium clearance in human ventricular myocytes. Understanding its regulation is essential for understanding both normal relaxation and the diastolic dysfunction that characterizes heart failure.

SERCA2a Kinetics

SERCA2a transports 2 Ca$^{2+}$ ions per ATP hydrolyzed, with a $K_m$ of $\sim$0.5–1 $\mu$M for calcium — meaning it operates at roughly half-maximal velocity at resting diastolic $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]$. The pump consumes an estimated 30–40% of the myocyte's total ATP budget during active beating — a staggering metabolic cost that underscores calcium cycling's importance. A typical human ventricular myocyte contains $\sim$1,000–2,000 SERCA2a molecules per unit SR surface.

Figure 4.3. The SERCA2a/phospholamban pump-brake system. Left: At baseline, PLB inhibits SERCA2a by reducing its calcium affinity (high $K_m$), resulting in slow calcium reuptake. Right: After PKA phosphorylation (β-adrenergic stimulation), PLB dissociates, restoring SERCA2a's full affinity and dramatically accelerating calcium reuptake — the molecular basis of lusitropy.

Phospholamban: The Molecular Brake

Phospholamban (PLB) is a 52-amino-acid transmembrane protein that constitutively inhibits SERCA2a in its unphosphorylated state. It reduces SERCA2a's calcium affinity 2–3-fold (shifting $K_m$ from $\sim$0.5 $\mu$M to $\sim$1.5 $\mu$M), dramatically slowing calcium reuptake at diastolic calcium concentrations. PLB is the reason that at baseline, SERCA2a operates far below its maximum capacity — a reserve that can be unlocked on demand.

During beta-adrenergic stimulation, PKA phosphorylates PLB at serine-16 (and CaMKII at threonine-17), causing PLB to dissociate from SERCA2a. This relieves inhibition and dramatically accelerates calcium reuptake — the molecular basis of the lusitropic (relaxation-enhancing) effect of sympathetic stimulation. The coordinated phosphorylation of both RyR2 (more release) and PLB (faster reuptake) ensures that the enhanced systolic transient is followed by faster relaxation, allowing the heart to fill adequately at elevated heart rates.

The CUPID trials (Calcium Upregulation by Percutaneous Administration of Gene Therapy in Cardiac Disease) attempted to restore SERCA2a expression in failing hearts using AAV1-delivered gene therapy. Early-phase results were encouraging, but the pivotal CUPID 2 trial (2016) did not meet its primary endpoint. Current research has shifted toward upstream approaches: rather than adding more pump protein, investigators are targeting phosphatase-1 inhibitors (e.g., AB-1002) to relieve PLB-mediated inhibition of the endogenous SERCA2a — a more physiologically regulated strategy.

4.5   The Sodium-Calcium Exchanger — Dual-Mode Transport

The sodium-calcium exchanger NCX1 is a bidirectional transporter that exchanges 3 Na$^+$ for 1 Ca$^{2+}$ across the sarcolemma. The direction of transport depends on the electrochemical gradients of both ions and the membrane potential — making NCX a voltage-sensitive, context-dependent player in calcium homeostasis.

Figure 4.4. The two operating modes of the Na$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$ exchanger (NCX). Left: Forward mode — dominant during diastole and late repolarization — extrudes Ca$^{2+}$ while importing Na$^+$, generating a net inward (depolarizing) current. Right: Reverse mode — briefly active during early depolarization — imports Ca$^{2+}$ and exports Na$^+$, generating a net outward current. The reversal potential depends on both $[\text{Na}^+]$ and $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]$ gradients plus $V_m$.

Forward Mode (Calcium Extrusion)

Under normal diastolic conditions, NCX operates in forward mode: 3 Na$^+$ ions enter the cell down their concentration gradient, providing the energy to extrude 1 Ca$^{2+}$ against its gradient. This accounts for $\sim$10–30% of diastolic calcium removal (with SERCA2a handling the remainder). Because the exchange is electrogenic (net transfer of one positive charge inward), forward-mode NCX generates a small depolarizing current — normally negligible, but significant when calcium is elevated.

Reverse Mode and Arrhythmogenesis

When intracellular sodium is elevated (as in heart failure or digitalis toxicity) or the membrane is strongly depolarized, NCX reverses: Na$^+$ exits and Ca$^{2+}$ enters. This reverse-mode operation contributes to calcium overload. More importantly, during spontaneous calcium waves, the large forward-mode NCX current (extruding the wave-elevated calcium) generates substantial inward current — 100–500 pA when summed over the cell — sufficient to depolarize the membrane and trigger delayed afterdepolarizations (DADs). This is the primary mechanism linking calcium instability to electrical arrhythmia.

Recent cryo-EM structures of human NCX1 (2023) revealed distinct inactivated and activated conformations, with a sodium-dependent inactivation mechanism that serves as an intrinsic "brake" when intracellular sodium is chronically elevated. A 2024 study showed that gain-of-function NCX1 mutations paradoxically depress contractility by excessive calcium extrusion, demonstrating that too much of either forward or reverse NCX activity is maladaptive.

The Calcium Clock in Pacemaker Cells

In sinoatrial node (SAN) cells, a fundamentally different calcium cycling mechanism contributes to automaticity. During diastole, spontaneous calcium sparks from the SR activate NCX in forward mode, generating a ramp-like inward current that gradually depolarizes the membrane toward threshold. This "calcium clock" works in concert with the classical "membrane clock" (funny current $I_f$, T-type calcium current) to set heart rate. Disruption of the calcium clock — through RyR2 inhibition or SERCA2a depression — slows or abolishes pacemaker activity, demonstrating that spontaneous calcium cycling is not merely a byproduct but an active driver of the heartbeat.

4.6   Calcium Buffering — The 1% Rule

Perhaps the most underappreciated fact in cardiac calcium biology is that only $\sim$1–2% of total intracellular calcium exists as free Ca$^{2+}$ ions. The remaining 98–99% is bound to proteins. This massive buffering has profound consequences for calcium signaling: it drastically slows effective calcium diffusion (to $\sim$10–30 $\mu$m$^2$/s, roughly 10× slower than free aqueous diffusion) and shapes the kinetics of every calcium-dependent process.

The buffer capacity $\beta$ — defined as the ratio of total calcium change to free calcium change — ranges from $\sim$20 to 100 depending on the cell compartment. During a typical beat, free $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$ rises from $\sim$100 nM to $\sim$1–2 $\mu$M, a 10–20-fold increase. But the total calcium flux required to achieve this is 20–100 times larger, because most of the released calcium is immediately captured by buffers.

Figure 4.5. Calcium buffering in the cardiomyocyte. Left: Only 1–2% of total cytoplasmic calcium is free; the rest is bound to buffer proteins. Right: Partitioning among the major cytoplasmic buffers. Troponin C (the contractile sensor) is the largest single buffer pool. Inside the SR, calsequestrin-2 (CASQ2) binds 95–98% of luminal calcium.

The Major Buffers

Troponin C ($\sim$35% of cytoplasmic buffering): The regulatory calcium sensor of the contractile apparatus. Each troponin C molecule has 4 calcium-binding sites (2 regulatory with $K_d \approx 1{-}10\;\mu$M, 2 structural with $K_d \approx 100$ nM). With $\sim$10–15 million troponin C molecules per myocyte, this represents the largest single buffer pool. Calcium binding to the regulatory sites triggers the conformational cascade (tropomyosin shift → myosin binding site exposure → cross-bridge cycling) that produces contraction. Thus troponin C is simultaneously a buffer, a sensor, and the effector of the entire contractile process.

Calmodulin ($\sim$12% of buffering): A ubiquitous calcium sensor with 4 EF-hand binding motifs ($K_d$ range 100 nM to 5 $\mu$M). Calmodulin activates CaMKII, calcineurin, and numerous other enzymes — serving as the cell's primary calcium-to-kinase signal transducer. With $\sim$1–2 million molecules per myocyte and faster on/off kinetics than troponin C, calmodulin is particularly responsive to rapid calcium transients.

Calsequestrin 2 ($\sim$95–98% of SR luminal buffering): Inside the SR, CASQ2 binds $\sim$40–50 calcium ions per molecule, maintaining total SR calcium at 1–2 mM while keeping free SR calcium at only $\sim$20–50 $\mu$M. This enormous buffer capacity allows the SR to function as a high-capacity store without the osmotic stress that millimolar free calcium would create.

Other contributors: ATP and its magnesium complexes ($\sim$15%), mitochondrial uptake ($\lt$5% acutely but important for long-term calcium homeostasis), and various smaller calcium-binding proteins collectively account for the remaining buffering capacity.

In computational models, calcium buffering is typically handled by either rapid-equilibrium approximation (assuming buffer binding/unbinding is instantaneous, valid when buffer kinetics are much faster than calcium transient kinetics) or dynamic buffering ODEs (explicitly tracking bound and free buffer pools). TTP06 uses a rapid-equilibrium approach for most buffers, while ORd tracks selected buffer states dynamically. The choice matters: dynamic buffering adds state variables but captures the delay between calcium rise and buffer saturation — important for accurate spark modeling.

4.7   T-Tubule Remodeling — When Structure Fails

The T-tubule network consists of regular sarcolemmal invaginations ($\sim$0.2–0.5 $\mu$m diameter) that penetrate deep into the myocyte at $\sim$2 $\mu$m intervals, aligned with the Z-lines of the sarcomere. This regular architecture ensures that every CRU across the cell volume is within $\sim$1 $\mu$m of a T-tubule, enabling near-synchronous calcium release during the action potential.

T-Tubule Loss in Heart Failure

One of the earliest and most consequential structural changes in the failing myocardium is progressive loss and disorganization of the T-tubule network. Advanced heart failure can show 50–80% reduction in T-tubular density, with the remaining tubules becoming tortuous, dilated, and misaligned.

The primary mechanism is calpain-mediated cleavage of junctophilin-2. Elevated diastolic calcium in failing cells activates the protease calpain, which cleaves JPH2 — the very protein that anchors T-tubules to the jSR. Once this anchor is lost, the T-tubule membrane retracts from the SR surface, physically separating L-type channels from RyR2 clusters.

Orphaned RyR2 and Dyssynchronous Release

As T-tubules withdraw, the RyR2 clusters they once faced become orphaned — physically uncoupled from their L-type channel triggers. These orphaned RyR2s cannot be activated by the normal CICR mechanism (the L-type channel calcium signal no longer reaches them). Instead, they can only be activated secondarily, by the slower wave-like diffusion of calcium from neighboring intact dyads. The result is dyssynchronous calcium release: surface dyads fire promptly with the action potential, while interior orphaned clusters fire with a variable delay of 10–100 ms.

This dyssynchrony has dual consequences. First, the contractile force is reduced — not all sarcomeres are activated simultaneously. Second, the orphaned RyR2s, no longer under the tight local control of a nearby L-type channel, often exhibit increased sensitivity to luminal calcium and are prone to spontaneous opening. They become sources of diastolic calcium leak and spontaneous waves — contributing to both systolic dysfunction and arrhythmia susceptibility.

From a modeling perspective, the T-tubule remodeling story highlights a limitation of standard ionic models like TTP06 and ORd: they assume a single, well-coupled dyadic compartment. Capturing the spatial heterogeneity of heart failure — some CRUs coupled, some orphaned, some partially coupled — requires either multi-compartment extensions or spatially explicit subcellular models. This remains an active area of computational research, with multiscale models (2021–2024) integrating nanoscale dyadic dynamics into whole-cell and tissue-level simulations.

4.8   Emerging Frontiers: Imaging, Modeling, and Therapy

Super-Resolution Imaging of Living Cardiomyocytes

The past five years have seen transformative advances in visualizing calcium handling machinery in intact, living cells. MINFLUX microscopy (2024–2025) can now resolve individual RyR2 subunits and determine their 3D orientation within clusters — approaching atomic-level structural information in the native cellular environment. Live-cell photoactivated localization microscopy (PALM, 2022) has correlated nanoscale RyR2 cluster configuration with functional calcium spark properties, revealing that heart failure induces fragmentation of large RyR2 clusters into numerous smaller groupings with increased nearest-neighbor distances and impaired cooperative gating.

Next-Generation Calcium Indicators

Genetically encoded calcium indicators continue to evolve. The jGCaMP8 family (2023) achieves submillisecond rise times with the highest sensitivity yet — enabling real-time nanodomain calcium imaging. The NEMOer sensor family (2025) was specifically designed for endoplasmic/sarcoplasmic reticulum calcium imaging, overcoming the limited dynamic range of previous luminal indicators. These tools are beginning to provide direct experimental access to the $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$ dynamics that models have long predicted but experiments could not resolve.

Graph-Theoretic Prediction of Spark Fidelity

A striking computational advance (2023) uses graph theory to predict calcium spark probability directly from nanoscale cluster architecture. By constructing an adjacency graph of RyR2 positions within a CRU (nodes = channels, edges = functional coupling), researchers showed that the maximum eigenvalue of this adjacency matrix predicts spark probability with remarkable accuracy. This bridges the gap between structure (super-resolution images) and function (calcium spark recordings), explaining why some RyR2 clusters reliably produce sparks while others are functionally "silent."

With this deep understanding of calcium handling in hand, we are now prepared to examine the quantitative ionic models that encode these mechanisms computationally. The next chapter dissects the ten Tusscher–Panfilov 2006 model — 18 state variables that capture the calcium cycling machinery alongside every ionic current we have discussed.

Chapter 5

The ten Tusscher–Panfilov 2006 Model

TTP06 is the workhorse model for human ventricular electrophysiology. With 18 state variables — 12 gating variables and 6 ionic concentrations — it captures every phase of the action potential we dissected in Chapter 3, plus the calcium cycling machinery that drives contraction.

5.1   18 State Variables: What and Why

A natural question when encountering TTP06 for the first time is: why 18 variables? The answer lies in the variety of currents and intracellular processes that shape the cardiac AP. Each current requires gates, and each ion species requires a concentration variable to track changes over multiple beats.

The 18 states fall into two natural groups:

12 gating variables (each between 0 and 1, following Hodgkin–Huxley-style kinetics):

GateCurrentRoleSpeed
$m$$I_{\text{Na}}$Na$^+$ activation$\lt 1$ ms
$h$, $j$$I_{\text{Na}}$Na$^+$ fast and slow inactivation1–100 ms
$d$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Ca$^{2+}$ activation1–5 ms
$f$, $f_2$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Ca$^{2+}$ voltage inactivation20–300 ms
$f_{\text{Ca}}$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Ca$^{2+}$-dependent inactivation2–50 ms
$r$$I_{\text{to}}$Transient outward activation1–5 ms
$s$$I_{\text{to}}$Transient outward inactivation5–50 ms
$x_{r1}$, $x_{r2}$$I_{\text{Kr}}$Rapid delayed rectifier10–100 ms
$x_s$$I_{\text{Ks}}$Slow delayed rectifier100–500 ms

6 concentration variables (dynamic, changing each beat):

VariableDescriptionTypical Value
$[\text{Na}^+]_i$Intracellular sodium$\sim$10 mM
$[\text{K}^+]_i$Intracellular potassium$\sim$138 mM
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$Cytoplasmic calcium (free)$\sim$0.0001 mM
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$SR calcium store$\sim$1–3 mM
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$Subspace calcium (near L-type channels)$\sim$0.0001 mM
$R'$SR release recovery variable$\sim$1

The distinction between $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$, $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$, and $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$ reflects the compartmentalization of calcium handling. Calcium entering through L-type channels first reaches a tiny subspace near the SR release channels, triggering CICR locally before diffusing into the bulk cytoplasm.

5.2   The Complete Current Inventory

TTP06 models 12 distinct membrane currents plus 4 internal calcium fluxes. Every membrane current contributes to the total ionic current:

(5.1) $$I_{\text{ion}} = \underbrace{I_{\text{Na}} + I_{\text{CaL}}}_{\text{depolarizing}} + \underbrace{I_{\text{to}} + I_{\text{Kr}} + I_{\text{Ks}} + I_{\text{K1}} + I_{\text{pK}}}_{\text{repolarizing}} + \underbrace{I_{\text{NaK}} + I_{\text{NaCa}} + I_{\text{pCa}}}_{\text{pumps/exchangers}} + \underbrace{I_{\text{bNa}} + I_{\text{bCa}}}_{\text{background}}$$

This equation is the heart of the model: at every timestep, the simulator evaluates all 12 terms, sums them, and uses the total to update $V_m$. The sign convention follows physiology — positive current is outward (repolarizing), negative current is inward (depolarizing). The table below provides a complete reference, mapping each current to its AP phase, physiological role, and clinical significance.

CurrentTypeIonAP PhaseDirectionClinical Relevance
$I_{\text{Na}}$Voltage-gated channelNa$^+$0 (upstroke)InwardConduction velocity; Class I antiarrhythmics
$I_{\text{CaL}}$Voltage-gated channelCa$^{2+}$2 (plateau)InwardEC coupling trigger; Ca$^{2+}$ channel blockers (verapamil)
$I_{\text{to}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$1 (notch)OutwardTransmural heterogeneity; Brugada syndrome
$I_{\text{Kr}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$3 (repolarization)OutwardPrimary drug target; Long QT syndrome (hERG block)
$I_{\text{Ks}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$3 (repolarization reserve)OutwardRate adaptation; LQT1; adrenergic modulation
$I_{\text{K1}}$Inward rectifierK$^+$4 (rest) / 3 (late)OutwardResting potential; Andersen-Tawil syndrome (LQT7)
$I_{\text{pK}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$2 (plateau)OutwardSmall contribution to repolarization reserve
$I_{\text{NaK}}$ATP-driven pumpNa$^+$/K$^+$AllNet outwardGradient maintenance; digitalis target
$I_{\text{NaCa}}$ExchangerNa$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$2–3 / 4Both$^*$Calcium extrusion; DAD generation; heart failure
$I_{\text{pCa}}$ATP-driven pumpCa$^{2+}$AllOutwardFine calcium homeostasis
$I_{\text{bNa}}$Background leakNa$^+$AllInwardResting Na$^+$ influx; balanced by $I_{\text{NaK}}$
$I_{\text{bCa}}$Background leakCa$^{2+}$AllInwardResting Ca$^{2+}$ influx; balanced by pumps

$^*$$I_{\text{NaCa}}$ operates in forward mode (inward, extruding Ca$^{2+}$) during late repolarization and diastole, and in reverse mode (outward, bringing Ca$^{2+}$ in) briefly during early depolarization.

5.3   Voltage-Gated Depolarizing Currents

Fast Sodium Current ($I_{\text{Na}}$) — Phase 0

The fast sodium current is responsible for the action potential upstroke and determines conduction velocity in tissue. It follows the classical HH formulation with three activation gates ($m$) and two inactivation gates (fast $h$ and slow $j$):

(5.2) $$I_{\text{Na}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Na}}\,m^3\,h\,j\,(V_m - E_{\text{Na}})$$

The maximal conductance $\bar{g}_{\text{Na}} = 14.838$ nS/pF is one of the largest in the model. The $m^3$ activation is ultrafast ($\tau_m \lt 1$ ms), providing the explosive upstroke. The dual inactivation ($h$ fast, $j$ slow) ensures that sodium channels close within a few milliseconds and remain closed throughout the plateau — creating the absolute refractory period. Physiologically, $I_{\text{Na}}$ determines the maximum rate of rise $dV/dt_{\text{max}} \approx 250{-}350$ V/s, which is a direct clinical measure of sodium channel availability. Class I antiarrhythmic drugs (lidocaine, flecainide) work by reducing $\bar{g}_{\text{Na}}$, slowing conduction.

L-Type Calcium Current ($I_{\text{CaL}}$) — Phase 2

The L-type calcium current sustains the plateau and triggers excitation-contraction coupling. Unlike $I_{\text{Na}}$, it uses the Goldman–Hodgkin–Katz (GHK) formulation rather than an Ohmic driving force, because Ca$^{2+}$ has a very low intracellular concentration and a 2+ valence:

(5.3) $$I_{\text{CaL}} = P_{\text{CaL}}\,d\,f\,f_2\,f_{\text{Ca}}\cdot 4\,\frac{F^2}{RT}\,(V_m - 15)\,\frac{0.25\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}\,e^{2F(V_m-15)/RT} - [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o}{e^{2F(V_m-15)/RT}-1}$$

Note three features that distinguish this from a simple Ohmic current. First, TTP06 uses a permeability $P_{\text{CaL}} = 3.98 \times 10^{-5}$ cm/s rather than a conductance — the natural parameter for the GHK formulation. Second, the voltage is shifted by $-15$ mV ($V_m - 15$), reflecting the experimental observation that the current–voltage relationship of L-type channels peaks near $+15$ mV rather than $0$ mV. Third, the intracellular calcium carries an activity coefficient of 0.25, while the extracellular side uses bare $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o$ — this asymmetry accounts for the different ionic environments on each side. The four gates represent distinct biophysical mechanisms: $d$ is voltage activation ($\tau_d \sim 1{-}5$ ms), $f$ and $f_2$ are voltage-dependent inactivation (slow, $\tau \sim 20{-}300$ ms — this determines how long the plateau lasts), and $f_{\text{Ca}}$ is calcium-dependent inactivation (CDI), which responds to the local subspace calcium $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$. CDI is the primary negative feedback mechanism that limits calcium entry: as CICR raises subspace calcium, $f_{\text{Ca}}$ inactivates the channel, shutting off the trigger.

The GHK equation replaces the simple $(V_m - E_{\text{Ca}})$ driving force with a nonlinear function of voltage and concentrations on both sides. Why? For ions with large concentration gradients ($[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o / [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i \approx 20{,}000$) and multivalent charge, the Ohmic approximation badly overestimates current at large depolarizations. The GHK formula — derived from the Nernst–Planck electrodiffusion equation — accounts for the exponential concentration profile across the membrane and gives physically correct saturation behavior.

5.4   Voltage-Gated Repolarizing Currents

Transient Outward Current ($I_{\text{to}}$) — Phase 1

The transient outward K$^+$ current creates the Phase 1 notch. It activates rapidly during depolarization ($r$ gate, $\tau \sim 1{-}5$ ms) and inactivates within tens of milliseconds ($s$ gate, $\tau \sim 5{-}50$ ms):

(5.4) $$I_{\text{to}} = \bar{g}_{\text{to}}\,r\,s\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

The maximal conductance $\bar{g}_{\text{to}}$ varies dramatically between cell types: 0.294 nS/pF in epicardial cells (deep notch) vs. 0.073 nS/pF in endocardial cells (minimal notch). This transmural gradient is the single largest source of AP shape variation across the ventricular wall and produces the J-point deflection on the surface ECG. In Brugada syndrome, enhanced $I_{\text{to}}$ in right ventricular epicardium creates an exaggerated notch that can trigger reentrant arrhythmias.

Rapid Delayed Rectifier ($I_{\text{Kr}}$) — Phase 3

$I_{\text{Kr}}$ is the primary repolarization current, carried by hERG (Kv11.1) channels. It has unusual kinetics — fast inactivation at positive voltages, rapid recovery from inactivation during repolarization — that produce a characteristic "resurgent" current during Phase 3:

(5.5) $$I_{\text{Kr}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Kr}}\,\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o / 5.4}\,x_{r1}\,x_{r2}\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

The $\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o / 5.4}$ factor captures the experimental observation that hERG conductance scales with the square root of extracellular potassium. The two gating variables $x_{r1}$ (activation, $\tau \sim 10{-}100$ ms) and $x_{r2}$ (inactivation, fast) together create the voltage-dependent rectification that makes $I_{\text{Kr}}$ small during the plateau but large during Phase 3. Clinically, $I_{\text{Kr}}$ is the most important current in cardiac pharmacology: hERG block is the leading cause of drug-induced Long QT syndrome, and the CiPA initiative focuses heavily on predicting hERG interactions in silico.

Slow Delayed Rectifier ($I_{\text{Ks}}$) — Repolarization Reserve

$I_{\text{Ks}}$ activates very slowly ($\tau_{x_s} \sim 100{-}500$ ms) and provides a "safety net" when $I_{\text{Kr}}$ is compromised:

(5.6) $$I_{\text{Ks}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Ks}}\,x_s^2\,(V_m - E_{\text{Ks}})$$

Note that $I_{\text{Ks}}$ uses the potassium–sodium reversal potential $E_{\text{Ks}}$ (computed from the Goldman equation for K$^+$ and Na$^+$ permeability) rather than the pure potassium reversal $E_{\text{K}}$, because the KvLQT1/minK channel complex has slight Na$^+$ permeability. The $x_s^2$ activation gives the current a sigmoid-like voltage dependence with a slower foot than $I_{\text{Kr}}$. Under normal conditions, $I_{\text{Ks}}$ contributes modestly to repolarization. Its importance emerges when $I_{\text{Kr}}$ is blocked — then $I_{\text{Ks}}$ becomes the backup repolarization pathway. This is the concept of repolarization reserve: a healthy heart can tolerate partial block of one K$^+$ channel because others compensate. Loss of repolarization reserve (e.g., combined $I_{\text{Kr}}$ + $I_{\text{Ks}}$ reduction) dramatically increases arrhythmia risk.

Inward Rectifier ($I_{\text{K1}}$) — Phase 4 and Late Phase 3

(5.7) $$I_{\text{K1}} = \bar{g}_{\text{K1}}\,\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o / 5.4}\,x_{K1,\infty}(V_m)\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

$I_{\text{K1}}$ is unique among TTP06 currents: it has no dynamic gating variable. Its rectification is instantaneous, captured entirely by the steady-state function $x_{K1,\infty}(V_m)$, which transitions from $\sim$1 below $-80$ mV to $\sim$0 above $-40$ mV. Physiologically, this reflects the block of Kir2.1 channels by intracellular Mg$^{2+}$ and polyamines at depolarized voltages. The result is a current that behaves as a strong "anchor" at rest (holding $V_m$ near $E_K \approx -88$ mV), vanishes during the plateau (allowing the ICaL/IKr balance to play out), and then reactivates during Phase 3 to accelerate the final phase of repolarization.

Plateau Potassium Current ($I_{\text{pK}}$)

(5.8) $$I_{\text{pK}} = \bar{g}_{\text{pK}}\,\frac{1}{1 + e^{(25 - V_m)/5.98}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

$I_{\text{pK}}$ is a small outward K$^+$ current active during the plateau. It has no dynamic gates — the sigmoidal voltage dependence is instantaneous. Its contribution to total current is minor ($\bar{g}_{\text{pK}} = 0.0146$ nS/pF), but it participates in the repolarization reserve and affects APD at the margins.

5.5   Pumps, Exchangers, and Background Currents

The six voltage-gated currents above shape the AP waveform on a beat-to-beat basis. But the cell must also maintain its ionic gradients over thousands of beats. This is the job of the pumps, exchangers, and background leak currents — a group that is often overlooked but is essential for long-term stability.

Na$^+$/K$^+$ ATPase ($I_{\text{NaK}}$) — The Gradient Keeper

(5.9) $$I_{\text{NaK}} = \frac{P_{\text{NaK}}\,[\text{K}^+]_o\,[\text{Na}^+]_i}{([\text{K}^+]_o + K_{mK})([\text{Na}^+]_i + K_{mNa})}\cdot\frac{1}{1 + 0.1245\,e^{-0.1FV_m/RT} + 0.0353\,e^{-FV_m/RT}}$$

The Na$^+$/K$^+$-ATPase pumps 3 Na$^+$ ions out and 2 K$^+$ ions in per ATP hydrolyzed, generating a net outward current. This pump is what maintains the sodium and potassium gradients that all voltage-gated currents depend on. Without it, gradients would run down within minutes and the cell would depolarize. The current is voltage-dependent (less effective at positive voltages), saturates with intracellular Na$^+$ ($K_{mNa} = 40$ mM) and extracellular K$^+$ ($K_{mK} = 1$ mM), and is the target of cardiac glycosides (digoxin/digitalis): by blocking $I_{\text{NaK}}$, intracellular Na$^+$ rises, which reduces the driving force for the Na$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$ exchanger, indirectly increasing intracellular Ca$^{2+}$ and strengthening contraction (positive inotropy).

Na$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$ Exchanger ($I_{\text{NaCa}}$) — The Bidirectional Bridge

(5.10) $$I_{\text{NaCa}} = \frac{k_{\text{NaCa}}\bigl([\text{Na}^+]_i^3\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o\,e^{\gamma FV_m/RT} - \alpha\,[\text{Na}^+]_o^3\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i\,e^{(\gamma-1)FV_m/RT}\bigr)}{(K_{m,\text{Na}}^3 + [\text{Na}^+]_o^3)(K_{m,\text{Ca}} + [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o)(1 + k_{\text{sat}}\,e^{(\gamma-1)FV_m/RT})}$$

The NCX exchanges 3 Na$^+$ for 1 Ca$^{2+}$ and can operate in either direction depending on the electrochemical gradients. The numerator contains two terms: the first (forward) drives Na$^+$ in and Ca$^{2+}$ out; the second (reverse, scaled by $\alpha = 2.5$) drives Na$^+$ out and Ca$^{2+}$ in. The factor $\alpha$ sets the relative strength of reverse-mode transport. The denominator has three components: the $K_m$ saturation terms for Na$^+$ and Ca$^{2+}$, and a voltage-dependent saturation factor $1 + k_{\text{sat}}\,e^{(\gamma-1)FV_m/RT}$ that prevents the exchanger from running at unphysically high rates at extreme potentials. In forward mode (dominant during late repolarization and diastole): Na$^+$ enters, Ca$^{2+}$ is extruded → net inward current. This is the primary mechanism for removing calcium from the cytoplasm after each beat (contributing $\sim$28% of calcium removal in human ventricular myocytes, with SERCA handling the remaining $\sim$70%). In reverse mode (brief, during early depolarization): Na$^+$ exits, Ca$^{2+}$ enters → net outward current. The voltage-dependent partition factor $\gamma \approx 0.35$ determines the relative influence of voltage on the forward vs. reverse rates.

$I_{\text{NaCa}}$ is critically important in disease. In heart failure, elevated $[\text{Na}^+]_i$ reduces forward-mode NCX activity, impairing calcium removal and contributing to diastolic dysfunction. In conditions of calcium overload, the inward current generated by forward-mode NCX during diastole can trigger delayed afterdepolarizations (DADs) — a major arrhythmia mechanism (Chapter 4, Section 4.5).

Sarcolemmal Ca$^{2+}$ Pump ($I_{\text{pCa}}$)

(5.11) $$I_{\text{pCa}} = \frac{\bar{g}_{\text{pCa}}\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i}{K_{\text{pCa}} + [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i}$$

The plasma membrane Ca$^{2+}$-ATPase (PMCA) provides a secondary route for calcium extrusion — slower than NCX but with higher affinity ($K_{\text{pCa}} = 0.0005$ mM). It contributes only $\sim$1–2% of total calcium removal per beat but is important for fine-tuning the diastolic calcium level over many beats.

Background Leak Currents ($I_{\text{bNa}}$, $I_{\text{bCa}}$)

(5.12) $$I_{\text{bNa}} = \bar{g}_{\text{bNa}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Na}}), \qquad I_{\text{bCa}} = \bar{g}_{\text{bCa}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Ca}})$$

These small Ohmic currents represent the passive leak of Na$^+$ and Ca$^{2+}$ through non-specific channels. Their conductances are tiny ($\bar{g}_{\text{bNa}} = 2.9 \times 10^{-4}$, $\bar{g}_{\text{bCa}} = 5.92 \times 10^{-4}$ nS/pF), but they serve a critical bookkeeping role: they provide the steady inward Na$^+$ and Ca$^{2+}$ leak that the pumps ($I_{\text{NaK}}$, $I_{\text{pCa}}$, NCX) must counterbalance. Without these background currents, the model would slowly drift to non-physiological concentrations over hundreds of beats.

The balance between background leak currents and pump/exchanger currents is what determines the long-term stability of ionic concentrations. If you modify pump parameters (e.g., reducing $I_{\text{NaK}}$ to simulate ischemia), you must simulate hundreds of beats to reach a new steady state — the first few beats will look normal, but gradual Na$^+$ accumulation will progressively alter the AP shape. This is why cardiac modelers must always run to steady state before collecting data.

5.6   Currents During Each AP Phase — A Visual Map

Figure 5.1. Activity map of all 12 TTP06 membrane currents across the five AP phases. Bar width represents the temporal extent; opacity represents relative magnitude. Red bars are inward (depolarizing), blue bars are outward (repolarizing), gray bars are housekeeping currents active throughout the cycle.

All gating variables in TTP06 satisfy the same relaxation ODE we derived in equation (1.3): $dx/dt = (x_\infty - x)/\tau_x$. This uniformity is what makes the Rush–Larsen method (Chapter 8) so powerful: you can integrate every gate with the same exponential formula $x^{n+1} = x_\infty + (x^n - x_\infty)\,e^{-\Delta t/\tau_x}$, regardless of how stiff the kinetics are. The concentration variables ($[\text{Na}^+]_i$, $[\text{K}^+]_i$, etc.) do not follow relaxation kinetics and must be integrated with Forward Euler or a similar method.

5.7   Endo, Epi, and M-Cells: Transmural Heterogeneity

One of TTP06's most important features is its ability to represent three distinct cell types across the ventricular wall, each with a different AP morphology:

Epicardial cells (outer wall): Large $I_{\text{to}}$ → deep Phase 1 notch → shorter AP. Endocardial cells (inner wall): Small $I_{\text{to}}$ → minimal notch → moderate AP duration. M-cells (mid-wall): Small $I_{\text{Ks}}$ → very long plateau → longest AP duration.

These differences arise from changing just a few maximal conductance values ($\bar{g}_{\text{to}}$, $\bar{g}_{\text{Ks}}$) and some kinetic parameters. The fact that the same model structure, with minor parameter adjustments, reproduces three qualitatively different AP shapes is a testament to how well TTP06 captures the underlying biophysics.

In Engine V5.4, the TTP06 implementation lives in the cardiac_sim/ionic/ttp06/ package: model.py (the TTP06Model class), currents.py (all 12 current functions), gating.py (steady-states and time constants), calcium.py (concentration update equations), and parameters.py (the StateIndex enum and TTP06Parameters dataclass). V is stored separately; the 18 ionic states live in a tensor of shape (n_cells, 18). The cell_type parameter (CellType.ENDO, CellType.EPI, CellType.M_CELL) selects endo/epi/M-cell variants by switching maximal conductances and I$_{\text{to}}$ kinetics. A single tissue simulation can represent the full transmural gradient by assigning different cell types to different spatial regions — essential for realistic ECG simulation.

5.8   Calcium Handling and CICR

The calcium subsystem is perhaps the most physiologically important part of TTP06, because calcium is the link between electrical excitation and mechanical contraction. The model tracks calcium through three compartments connected by fluxes:

Figure 4.1. Calcium cycling in TTP06. The L-type current $I_{\text{CaL}}$ brings Ca$^{2+}$ into the subspace, triggering CICR from the SR ($J_{\text{rel}}$). Ca$^{2+}$ diffuses to the cytoplasm ($J_{\text{xfer}}$), drives contraction, then is pumped back into the SR by SERCA ($J_{\text{up}}$).

The CICR mechanism works like a trigger and amplifier:

Trigger: L-type calcium channels open during Phase 2, admitting a small amount of Ca$^{2+}$ into the subspace. This raises $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$.

Amplification: The elevated $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$ activates ryanodine receptors (RyR) on the SR membrane, which release a much larger bolus of Ca$^{2+}$ from the SR store into the subspace. This is the "release" flux $J_{\text{rel}}$.

Diffusion and contraction: Ca$^{2+}$ diffuses from the subspace to the bulk cytoplasm ($J_{\text{xfer}}$), where it binds to troponin C and triggers the contractile machinery of the sarcomere.

Recovery: The SERCA pump ($J_{\text{up}}$) actively transports Ca$^{2+}$ from the cytoplasm back into the SR, refilling the store for the next beat. A small leak ($J_{\text{leak}}$) allows some Ca$^{2+}$ to trickle back out.

Getting this calcium balance right is critical: if the SR is overloaded, spontaneous Ca$^{2+}$ release can trigger delayed afterdepolarizations (DADs) and arrhythmias. If the SR is depleted, contractile force drops and the AP shape distorts. The three-compartment model in TTP06 captures these dynamics with reasonable fidelity while remaining computationally tractable for tissue-scale simulations.

Chapter 6

The O'Hara–Rudy 2011 Model

The O'Hara–Rudy dynamic (ORd) model was built from an unprecedented dataset of undiseased human ventricular myocyte recordings. With 41 state variables, 15 membrane currents, and a CaMKII signaling pathway that confers rate adaptation, it represents the current gold standard for human ventricular electrophysiology simulation. This chapter provides the same comprehensive treatment given to TTP06 in Chapter 5 — every current, every equation, organized by physiological function.

6.1   41 State Variables: What and Why

ORd has more than twice the state variables of TTP06 (41 vs. 18). The additional complexity arises from three sources: (1) separate fast and slow inactivation gates for most currents, (2) CaMKII-phosphorylated variants of several gates, and (3) two additional ionic compartments (subspace sodium and potassium). Every state variable has a clear biophysical justification.

29 gating variables (each between 0 and 1, following Hodgkin–Huxley-style kinetics):

Gate(s)CurrentRoleCaMKII variant?
$m$$I_{\text{Na}}$Fast Na$^+$ activationNo
$h_f$, $h_s$$I_{\text{Na}}$Fast and slow inactivation$h_{sp}$ (slow, phosphorylated)
$j$$I_{\text{Na}}$Recovery from inactivation$j_p$
$m_L$$I_{\text{NaL}}$Late Na$^+$ activationNo
$h_L$$I_{\text{NaL}}$Late Na$^+$ inactivation$h_{Lp}$
$d$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Ca$^{2+}$ activationNo
$f_f$, $f_s$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Fast and slow voltage inactivation$f_{fp}$
$f_{\text{Ca},f}$, $f_{\text{Ca},s}$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Fast and slow Ca$^{2+}$-dependent inactivation$f_{\text{Ca},fp}$
$j_{\text{Ca}}$$I_{\text{CaL}}$Ca$^{2+}$-dependent recoveryNo
$n_{\text{Ca}}$$I_{\text{CaL}}$CaMKII phosphorylation transition—
$a$$I_{\text{to}}$Transient outward activation$a_p$
$i_F$, $i_S$$I_{\text{to}}$Fast and slow inactivation$i_{Fp}$, $i_{Sp}$
$x_{r,f}$, $x_{r,s}$$I_{\text{Kr}}$Fast and slow activationNo
$x_{s1}$, $x_{s2}$$I_{\text{Ks}}$Slow delayed rectifier gatesNo
$x_{K1}$$I_{\text{K1}}$Inward rectifier (now dynamic!)No

A key design principle of ORd is the CaMKII-phosphorylated population concept. For currents modulated by CaMKII ($I_{\text{Na}}$, $I_{\text{NaL}}$, $I_{\text{CaL}}$, $I_{\text{to}}$), the model maintains separate gating variables for the phosphorylated channel population. The total current is then a weighted average: $(1 - \Phi)\cdot I_{\text{normal}} + \Phi\cdot I_{\text{phosphorylated}}$, where $\Phi$ is the CaMKII-dependent phosphorylation fraction. This doubles the gate count for affected currents but allows rate-dependent modulation of channel kinetics.

8 concentration variables (dynamic, changing each beat):

VariableDescriptionTypical ValueNew vs. TTP06?
$[\text{Na}^+]_i$Intracellular sodium (bulk)$\sim$7 mMSame concept
$[\text{Na}^+]_{\text{ss}}$Subspace sodium$\sim$7 mMNew
$[\text{K}^+]_i$Intracellular potassium (bulk)$\sim$145 mMSame concept
$[\text{K}^+]_{\text{ss}}$Subspace potassium$\sim$145 mMNew
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$Cytoplasmic calcium (free)$\sim$0.0001 mMSame concept
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$Subspace calcium$\sim$0.0001 mMSame concept
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{nsr}}$Network SR calcium$\sim$1.2 mMSplit from $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$
$[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{jsr}}$Junctional SR calcium$\sim$1.2 mMSplit from $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{SR}}$

Additional state variables:

VariableDescriptionRole
$\text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}}$CaMKII trapped (autophosphorylated) fractionLong-term kinase memory; drives rate adaptation
$V_m$Membrane potentialPrimary electrical variable
$J_{\text{rel,NP}}$SR release flux (non-phosphorylated)RyR release dynamics
$J_{\text{rel,CaMK}}$SR release flux (CaMKII-phosphorylated)Enhanced RyR release

The most important structural differences from TTP06 are the addition of subspace Na$^+$ and K$^+$ compartments (reflecting the fact that these ions also have local concentrations near the membrane), the split of the SR into network and junctional compartments (SERCA pumps into the nSR; RyR releases from the jSR), and the CaMKII trapped state that provides long-term "memory" of recent heart rate.

Why does ORd track subspace Na$^+$ and K$^+$? In TTP06, only Ca$^{2+}$ had a subspace compartment because its concentration can change dramatically during a single beat ($\times$10–100 locally). Na$^+$ and K$^+$ concentrations change by much less ($\lt$1%), so a single bulk compartment seemed sufficient. But ORd found that even small Na$^+$ gradients near the membrane affect NCX and Na$^+$/K$^+$-ATPase activity — the subspace compartments improve accuracy when these transporters are the focus.

6.2   The Complete Current Inventory

ORd models 15 distinct membrane currents. Every current contributes to the total ionic current:

(6.1) $$\begin{aligned} I_{\text{ion}} \;=\; & \underbrace{I_{\text{Na}} + I_{\text{NaL}} + I_{\text{CaL}}}_{\text{depolarizing}} \;+\; \underbrace{I_{\text{to}} + I_{\text{Kr}} + I_{\text{Ks}} + I_{\text{K1}} + I_{\text{Kb}}}_{\text{repolarizing}} \\[6pt] & +\; \underbrace{I_{\text{NaK}} + I_{\text{NaCa,i}} + I_{\text{NaCa,ss}} + I_{\text{pCa}}}_{\text{pumps/exchangers}} \;+\; \underbrace{I_{\text{Nab}} + I_{\text{Cab}}}_{\text{background}} \;+\; \underbrace{I_{\text{ClCa}} + I_{\text{Clb}}}_{\text{chloride}} \end{aligned}$$

Compared to TTP06's 12 currents, ORd adds $I_{\text{NaL}}$ (late sodium), splits $I_{\text{NaCa}}$ into two populations, adds chloride currents ($I_{\text{ClCa}}$, $I_{\text{Clb}}$), and replaces the plateau potassium current $I_{\text{pK}}$ with a background potassium current $I_{\text{Kb}}$. The L-type calcium current is further decomposed into three ionic components ($I_{\text{CaL,Ca}}$, $I_{\text{CaL,Na}}$, $I_{\text{CaL,K}}$). The table below provides the complete reference.

CurrentTypeIonAP PhaseDirectionClinical Relevance
$I_{\text{Na}}$Voltage-gated channelNa$^+$0 (upstroke)InwardConduction velocity; Class I antiarrhythmics
$I_{\text{NaL}}$Voltage-gated channelNa$^+$2 (plateau)InwardNEW: APD modulation; ranolazine target; LQT3
$I_{\text{CaL}}$Voltage-gated channelCa$^{2+}$/Na$^+$/K$^+$2 (plateau)InwardEC coupling trigger; 3 ionic components; CaMKII-modulated
$I_{\text{to}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$1 (notch)OutwardTransmural heterogeneity; Brugada syndrome
$I_{\text{Kr}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$3 (repolarization)OutwardhERG block; drug-induced LQTS; CiPA assessment
$I_{\text{Ks}}$Voltage-gated channelK$^+$3 (repolarization reserve)OutwardRate adaptation (CaMKII); LQT1; $\beta$-adrenergic
$I_{\text{K1}}$Inward rectifierK$^+$4 (rest) / 3 (late)OutwardResting potential; now with dynamic gate
$I_{\text{Kb}}$Background leakK$^+$AllOutwardReplaces TTP06's $I_{\text{pK}}$; small repolarization
$I_{\text{NaK}}$ATP-driven pumpNa$^+$/K$^+$AllNet outwardThermodynamic cycle model; digitalis target
$I_{\text{NaCa,i}}$ExchangerNa$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$2–3 / 4Both$^*$Bulk cytoplasm NCX; calcium extrusion
