$I_{\text{NaCa,ss}}$ExchangerNa$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$2–3 / 4Both$^*$NEW: Subspace NCX; local Ca$^{2+}$ control
$I_{\text{pCa}}$ATP-driven pumpCa$^{2+}$AllOutwardFine calcium homeostasis (PMCA)
$I_{\text{Nab}}$Background leakNa$^+$AllInwardResting Na$^+$ influx; balanced by $I_{\text{NaK}}$
$I_{\text{Cab}}$Background leakCa$^{2+}$AllInwardResting Ca$^{2+}$ influx; GHK formulation
$I_{\text{ClCa}}$Ca$^{2+}$-activated channelCl$^-$1–2Inward$^\dagger$NEW: Contributes to Phase 1 notch and plateau
$I_{\text{Clb}}$Background leakCl$^-$AllInward$^\dagger$NEW: Background Cl$^-$ leak

$^*$$I_{\text{NaCa}}$ operates in forward mode (inward, extruding Ca$^{2+}$) during late repolarization and diastole, and in reverse mode (outward, bringing Ca$^{2+}$ in) briefly during early depolarization. $^\dagger$Cl$^-$ currents are depolarizing (inward by convention) because Cl$^-$ efflux = inward current, with $E_{\text{Cl}} \approx -40$ mV.

6.3   Voltage-Gated Depolarizing Currents

Fast Sodium Current ($I_{\text{Na}}$) — Phase 0

The fast sodium current retains the classical HH structure from TTP06, but with two key upgrades: refitted kinetics from undiseased human data, and CaMKII phosphorylation of the slow inactivation gate. The total current blends non-phosphorylated and phosphorylated channel populations:

(6.2) $$I_{\text{Na}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Na}}\,m^3\,\bigl[(1 - \Phi_{\text{Na}})\,h\,j + \Phi_{\text{Na}}\,h_p\,j_p\bigr]\,(V_m - E_{\text{Na}})$$

Here $\Phi_{\text{Na}}$ is the CaMKII phosphorylation fraction for the sodium channel, and the composite inactivation gate $h$ is a weighted sum of fast and slow components: $h = A_{hf}\,h_f + (1 - A_{hf})\,h_s$, with the weight $A_{hf}$ depending on voltage (fast inactivation dominates at depolarized potentials). The phosphorylated population uses $h_p = A_{hf}\,h_f + (1 - A_{hf})\,h_{sp}$, where $h_{sp}$ has a shifted voltage dependence — CaMKII phosphorylation slows recovery from inactivation, contributing to rate-dependent sodium channel availability. The maximal conductance $\bar{g}_{\text{Na}} = 75$ mS/$\mu$F is substantially larger than TTP06's 14.838 nS/pF (note different units), reflecting the refitting to human data.

Late Sodium Current ($I_{\text{NaL}}$) — Phase 2

The late sodium current is the most important addition in ORd relative to TTP06. It represents a small but persistent Na$^+$ influx that flows throughout the plateau — generated by sodium channels that briefly reopen or fail to fully inactivate:

(6.3) $$I_{\text{NaL}} = \bar{g}_{\text{NaL}}\,m_L\,\bigl[(1 - \Phi_{\text{NaL}})\,h_L + \Phi_{\text{NaL}}\,h_{Lp}\bigr]\,(V_m - E_{\text{Na}})$$

The maximal conductance $\bar{g}_{\text{NaL}} = 0.0075$ mS/$\mu$F is roughly 1/10,000th of peak $I_{\text{Na}}$, but because $I_{\text{NaL}}$ flows for 200+ ms (vs. $\lt$2 ms for peak $I_{\text{Na}}$), it carries significant charge and contributes approximately 5% of APD. The activation gate $m_L$ is similar to the fast sodium $m$ gate but with slightly slower kinetics. The inactivation gate $h_L$ has a time constant of $\sim$200 ms — orders of magnitude slower than the fast sodium $h$ gate — explaining why this current persists through the plateau.

CaMKII phosphorylation of $I_{\text{NaL}}$ ($h_{Lp}$) shifts the inactivation, increasing the late current at faster heart rates. This creates a positive feedback loop: faster rate → more Ca$^{2+}$ entry → more CaMKII activation → more $I_{\text{NaL}}$ → more Na$^+$ entry → potentially dangerous if unchecked. Clinically, this pathway is relevant to arrhythmia in heart failure.

$I_{\text{NaL}}$ is the primary target of ranolazine, an antianginal drug that selectively inhibits late sodium current without significantly affecting peak $I_{\text{Na}}$. This specificity is impossible to study computationally without explicitly modeling $I_{\text{NaL}}$ — one of the main reasons TTP06 is inadequate for drug safety assessment. In Long QT syndrome type 3 (LQT3), gain-of-function mutations in SCN5A increase $I_{\text{NaL}}$ by 2–5 fold, dangerously prolonging the plateau. ORd can reproduce this pathology by simply scaling $\bar{g}_{\text{NaL}}$.

L-Type Calcium Current ($I_{\text{CaL}}$) — Phase 2

The L-type calcium current in ORd is substantially more complex than in TTP06. It is decomposed into three ionic components, reflecting the imperfect selectivity of the Cav1.2 channel:

(6.4) $$I_{\text{CaL}} = I_{\text{CaL,Ca}} + I_{\text{CaL,Na}} + I_{\text{CaL,K}}$$

Each component uses the GHK flux equation with its respective permeability. The calcium component (by far the largest) is:

(6.5) $$I_{\text{CaL,Ca}} = P_{\text{Ca}}\,d\,\bigl[(1 - \Phi_{\text{CaL}})\,f\,j_{\text{Ca}}\,f_{\text{Ca}} + \Phi_{\text{CaL}}\,f_p\,j_{\text{Ca}}\,f_{\text{Ca},p}\bigr] \cdot \Psi_{\text{Ca}}(V_m, [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}})$$

where $\Psi_{\text{Ca}}$ is the GHK flux: $\Psi_{\text{Ca}} = 4\tfrac{F^2}{RT}\,V_m\,\tfrac{[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}\,e^{2FV_m/RT} - 0.341\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_o}{e^{2FV_m/RT}-1}$, and the composite gates are: $f = A_{ff}\,f_f + (1 - A_{ff})\,f_s$ (voltage inactivation, blending fast and slow components) and $f_{\text{Ca}} = A_{\text{Ca},f}\,f_{\text{Ca},f} + (1 - A_{\text{Ca},f})\,f_{\text{Ca},s}$ (calcium-dependent inactivation).

The CaMKII-phosphorylated population ($\Phi_{\text{CaL}}$) uses separate gates $f_p$ and $f_{\text{Ca},p}$ with shifted kinetics — phosphorylation facilitates channel reopening and slows CDI, increasing calcium influx at faster rates. This CaMKII-mediated enhancement of $I_{\text{CaL}}$ is counterbalanced by increased SERCA activity (also CaMKII-dependent), maintaining calcium homeostasis while strengthening contraction — a key component of the positive force-frequency relationship.

The Na$^+$ and K$^+$ components follow the same gating but use sodium and potassium permeabilities ($P_{\text{Na}} = 0.00125\,P_{\text{Ca}}$ and $P_{\text{K}} = 3.574\times10^{-4}\,P_{\text{Ca}}$) and their respective GHK fluxes. Though individually small, these components contribute to the ion balance over many beats.

Why split $I_{\text{CaL}}$ into three components? In TTP06, only Ca$^{2+}$ permeation was modeled because Ca$^{2+}$ is the dominant charge carrier. But when tracking Na$^+$ and K$^+$ concentrations dynamically over thousands of beats (as ORd does with its subspace compartments), even the small Na$^+$ and K$^+$ fluxes through L-type channels contribute to concentration drift. Ignoring them causes slow, insidious accumulation errors that manifest as AP duration changes after hundreds of beats — exactly the kind of subtle bug that plagues long-running simulations.

6.4   Voltage-Gated Repolarizing Currents

Transient Outward Current ($I_{\text{to}}$) — Phase 1

The transient outward current in ORd has separate fast and slow inactivation gates (replacing TTP06's single $s$ gate), plus CaMKII-phosphorylated variants:

(6.6) $$I_{\text{to}} = \bar{g}_{\text{to}}\,\bigl[(1 - \Phi_{\text{to}})\,a\,i + \Phi_{\text{to}}\,a_p\,i_p\bigr]\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

where $i = A_{iF}\,i_F + (1 - A_{iF})\,i_S$ blends fast and slow inactivation, with the weight $A_{iF}$ depending on voltage. The CaMKII-phosphorylated inactivation $i_p = A_{iF}\,i_{Fp} + (1-A_{iF})\,i_{Sp}$ has shifted kinetics. The maximal conductance $\bar{g}_{\text{to}}$ retains the dramatic transmural gradient: 0.02 mS/$\mu$F in endocardium vs. 0.08 mS/$\mu$F in epicardium and M-cells.

Rapid Delayed Rectifier ($I_{\text{Kr}}$) — Phase 3

$I_{\text{Kr}}$ in ORd uses refitted kinetics from undiseased human hERG data. Like TTP06, it has two activation components, but ORd blends fast and slow activation:

(6.7) $$I_{\text{Kr}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Kr}}\,\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o / 5.4}\,x_r\,r_{Kr}(V_m)\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

Here $x_r = A_{xr,f}\,x_{r,f} + (1 - A_{xr,f})\,x_{r,s}$ is a weighted blend of fast and slow activation, and $r_{Kr}(V_m)$ is an instantaneous rectification factor that captures the voltage-dependent inactivation of hERG channels. The $\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o/5.4}$ factor preserves the extracellular K$^+$ dependence from TTP06. Notably, $I_{\text{Kr}}$ is not CaMKII-modulated in ORd — its kinetics are purely voltage-dependent. This is the key current for the CiPA (Comprehensive in vitro Proarrhythmia Assay) initiative, which uses ORd as its reference model for predicting drug-induced arrhythmia risk.

Slow Delayed Rectifier ($I_{\text{Ks}}$) — Repolarization Reserve

(6.8) $$I_{\text{Ks}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Ks}}\,K_{\text{s,Ca}}\,x_{s1}\,x_{s2}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Ks}})$$

A key difference from TTP06 is the calcium-sensitivity factor $K_{\text{s,Ca}} = 1 + 0.6\bigl/\bigl(1 + (3.8\times10^{-5}/[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i)^{1.4}\bigr)$, which increases $I_{\text{Ks}}$ when intracellular calcium is elevated. At resting calcium ($\sim$100 nM), $K_{\text{s,Ca}} \approx 1$ and the current is near baseline. During systolic calcium peaks ($\sim$1 $\mu$M), $K_{\text{s,Ca}}$ increases to $\sim$1.6, boosting repolarization reserve precisely when the cell needs it most. Combined with CaMKII modulation of $\bar{g}_{\text{Ks}}$ at faster heart rates, this creates robust rate-dependent enhancement of repolarization reserve. The reversal potential $E_{\text{Ks}}$ remains a mixed Na$^+$/K$^+$ potential, computed from the Goldman equation. The maximal conductance shows strong transmural variation: $\bar{g}_{\text{Ks}} = 0.0034$ mS/$\mu$F (baseline) in endocardial cells, scaled $1.4\times$ in epicardial cells, with reduced values in M-cells explaining the prolonged M-cell APD and the T-wave morphology on the ECG.

Inward Rectifier ($I_{\text{K1}}$) — Phase 4 and Late Phase 3

(6.9) $$I_{\text{K1}} = \bar{g}_{\text{K1}}\,\sqrt{[\text{K}^+]_o}\,x_{K1}\,r_{K1}(V_m)\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

Unlike TTP06 — where $I_{\text{K1}}$ used only an instantaneous steady-state function — ORd gives $I_{\text{K1}}$ a dynamic gating variable $x_{K1}$ with its own time constant (though still fast, $\tau \sim 1{-}10$ ms). The instantaneous rectification factor $r_{K1}(V_m)$ captures the polyamine block at depolarized voltages. This formulation better reproduces the terminal repolarization phase and the resting membrane potential measured in human ventricular myocytes.

Background Potassium Current ($I_{\text{Kb}}$)

(6.10) $$I_{\text{Kb}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Kb}}\,x_{Kb}(V_m)\,(V_m - E_{\text{K}})$$

$I_{\text{Kb}}$ replaces TTP06's $I_{\text{pK}}$. The gating is instantaneous (a sigmoidal function of voltage). Like its predecessor, it provides a small outward current during the plateau.

6.5   Pumps, Exchangers, and Background Currents

Na$^+$/K$^+$ ATPase ($I_{\text{NaK}}$) — Thermodynamic Cycle Model

The Na$^+$/K$^+$-ATPase in ORd represents the most significant single-equation change from TTP06. Instead of the Hill-type empirical formulation, ORd uses a thermodynamically constrained cycle model that explicitly accounts for the pump's conformational states:

(6.11) $$I_{\text{NaK}} = 30\,\frac{z_{\text{Na}} \cdot a_1 \cdot k_{1p} - z_{\text{Na}} \cdot b_1 \cdot k_{1m}}{1 + \frac{a_1\,k_{1p}}{b_2\,k_{2m}} + \frac{b_1\,k_{1m}}{a_2\,k_{2p}} + \frac{a_2\,k_{2p}}{b_3\,k_{3m}} + \frac{b_2\,k_{2m}}{a_3\,k_{3p}} + \frac{a_3\,k_{3p}}{b_4\,k_{4m}} + \frac{b_3\,k_{3m}}{a_4\,k_{4p}}}$$

The rate constants $a_i$, $b_i$, $k_{ip}$, $k_{im}$ depend on $[\text{Na}^+]_i$, $[\text{K}^+]_o$, ATP, and $V_m$ through microscopic detailed balance. This formulation satisfies the second law of thermodynamics — the pump cannot generate flux against its electrochemical gradient without ATP hydrolysis, a constraint that the simpler TTP06 formulation does not enforce. The practical consequence is more accurate pump behavior under extreme conditions (ischemia, low ATP, hyperkalemia) where the driving forces are close to equilibrium.

Na$^+$/Ca$^{2+}$ Exchanger — Split Populations

ORd explicitly models two NCX populations operating in different calcium environments:

(6.12) $$I_{\text{NaCa}} = I_{\text{NaCa,i}} + I_{\text{NaCa,ss}}$$

where $I_{\text{NaCa,i}}$ (80% of NCX) senses bulk cytoplasmic $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$ and $[\text{Na}^+]_i$, while $I_{\text{NaCa,ss}}$ (20% of NCX) senses subspace $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}$ and $[\text{Na}^+]_{\text{ss}}$. Each uses a thermodynamic formulation:

(6.13) $$I_{\text{NaCa,x}} = 0.8\,\bar{g}_{\text{NaCa}}\cdot\frac{k_{3p}\,k_{4pp}\,[\text{Na}^+]_x^3 - k_{3pp}\,k_{4p}\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_x}{(1 + Q_{10})(h_1 \cdot h_2 + h_3)(1/h_7 + h_8)}$$

The split is physiologically motivated: NCX molecules near the dyadic cleft "see" a very different calcium signal (high, fast transient from CICR) than those in the bulk cytoplasm (lower, slower rise). In TTP06's single-population NCX, forward-mode extrusion is driven by a spatially averaged $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$ that underestimates the local signal near the release sites. The subspace NCX population responds to the actual local calcium in the dyad, providing more accurate calcium cycling and a better match to experimental measurements of NCX current during the action potential.

Sarcolemmal Ca$^{2+}$ Pump ($I_{\text{pCa}}$)

(6.14) $$I_{\text{pCa}} = \frac{\bar{g}_{\text{pCa}}\,[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i}{K_{m,\text{pCa}} + [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i}$$

The PMCA formulation is essentially unchanged from TTP06: a Michaelis–Menten-style saturating function of cytoplasmic calcium. It contributes roughly 1–2% of total calcium extrusion per beat.

Background Leak Currents ($I_{\text{Nab}}$, $I_{\text{Cab}}$)

(6.15) $$I_{\text{Nab}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Nab}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Na}})$$
(6.16) $$I_{\text{Cab}} = P_{\text{Cab}}\cdot\Psi_{\text{Ca}}(V_m, [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i)$$

The sodium background current retains TTP06's Ohmic formulation. However, the calcium background current in ORd switches from an Ohmic to a GHK formulation ($\Psi_{\text{Ca}}$), providing more physically correct behavior at the very low intracellular Ca$^{2+}$ concentrations where the Ohmic approximation breaks down.

Chloride Currents ($I_{\text{ClCa}}$, $I_{\text{Clb}}$) — New in ORd

(6.17) $$I_{\text{ClCa}} = \bar{g}_{\text{ClCa}}\,\frac{[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}}{K_{d,\text{ClCa}} + [\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Cl}})$$
(6.18) $$I_{\text{Clb}} = \bar{g}_{\text{Clb}}\,(V_m - E_{\text{Cl}})$$

ORd introduces chloride currents, absent from TTP06. The calcium-activated chloride current $I_{\text{ClCa}}$ is gated by subspace calcium and contributes to the Phase 1 notch alongside $I_{\text{to}}$. With $E_{\text{Cl}} \approx -40$ mV (between rest and plateau), this current is depolarizing at rest and repolarizing during the plateau, adding nuance to the Phase 1–2 transition. The background chloride leak $I_{\text{Clb}}$ is small but ensures chloride balance.

Engine V5.4's ORd implementation omits the chloride currents ($I_{\text{ClCa}}$ and $I_{\text{Clb}}$). Their combined contribution is typically $\lt$1% of total ionic current, and many benchmark ORd implementations (including the CiPA reference) also exclude them. If chloride sensitivity is needed for a specific study, these currents can be added as post-hoc corrections without modifying the core state variable system.

6.6   The CaMKII Signaling Pathway

The calcium/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase II (CaMKII) signaling pathway is the largest conceptual addition in ORd and the primary mechanism underlying rate adaptation. CaMKII is activated by calcium/calmodulin binding and can autophosphorylate to maintain activity even after calcium levels fall — creating a molecular "memory" of recent heart rate.

The active CaMKII fraction in ORd is:

(6.19) $$\text{CaMK}_{\text{active}} = \text{CaMK}_{\text{bound}} + \text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}}$$

where $\text{CaMK}_{\text{bound}} = \text{CaMK}_0\,(1 - \text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}})/(1 + K_m/[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{ss}})$ is the fraction bound to calcium/calmodulin (instantaneous, set by current subspace Ca$^{2+}$), and $\text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}}$ is the autophosphorylated fraction that decays slowly:

(6.20) $$\frac{d\,\text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}}}{dt} = \alpha_{\text{CaMK}}\,\text{CaMK}_{\text{bound}}\,\text{CaMK}_{\text{active}} - \beta_{\text{CaMK}}\,\text{CaMK}_{\text{trap}}$$

The time constant for CaMKII trapping is on the order of seconds — much slower than any gating variable. This creates a hierarchical timescale structure:

Milliseconds: Gate kinetics (m, h, d, f, etc.) — respond within a single AP. Hundreds of milliseconds: Concentration changes ($[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_i$, $[\text{Na}^+]_i$) — settle over a few beats. Seconds to minutes: CaMKII trapping — requires dozens to hundreds of beats to equilibrate.

Active CaMKII phosphorylates four main targets in ORd:

TargetEffect of CaMKII PhosphorylationConsequence for AP
$I_{\text{CaL}}$Slowed voltage inactivation; enhanced recoveryIncreased Ca$^{2+}$ entry → stronger contraction
$I_{\text{NaL}}$Slowed inactivation → increased late Na$^+$Slight APD prolongation at fast rates
RyR / $J_{\text{rel}}$Increased open probability and release fractionLarger Ca$^{2+}$ transient; potential for diastolic leak
SERCA / $J_{\text{up}}$Faster Ca$^{2+}$ reuptake into SRFaster relaxation; maintains SR load at fast rates

The net effect of CaMKII at faster heart rates is: more calcium entry per beat (via enhanced $I_{\text{CaL}}$), faster calcium reuptake (via enhanced SERCA), larger SR release (via enhanced RyR sensitivity), and slightly increased late sodium influx. The enhanced SERCA activity dominates the APD effect — by removing calcium from the cytoplasm faster, it reduces the duration of the calcium transient and the plateau currents that depend on it, resulting in APD shortening. This is how ORd achieves rate adaptation.

CaMKII makes ORd simulations significantly more expensive than TTP06. With 41 state variables (vs. 18), the per-cell cost roughly doubles. But the bigger issue is the slow CaMKII equilibration time: reaching steady state after a rate change requires 300–600 beats (2–5 minutes of simulated time), compared to 50–100 beats for TTP06. For tissue-scale studies where CaMKII effects are not the primary focus, TTP06 remains the pragmatic choice.

6.7   Currents During Each AP Phase — A Visual Map

Figure 6.1. Activity map of all ORd membrane currents across the five AP phases. Bar width represents temporal extent; opacity represents relative magnitude. Red bars are inward (depolarizing), blue bars are outward (repolarizing), gray bars are housekeeping currents, gold bars are chloride currents. Compare with Figure 5.1 for TTP06 — note the addition of $I_{\text{NaL}}$ (persistent through the plateau), split NCX populations, and chloride currents.

As in TTP06, all gating variables in ORd satisfy the relaxation ODE $dx/dt = (x_\infty - x)/\tau_x$. The Rush–Larsen exponential integrator (Chapter 8) applies to every gate, regardless of CaMKII phosphorylation status. The key numerical difference is that ORd has 29 gates vs. TTP06's 12, so the Rush–Larsen evaluation cost roughly doubles. The concentration ODEs remain non-relaxation and require explicit or implicit integration.

6.8   Endo, Epi, and M-Cells: Transmural Heterogeneity

Like TTP06, ORd supports three transmural cell types. However, ORd's transmural differences are more nuanced, affecting more parameters:

ParameterEndocardialEpicardialM-CellPhysiological Basis
$\bar{g}_{\text{to}}$0.020.08 ($4\times$)0.08 ($4\times$)Large epi/M-cell notch; minimal endo notch
$P_{\text{CaL}}$$1\times$$1.2\times$$2.5\times$Enhanced plateau in M-cells
$\bar{g}_{\text{Kr}}$$1\times$$1.3\times$$0.8\times$M-cell APD prolongation
$\bar{g}_{\text{Ks}}$0.00340.00476 ($1.4\times$)ReducedM-cell APD prolongation (reduced repol. reserve)
$\bar{g}_{\text{K1}}$$1\times$$1.2\times$$1.3\times$Resting potential variation
$\bar{g}_{\text{NaL}}$$1\times$$0.6\times$$1\times$Reduced late Na$^+$ in epicardium
$I_{\text{NaCa}}$$1\times$$1.1\times$$1.4\times$Enhanced Ca$^{2+}$ extrusion in M-cells
$I_{\text{NaK}}$$1\times$$0.9\times$$0.7\times$Reduced pump activity in M-cells

The M-cell phenotype in ORd arises from the combination of reduced $I_{\text{Ks}}$ (less repolarization reserve), reduced $I_{\text{Kr}}$ ($0.8\times$), enhanced $I_{\text{CaL}}$ ($2.5\times$), and reduced $I_{\text{NaK}}$ ($0.7\times$). These changes collectively produce the longest APD of any cell type — a feature that is important for generating the T-wave on the surface ECG. The transmural APD gradient (endo $\lt$ epi $\lt$ M-cell) drives the voltage gradient that produces the terminal T-wave inscription.

In Engine V5.4, both TTP06 and ORd implement the same IonicModel interface. The cell_type parameter selects transmural variant by switching maximal conductances. A simulation can mix cell types spatially — endocardial cells on the inner surface, epicardial on the outer surface, M-cells in between — reproducing the full transmural gradient with a single model class.

6.9   Calcium Handling: Two SR Compartments

ORd refines the calcium subsystem beyond TTP06's three-compartment model (subspace, cytoplasm, SR) by splitting the SR into two functionally distinct regions:

Network SR (nSR): The bulk of the SR volume. SERCA pumps calcium from the cytoplasm into the nSR. Calcium diffuses freely within the nSR toward the junctional terminals.

Junctional SR (jSR): The terminal cisternae directly apposed to the T-tubule membrane, containing the RyR2 clusters. This compartment empties rapidly during CICR and must refill from the nSR between beats.

The calcium fluxes in ORd are:

$J_{\text{up}}$ (SERCA2a, cytoplasm → nSR): CaMKII-enhanced pump with phospholamban relief. At faster rates, CaMKII increases the maximum pump rate, enabling faster reuptake.

$J_{\text{tr}}$ (nSR → jSR diffusion): A simple first-order transfer with time constant $\tau_{\text{tr}} = 60$ ms. This creates a delay between SERCA refilling and jSR recovery — the jSR content lags behind the nSR, explaining why premature beats (before full jSR recovery) produce weaker calcium transients.

$J_{\text{rel}}$ (RyR, jSR → subspace): CaMKII-enhanced release. The release flux is proportional to the jSR-subspace calcium gradient and is activated by subspace calcium (CICR trigger). CaMKII phosphorylation increases the RyR open probability, producing larger releases at faster rates. The ORd release model blends non-phosphorylated and CaMKII-phosphorylated populations, with the total release $J_{\text{rel}} = (1 - \Phi)\,J_{\text{rel,NP}} + \Phi\,J_{\text{rel,CaMK}}$.

$J_{\text{xfer}}$ (subspace → cytoplasm diffusion): First-order transfer with time constant $\tau_{\text{xfer}} = 8$ ms. Combined with the subspace Na$^+$ and K$^+$ diffusion, this maintains the subspace as a distinct microenvironment that equilibrates with the bulk cytoplasm on a timescale of tens of milliseconds.

$J_{\text{leak}}$ (nSR → cytoplasm): A small passive leak proportional to $[\text{Ca}^{2+}]_{\text{nsr}}$. This leak is the starting point for modeling diastolic SR calcium leak in heart failure — by increasing the leak rate, one can simulate the RyR2 dysfunction that drives spontaneous calcium waves and DADs.

6.10   TTP06 vs. ORd at a Glance

The following table summarizes the key differences between TTP06 and ORd for quick reference:

FeatureTTP06ORd
State variables1841
Membrane currents1215 (+ chloride)
Data sourceMixed species/cell linesUndiseased human ventricle
Late Na$^+$ currentNot modeledExplicit $I_{\text{NaL}}$
CaMKII signalingNot modeledFull pathway; rate adaptation
SR compartments1 (single SR)2 (nSR + jSR)
NCX populations1 (bulk)2 (bulk + subspace)
Na$^+$/K$^+$ pumpHill-type empiricalThermodynamic cycle

Engine V5.4 implements both models behind the same IonicModel interface. Switching from TTP06 to ORd requires changing a single configuration parameter — the spatial solver, mesh, operator splitting strategy, and output pipeline remain identical. The step(V, ionic_states, dt) method handles the internal complexity, whether updating TTP06's 18 or ORd's 40 ionic state variables (V stored separately in both). The RushLarsenSolver calls each model's compute_gate_steady_states() and compute_gate_time_constants() to perform the exponential update, and compute_concentration_rates() for Forward Euler — all defined identically on the IonicModel ABC. This abstraction is what allows the same tissue solver to work with any ionic model.

With both ionic models thoroughly characterized — from 18-variable TTP06 to 41-variable ORd — we now have the single-cell building blocks that will be embedded at every spatial point in the tissue. In Part II, we add the spatial dimension: diffusion, the monodomain equation, and the numerical methods that make tissue-scale simulation possible.

Part II
Tissue-Level Monodomain Modeling
Connecting millions of cells with diffusion — from the reaction–diffusion PDE to the numerical solvers that make whole-heart simulation possible.
Chapter 7

The Monodomain Equation

A single cell model tells you what happens at one point. To simulate a wave of activation spreading across the heart, we need to couple millions of these cells through the physics of electrical diffusion. The monodomain equation is this coupling — and it is the equation that Engine V5.4 was built to solve.

7.1   From Single Cell to Tissue: Adding Diffusion

Imagine you have a row of light bulbs connected by wires. If you turn on one bulb in the middle, electrical current flows through the wires to its neighbors, and they start to glow too — then their neighbors light up, and so on down the line. That is exactly what happens in cardiac tissue. One cell depolarizes (the "bulb turns on"), and current flows through gap junctions (the "wires") to neighboring cells, triggering them in sequence. This chain reaction is the propagating action potential.

In Chapter 1, we wrote the membrane current equation for a single cell: $C_m \, dV_m/dt = -I_{\text{ion}}$. This says "the voltage changes because ions flow through channels." But in tissue, there is a second source of current: electrotonic current from neighboring cells, flowing through gap junctions.

When cell A depolarizes before cell B, a voltage gradient exists between them, and current flows from A to B through the intracellular pathway. This is just Ohm's law — voltage difference divided by resistance gives current — written in continuous form:

$$\mathbf{J}_i = -\mathbf{D}_i \nabla V_m$$

where $\mathbf{D}_i$ is the effective intracellular conductivity tensor — it encodes both the gap-junction coupling strength and the geometric anisotropy of the tissue. The divergence of this current, $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}_i \nabla V_m)$, gives the net current arriving at each point from its neighbors. Adding this to the single-cell equation gives us the monodomain equation.

7.2   The Reaction–Diffusion Formulation

Before writing the equation, let us understand what it will say in plain language. At every point in the tissue, the voltage changes for exactly two reasons: (1) ion channels at that point open and close, pushing the voltage up or down — this is the reaction, the local chemistry; (2) current leaks in from depolarized neighbors or out to resting neighbors through gap junctions — this is the diffusion, the spatial coupling. The monodomain equation is simply the mathematical statement that "voltage change = reaction + diffusion."

Written out, the monodomain equation is:

(7.1) $$\chi C_m \frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} = \nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m) - \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, \mathbf{w}) + I_{\text{stim}}$$

This is a reaction–diffusion equation — one of the most important classes of PDEs in mathematical biology. The "reaction" is the ionic current $I_{\text{ion}}$ (local chemistry at each cell), and the "diffusion" is $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)$ (spatial coupling between cells). The stimulus current $I_{\text{stim}}$ represents external pacing.

The monodomain equation says: "at every point in the tissue, the voltage changes for two reasons — (1) ion channels open and close locally (the reaction), and (2) current leaks in from depolarized neighbors or out to resting neighbors (the diffusion)." These two processes compete: the reaction creates sharp voltage fronts, while the diffusion smooths them out. The balance determines the conduction velocity — typically 60–80 cm/s along fibers in healthy ventricular tissue.

The state vector $\mathbf{w}$ contains all the gating variables and concentrations from the ionic model (18 for TTP06, 40 for ORd). These evolve by their own ODEs at each spatial point:

(7.2) $$\frac{\partial \mathbf{w}}{\partial t} = \mathbf{g}(V_m, \mathbf{w})$$

Note that $\mathbf{w}$ has no diffusion term — gating variables do not diffuse from cell to cell. Only voltage (and in the bidomain model, extracellular potential) spreads spatially. This locality of the ionic state is what makes operator splitting so natural.

7.3   Conductivity, Fibers, and Anisotropy

Think of cardiac tissue like a bundle of straws, all pointing roughly the same direction. If you push water into one straw, it flows easily along the straw's length but very poorly sideways into neighboring straws. Heart muscle works the same way: myocytes are long, thin cells connected end-to-end by gap junctions. Electrical current zips along the fiber direction about three times faster than it crosses fibers. This directional preference is called anisotropy, and it shapes the entire wavefront: elliptical in a tissue slab, spiraling in the ventricle wall where fibers rotate transmurally from epicardium to endocardium.

Mathematically, a single number (scalar conductivity) cannot capture "fast in one direction, slow in another." We need a tensor — essentially a $2 \times 2$ or $3 \times 3$ matrix that converts a voltage gradient in any direction into the resulting current in that direction. If $\mathbf{f}$ is the local fiber direction (a unit vector), the conductivity tensor is:

(7.3) $$\mathbf{D} = d_\parallel \mathbf{f}\mathbf{f}^T + d_\perp(\mathbf{I} - \mathbf{f}\mathbf{f}^T)$$

where $d_\parallel$ and $d_\perp$ are the conductivities along and across fibers, respectively. Typical values are $d_\parallel \approx 0.174$ and $d_\perp \approx 0.019$ mm$^2$/ms, giving an anisotropy ratio of about 3:1 in conduction velocity (since CV $\propto \sqrt{d}$). In 3D, a sheet-normal direction adds a third axis with even lower conductivity.

7.4   Spatial Discretization: What It Means Physically

The continuous diffusion operator $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)$ answers a simple physical question: how much net electrical current is flowing into this point from its neighbors? Spatial discretization replaces this continuous question with a discrete one: given the voltage at every mesh point, compute the net current arriving at each point from its neighbors. This computation turns out to be a matrix-vector product.

Think of it this way. If node $i$ is at $-85$ mV (resting) and its neighbor $j$ is at $+20$ mV (depolarized), there is a voltage difference of 105 mV driving current from $j$ to $i$ through the intracellular resistance. The amount of current depends on the conductivity between them and the distance. Summing up these pairwise contributions from all neighbors of node $i$ gives the net diffusive current — and that sum is exactly one row of the matrix-vector product $\mathbf{K}\cdot\mathbf{V}$.

Three classical methods exist for building this matrix. Each approaches the problem from a different mathematical direction, but all three produce the same object: a sparse matrix that encodes how current flows through the tissue mesh. The matrix is sparse because each node couples only to its immediate neighbors — a node in the middle of a 3D mesh might have 6–26 neighbors out of millions of total nodes.

MethodCore IdeaMesh RequirementBest For
FDMReplace derivatives with neighbor differencesRegular Cartesian gridTissue slabs, GPU computing, fast prototyping
FEMReformulate as integral (weak form)Any: triangles, tetrahedra, hexahedraRealistic heart geometries, curved boundaries
FVMBalance fluxes across control-volume facesAny with orthogonal dual meshSharp conductivity jumps (scar/healthy boundary)

We will work through each method with the same running philosophy: show the matrix. For each method, we will build the actual stiffness matrix for a tiny example mesh, inspect its structure, and verify that it makes physical sense. Then in Section 7.9, we will connect these matrices back to the ionic models from Chapters 5–6, showing how TTP06's 12 currents or ORd's 15 currents feed into the full system.

7.5   Finite Difference Method (FDM)

The Central Idea

FDM is the most direct approach: place nodes on a regular grid with uniform spacing $h$, and approximate the second derivative using the values at neighboring grid points. The fundamental approximation in 1D is:

$$d\,\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial x^2}\bigg|_i \;\approx\; \frac{d}{h^2}\bigl(V_{i-1} - 2V_i + V_{i+1}\bigr)$$

This is second-order accurate (error $\sim O(h^2)$), derived from Taylor expansion. But the deeper insight is geometric: the expression $V_{i-1} - 2V_i + V_{i+1}$ measures how much $V_i$ deviates from the average of its neighbors. If $V_i$ is higher than the average $\tfrac{1}{2}(V_{i-1} + V_{i+1})$, the Laplacian is negative — current flows outward, pulling $V_i$ down. If $V_i$ is lower than the average, current flows inward, pushing $V_i$ up. Diffusion always acts to smooth out voltage differences.

Worked Example: 5-Node 1D Cable

Consider a tiny 1D cardiac cable with 5 nodes, spacing $h = 0.2$ mm, and uniform conductivity $d = 0.1$ mm$^2$/ms. The no-flux (Neumann) boundary condition means no current flows out of the cable ends.

Figure 7.1. A 5-node 1D cable with node 3 depolarized to +20 mV (wavefront). All other nodes at resting potential −85 mV.

The FDM stencil $[1, -2, 1]$ is applied at each interior node. At boundary nodes (0 and 4), the no-flux condition $\partial V/\partial x = 0$ modifies the stencil to $[-1, 1]$ (the "missing" neighbor is replaced by mirroring). Collecting all stencils into a matrix, we get the stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$:

$$\mathbf{K} = \frac{d}{h^2}\begin{pmatrix} 1 & -1 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\ -1 & 2 & -1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & -1 & 2 & -1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & -1 & 2 & -1 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} = 2.5\begin{pmatrix} 1 & -1 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\ -1 & 2 & -1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & -1 & 2 & -1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & -1 & 2 & -1 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix}$$

Three properties of $\mathbf{K}$ to notice: (1) it is symmetric — the coupling from node $i$ to $j$ equals the coupling from $j$ to $i$; (2) it is tridiagonal — each node couples only to its immediate neighbors; (3) every row sums to zero — applying a uniform voltage $V = c\,\mathbf{1}$ gives $\mathbf{K}\cdot V = \mathbf{0}$, meaning diffusion does nothing to a spatially constant voltage (as it should).

Now let us compute the diffusion term $-\mathbf{K}\cdot\mathbf{V}$ for the wavefront scenario $\mathbf{V} = [-85, -85, -85, +20, -85]^T$ (mV):

$$-\mathbf{K}\cdot\mathbf{V} = -2.5\begin{pmatrix}1(-85) + (-1)(-85)\\(-1)(-85) + 2(-85) + (-1)(-85)\\(-1)(-85) + 2(-85) + (-1)(20)\\(-1)(-85) + 2(20) + (-1)(-85)\\(-1)(20) + 1(-85)\end{pmatrix} = -2.5\begin{pmatrix}0\\0\\-190\\210\\-105\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}0\\0\\475\\-525\\262.5\end{pmatrix}$$

Read this result physically:

Node 2 ($-85$ mV, next to wavefront): receives $+475$ mV/ms of diffusive current — a large positive push from the depolarized node 3. This is the electrotonic current that will soon trigger node 2's sodium channels, propagating the wave to the left.

Node 3 ($+20$ mV, the wavefront): loses $-525$ mV/ms — current flows out to both resting neighbors. The depolarized cell is the "source" that drives the wavefront forward.

Node 4 ($-85$ mV, behind the wavefront): receives $+262.5$ mV/ms from node 3. Only half the push that node 2 gets, because node 4 has only one neighbor (boundary).

Nodes 0–1 (far from wavefront): zero diffusive current — all neighbors have the same voltage, so there is nothing to diffuse.

This example reveals the core mechanism of wave propagation. The depolarized region (node 3) acts as a current source, pushing electrotonic current into the resting neighbors (nodes 2 and 4). When this current is strong enough to bring a neighbor above threshold ($\sim-65$ mV), the neighbor's sodium channels fire explosively, depolarizing it and extending the wavefront. The matrix $\mathbf{K}$ encodes all of this neighbor-to-neighbor coupling.

The 5-Point and 9-Point Stencils (2D)

In two dimensions with isotropic conductivity $d$, the Laplacian is approximated by the 5-point stencil:

$$d\,\nabla^2 V\big|_{i,j} \approx \frac{d}{h^2}\bigl(V_{i+1,j} + V_{i-1,j} + V_{i,j+1} + V_{i,j-1} - 4V_{i,j}\bigr)$$

Same idea as 1D: the value at the center minus the average of its four axis-aligned neighbors. The stiffness matrix now has 5 nonzeros per row (the center node and its four neighbors). On an $N \times N$ grid, $\mathbf{K}$ is $N^2 \times N^2$ — potentially millions of rows — but with only $\sim 5N^2$ nonzero entries. This extreme sparsity is what makes large-scale cardiac simulation tractable.

When the conductivity tensor $\mathbf{D}$ has off-diagonal entries (because cardiac fibers are rotated relative to the grid axes):

$$\mathbf{D} = \begin{pmatrix}D_{xx} & D_{xy} \\ D_{xy} & D_{yy}\end{pmatrix}$$

the 5-point stencil can only handle the diagonal terms $D_{xx}$ and $D_{yy}$. The cross-derivative $D_{xy}\,\partial^2 V / \partial x\,\partial y$ requires the 9-point stencil, which includes diagonal neighbors:

$$D_{xy}\,\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial x\,\partial y}\bigg|_{i,j} \approx \frac{D_{xy}}{4h^2}\bigl(V_{i+1,j+1} - V_{i+1,j-1} - V_{i-1,j+1} + V_{i-1,j-1}\bigr)$$

In 3D, the full anisotropic operator requires a 27-point stencil — the node plus all 26 neighbors in a $3 \times 3 \times 3$ cube. The stiffness matrix then has up to 27 nonzeros per row.

Figure 7.2. FDM stencils for the 2D diffusion operator. Left: The 5-point stencil uses axis-aligned neighbors only (isotropic or axis-aligned anisotropic). Right: The 9-point stencil adds diagonal neighbors (green) for the cross-derivative $D_{xy}$ terms from rotated fiber fields.

When to use FDM: FDM is fastest, simplest to implement, and cache-friendly — ideal for regular geometries (tissue slabs, rectangular patches), GPU computation, and rapid prototyping. Its limitation is that it requires a structured Cartesian grid. You cannot fit a regular grid to the curved walls of the left ventricle without staircase artifacts at the boundary. For realistic anatomical geometries, use FEM or FVM instead.

7.6   Finite Element Method (FEM)

Why FEM? The Geometry Problem

Real hearts are not rectangular. The left ventricle is a thick-walled ellipsoidal shell with curved boundaries, trabeculations, and papillary muscles. To represent this geometry faithfully, we need meshes made of triangles (2D) or tetrahedra (3D) that can conform to any shape. FDM cannot do this. FEM can.

The mathematical trick that enables unstructured meshes is a change of perspective. Instead of approximating derivatives pointwise (as FDM does), FEM reformulates the PDE in weak form — multiplying by a test function and integrating. This seemingly abstract step has a profound practical consequence: it lowers the smoothness requirement on the solution, allowing us to use simple piecewise-linear "hat functions" on arbitrary triangulations.

Weak Form: What Integration by Parts Really Does

Before diving into the math, let us understand the idea in plain language. We want to ask: "does my approximate solution satisfy the PDE?" But checking this point-by-point (like FDM does) fails on triangular meshes because our piecewise-linear hat functions have kinks — the second derivative is undefined at element boundaries. So instead, we check the PDE on average, by multiplying both sides by a smooth "test function" $v$ and integrating over the whole domain. If the average checks out for every possible test function, the solution must be correct. This is the weak form.

The magic trick is integration by parts (the multidimensional version of the product rule from calculus). It shifts one derivative from $V_m$ onto the test function $v$, so we only need first derivatives of both — and first derivatives of our hat functions are perfectly well-defined (they are just constant slopes on each triangle). This is why FEM can use simple piecewise-linear approximations on arbitrary meshes, while FDM needs smooth grids.

Now let us see this concretely. Start with the monodomain diffusion term. Multiply by a test function $v$, integrate over the domain $\Omega$, and apply integration by parts (the divergence theorem):

$$\underbrace{\int_\Omega \chi C_m \frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t}\,v\,dx}_{\text{time derivative}} + \underbrace{\int_\Omega \nabla V_m \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla v)\,dx}_{\text{diffusion (after IBP)}} = \underbrace{-\int_\Omega \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}\,v\,dx}_{\text{ionic current}} + \underbrace{\int_\Omega I_{\text{stim}}\,v\,dx}_{\text{stimulus}}$$

The crucial step is the integration by parts on the diffusion term. The original PDE contains $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)$ — a second derivative of $V_m$. After integration by parts, we have $\nabla V_m \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla v)$ — only first derivatives of both $V_m$ and $v$. The boundary integral vanishes because of the no-flux condition (physically: no current flows out of the heart). This matters because first derivatives of piecewise-linear functions are well-defined (they are piecewise constant), while second derivatives are not.

From Integrals to Matrices

Expand the voltage as $V_m(\mathbf{x}, t) = \sum_j V_j(t)\,\varphi_j(\mathbf{x})$, where each $\varphi_j$ is a "hat function" that equals 1 at node $j$ and 0 at all other nodes. Choose $v = \varphi_i$ for each node $i$. The integrals become matrix entries:

(7.4) $$\mathbf{M}\,\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m + \mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}_m = \mathbf{F}$$

with two matrices and one vector:

Mass matrix $M_{ij} = \int_\Omega \chi C_m\,\varphi_i\,\varphi_j\,dx$ — comes from the time derivative. Physically, it weights each node by the "volume" it represents. It is symmetric, positive definite, and sparse.

Stiffness matrix $K_{ij} = \int_\Omega \nabla\varphi_i \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla\varphi_j)\,dx$ — encodes the diffusive coupling. This is the FEM analogue of the FDM matrix we built above. It is symmetric, positive semi-definite (row sums = 0 under Neumann BC), and sparse.

Load vector $F_i = -\int_\Omega \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}\,\varphi_i\,dx + \int_\Omega I_{\text{stim}}\,\varphi_i\,dx$ — contains the ionic current and stimulus. This is where Chapters 5–6 connect: at each node, TTP06 or ORd evaluates $I_{\text{ion}}$, which becomes an entry in $\mathbf{F}$.

Worked Example: 4-Node, 2-Triangle Mesh

Consider the simplest 2D mesh: a unit square $[0,h] \times [0,h]$ divided into two triangles. We use isotropic conductivity $d$ and spacing $h$.

Figure 7.3. A minimal 2D FEM mesh: 4 nodes, 2 triangles ($T_1$ with nodes 1-2-3, $T_2$ with nodes 1-3-4). The diagonal splits the square into two right triangles of area $h^2/2$.

FEM assembles the global stiffness matrix element-by-element. For each triangle, we compute a small element stiffness matrix (3×3 for a triangle with 3 nodes), then "scatter" its entries into the correct positions in the global matrix.

For a triangle with vertices at $\mathbf{x}_a$, $\mathbf{x}_b$, $\mathbf{x}_c$ and area $A$, the gradients of the linear basis functions are constant vectors: $\nabla\varphi_a = \tfrac{1}{2A}(y_b - y_c,\; x_c - x_b)^T$ (and cyclic permutations). The element stiffness entry is:

$$K^e_{ab} = A\,\nabla\varphi_a \cdot (d\,\nabla\varphi_b) = \frac{d}{4A}\bigl[(y_b - y_c)(y_c - y_a) + (x_c - x_b)(x_a - x_c)\bigr]$$

Triangle $T_1$ (nodes 1, 2, 3) with vertices $(0,0)$, $(h,0)$, $(h,h)$, area $= h^2/2$:

$$\mathbf{K}^{T_1} = \frac{d}{2}\begin{pmatrix} 1 & -1 & 0 \\ -1 & 2 & -1 \\ 0 & -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \qquad \text{(for nodes 1, 2, 3)}$$

Triangle $T_2$ (nodes 1, 3, 4) with vertices $(0,0)$, $(h,h)$, $(0,h)$, area $= h^2/2$:

$$\mathbf{K}^{T_2} = \frac{d}{2}\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 0 & 1 & -1 \\ -1 & -1 & 2 \end{pmatrix} \qquad \text{(for nodes 1, 3, 4)}$$

Global assembly: Add element matrices into the global $4 \times 4$ matrix. Node 1 appears in both triangles, so its diagonal entry gets contributions from both. Node 2 appears only in $T_1$; node 4 only in $T_2$. The result:

$$\mathbf{K}_{\text{global}} = \frac{d}{2}\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 & 0 & -1 \\ -1 & 2 & -1 & 0 \\ 0 & -1 & 2 & -1 \\ -1 & 0 & -1 & 2 \end{pmatrix}$$

Check: every row sums to zero (conservation under Neumann BC). The matrix is symmetric and positive semi-definite with a single zero eigenvalue (the constant mode). Physically, each node couples to its connected neighbors with equal weight $-d/2$ — which makes sense because the mesh is symmetric and the conductivity is isotropic.

Notice what happened during assembly: the element stiffness matrices overlap at shared nodes. Node 1's diagonal entry is $d/2 + d/2 = d$ (contributions from both triangles). Node 3's diagonal entry is also $d/2 + d/2 = d$. The off-diagonal entries come from whichever element(s) connect the two nodes. Nodes 2 and 4 are not directly connected by any element edge, so $K_{24} = 0$ — even though they are geometric neighbors across the diagonal.

The Mass Matrix: Consistent vs. Lumped

The mass matrix for a linear triangle with area $A$ has entries:

$$M^e_{ab} = \frac{\chi C_m A}{12}\times\begin{cases} 2 & \text{if } a = b \\ 1 & \text{if } a \neq b \end{cases}$$

For our two-triangle mesh (each with area $h^2/2$), the assembled consistent mass matrix is:

$$\mathbf{M}_{\text{consistent}} = \frac{\chi C_m h^2}{12}\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 1 & 2 & 1 \\ 1 & 2 & 1 & 0 \\ 2 & 1 & 4 & 1 \\ 1 & 0 & 1 & 2 \end{pmatrix}$$

This matrix is full (no zero entries for connected nodes) and couples each node to its neighbors even for the time derivative. This coupling is mathematically correct but computationally inconvenient: for explicit time stepping, you would need to solve $\mathbf{M}\,\dot{\mathbf{V}} = \mathbf{b}$ at every timestep, which requires inverting $\mathbf{M}$.

The lumped mass matrix avoids this by concentrating each row's total mass onto the diagonal:

$$\mathbf{M}_{\text{lumped}} = \frac{\chi C_m h^2}{3}\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 2 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix}$$

The diagonal entries reflect the area "owned" by each node: nodes 1 and 3 (shared by two triangles) each own $2 \times h^2/6 = h^2/3$ of area, while nodes 2 and 4 (each in one triangle) own $h^2/6$. With a lumped mass matrix, explicit time stepping becomes trivial: $\dot{V}_i = b_i / M_{ii}$ — just a pointwise division, no linear solve required.

Mass lumping introduces an $O(h^2)$ error — the same order as the spatial discretization itself. So for P1 elements, lumping does not degrade the overall accuracy order. This is why lumped mass is the default choice in cardiac simulation: it preserves the convergence rate while eliminating the mass-matrix solve. The only case where consistent mass matters is when using higher-order elements (P2 or above), where lumping can reduce the accuracy benefit.

Element Types

For cardiac simulations, P1 elements (linear triangles in 2D, linear tetrahedra in 3D) are the workhorse, providing $O(h^2)$ accuracy with minimal overhead. The gradients are constant within each element, making the integrals trivial. P2 elements (quadratic) achieve $O(h^3)$ accuracy but at $\sim$8× the memory cost. For the monodomain problem — where the ionic model, not the linear solve, is the bottleneck — P1 elements with mesh refinement are generally preferred.

When to use FEM: FEM is the method of choice for realistic anatomical heart geometries. It handles any mesh shape (triangles, tetrahedra, hexahedra), naturally accommodates curved boundaries, and has rigorous mathematical convergence theory. Its main cost is the assembly step (computing element stiffness matrices and scattering into the global matrix) and the need for a mass matrix. For structured Cartesian grids, FDM is faster; for problems dominated by sharp conductivity jumps, FVM may be more appropriate.

Engine V5.4 supports P1 FEM on triangular (2D) and tetrahedral (3D) meshes. The mass matrix can be either consistent or lumped (diagonal). The stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$ is assembled element-by-element and stored in CSR (Compressed Sparse Row) format. Both explicit solvers (which need only $\mathbf{K}\mathbf{V}$ products) and implicit solvers (which solve $(\mathbf{M} + \alpha\,\mathbf{K})\mathbf{V} = \mathbf{b}$) operate through the same assembled matrices.

7.7   Finite Volume Method (FVM)

The Conservation Principle

FVM starts from the most physically intuitive formulation: conservation. Around each node $i$, draw a control volume $\Omega_i$ — the region of space "owned" by that node. Integrate the monodomain equation over $\Omega_i$:

$$\chi C_m\,|\Omega_i|\,\frac{dV_i}{dt} = \underbrace{\sum_{j \in \mathcal{N}(i)} F_{ij}}_{\text{net flux from neighbors}} - \chi\,|\Omega_i|\,I_{\text{ion},i}$$

where $|\Omega_i|$ is the volume (or area, in 2D) of the control cell, $\mathcal{N}(i)$ is the set of neighbors sharing a face with cell $i$, and $F_{ij}$ is the flux from cell $j$ into cell $i$. The key insight: the flux leaving cell $i$ into cell $j$ is exactly equal and opposite to the flux entering cell $j$ from cell $i$: $F_{ij} = -F_{ji}$. This ensures that total charge is conserved to machine precision — no charge is created or destroyed at any face.

Two-Point Flux Approximation (TPFA)

The flux between cells $i$ and $j$ is approximated using only the voltages at the two cell centers:

$$F_{ij} = \sigma_{ij}\,\frac{V_j - V_i}{|\mathbf{x}_j - \mathbf{x}_i|}\,A_{ij}$$

where $A_{ij}$ is the area of the shared face and $\sigma_{ij}$ is the effective conductivity at the face. When the two cells have different conductivities ($\sigma_i \neq \sigma_j$), the correct effective value is the harmonic mean:

$$\sigma_{ij} = \frac{2\sigma_i\,\sigma_j}{\sigma_i + \sigma_j}$$

This is the formula for two resistors in series — the face lies between the two cells, so the total resistance is the sum of half-cell resistances. Importantly, if one cell has zero conductivity (scar tissue), $\sigma_{ij} = 0$ regardless of the other cell's conductivity. This makes FVM naturally correct at sharp tissue boundaries.

Worked Example: 1D, 4-Cell FVM

Consider four control volumes in 1D with uniform cell width $h$ and uniform conductivity $\sigma$. Each face has area 1 (unit cross-section). The flux from cell $j$ to cell $i$ is $F_{ij} = \sigma\,(V_j - V_i)/h$.

The balance equation for cell $i$ (interior) is: $h\,\chi C_m\,dV_i/dt = \sigma(V_{i-1} - V_i)/h + \sigma(V_{i+1} - V_i)/h$, which simplifies to:

$$\chi C_m\,\frac{dV_i}{dt} = \frac{\sigma}{h^2}\bigl(V_{i-1} - 2V_i + V_{i+1}\bigr)$$

This is identical to the FDM formula! On a uniform 1D grid with uniform conductivity, FVM and FDM produce the same stiffness matrix. The difference emerges in two situations: (1) non-uniform grids, where FVM correctly accounts for variable cell sizes through the control-volume areas, and (2) discontinuous conductivity, where the harmonic mean gives the physically correct flux while FDM's arithmetic average does not.

When to use FVM: FVM excels when conservation is paramount and when conductivity has sharp jumps (e.g., scar tissue abutting healthy myocardium). The harmonic-mean flux ensures physically correct behavior at material interfaces. The trade-off is that the two-point flux approximation requires the mesh to be "K-orthogonal" (control-volume faces perpendicular to the line connecting cell centers) for full accuracy with anisotropic conductivity. On non-orthogonal meshes, more complex multi-point flux approximations are needed.

7.8   Choosing Your Method: A Practical Guide

The choice between FDM, FEM, and FVM is ultimately a practical engineering decision. No method is universally best — each has a domain where it excels. The following table summarizes the trade-offs:

CriterionFDMFEMFVM
Mesh typeStructured Cartesian onlyAny (triangles, tets, hex)Any with dual mesh
Geometry fidelityStaircase boundariesExact curved boundariesGood with Voronoi cells
ImplementationSimplest (arrays + stencil)Most complex (assembly)Moderate (flux loops)
Anisotropy9/27-pt stencil for off-diagonalNatural (in weak form)Needs K-orthogonal mesh
ConservationApproximateApproximate (exact in weak sense)Exact by construction
Sharp interfacesPoor (averaging artifacts)Good (per-element $\mathbf{D}$)Excellent (harmonic mean)
GPU friendlinessExcellent (regular access)Good (element-level parallelism)Good (face-level parallelism)
Memory per nodeLowestModerate (M + K storage)Low
Mass matrixNot needed ($\mathbf{M} = \mathbf{I}$)Required (can be lumped)Diagonal by construction
Accuracy order (P1)$O(h^2)$$O(h^2)$$O(h^2)$

In practice, the cardiac simulation community uses all three:

FDM dominates rapid prototyping, GPU-accelerated tissue slabs, and machine-learning training datasets where millions of simulations are needed on simple geometries.

FEM dominates patient-specific simulations on MRI-derived anatomical meshes — the standard for clinical applications and the default in major cardiac simulation packages (openCARP, FEniCS-based solvers, deal.II frameworks).

FVM is used for specialized problems involving sharp material interfaces (fibrosis modeling, scar characterization) and in Lattice Boltzmann-based solvers (Chapters 17–18) where conservation is the defining principle.

7.9   Connecting the Pieces: From PDE to Matrix System

We have spent the last five sections building spatial discretization tools — stiffness matrices from FDM, FEM, and FVM. Now comes the payoff: we will derive, step by step, the central matrix equation that the simulator actually solves. The goal is to show you exactly where every term comes from, so that equation (7.5) below feels inevitable rather than pulled from thin air.

Here is the roadmap. We start from the continuous PDE (equation 7.1), break it into pieces, replace each continuous piece with its discrete counterpart, and collect everything into one matrix equation. By the end, you will see that equation (7.5) is just a compact way of writing "voltage changes at every node because of ion channels (local) plus current from neighbors (spatial)."

Step 1: Start from the Continuous PDE

Recall the monodomain equation (7.1):

$$\chi C_m \frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} = \nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m) - \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, \mathbf{w}) + I_{\text{stim}}$$

This is a continuous equation — it holds at every point $\mathbf{x}$ in the tissue simultaneously. A computer cannot handle "every point" (there are infinitely many), so we pick a finite set of $N$ points (the mesh nodes) and ask: what does this equation look like at node $i$?

Step 2: Sample at Node $i$ (the Semi-Discrete Idea)

Let $V_{m,i}(t)$ denote the voltage at node $i$ as a function of time. The time derivative is simple — it just becomes $dV_{m,i}/dt$, an ordinary derivative because we are tracking a single node's voltage over time. The ionic current is also simple — it only depends on local quantities, so it becomes $I_{\text{ion}}(V_{m,i}, \mathbf{w}_i)$ evaluated entirely at node $i$. The tricky term is the diffusion operator $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)$, because it involves spatial derivatives — information from neighboring nodes. This is what sections 7.5–7.7 taught us to handle.

Step 3: Replace the Diffusion Operator with a Matrix Product

Every spatial discretization method does the same thing: it replaces the continuous diffusion operator at node $i$ with a weighted sum of voltages at node $i$ and its neighbors:

$$\bigl[\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)\bigr]_i \;\approx\; -\sum_{j=1}^{N} K_{ij}\,V_{m,j}$$

The minus sign is a convention: we define $\mathbf{K}$ so that it is positive semi-definite (the natural sign for a "stiffness" matrix). The entry $K_{ij}$ encodes how strongly node $j$ influences node $i$ through diffusion — this is exactly what we computed in the FDM stencil (Section 7.5), the FEM element assembly (Section 7.6), or the FVM flux balance (Section 7.7). If node $j$ is not a neighbor of node $i$, then $K_{ij} = 0$, which is why $\mathbf{K}$ is sparse.

In vector notation, gathering all $N$ node voltages into $\mathbf{V}_m = (V_{m,1}, V_{m,2}, \ldots, V_{m,N})^T$:

$$\text{Diffusion at all nodes} \;\approx\; -\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}_m$$

Step 4: Handle the Time Derivative (the Mass Matrix)

For FDM and FVM, the time derivative is straightforward. At node $i$, the left-hand side of the PDE is simply $\chi C_m\,dV_{m,i}/dt$, and we can write $\chi C_m\,\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m$ for the whole vector. In matrix language, this is $\mathbf{M}\,\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m$ where $\mathbf{M}$ is a diagonal matrix with $M_{ii} = \chi C_m |\Omega_i|$ and $|\Omega_i|$ is the volume associated with node $i$ (for FDM on a uniform grid, $|\Omega_i| = h^d$, so $\mathbf{M}$ is proportional to the identity).

For FEM, the mass matrix arises naturally from the weak form (Section 7.6). The time-derivative integral $\int_\Omega \chi C_m (\partial V_m / \partial t)\,\varphi_i\,dx$ produces the entry $M_{ij} = \int_\Omega \chi C_m\,\varphi_i\,\varphi_j\,dx$. This is a consistent mass matrix — it is not diagonal because the hat functions of neighboring nodes overlap. In practice, we often lump it (replace each row with its row sum on the diagonal) so that $\mathbf{M}$ becomes diagonal and trivially invertible, just like FDM's.

In all cases, $\mathbf{M}$ has the physical meaning: it weights each node's time derivative by the "volume of tissue" that node represents.

Step 5: Gather the Ionic Current into a Load Vector

The ionic current and stimulus terms are purely local — they don't involve spatial derivatives. At node $i$, they contribute:

$$F_i(t) = -\chi\,|\Omega_i|\,I_{\text{ion}}(V_{m,i},\,\mathbf{w}_i) \;+\; |\Omega_i|\,I_{\text{stim},i}(t)$$

Here $I_{\text{ion}}(V_{m,i},\,\mathbf{w}_i)$ is the total ionic current at node $i$ — this is equation (5.1) for TTP06 (12 currents, 17 state variables) or equation (6.1) for ORd (15 currents, 40 state variables). The state vector $\mathbf{w}_i$ lives entirely at node $i$: none of its components (gates, concentrations, CaMKII states) diffuse. Collecting all $N$ entries gives the load vector $\mathbf{F}(t)$.

The weighting factor $|\Omega_i|$ is the "volume" associated with node $i$: for FDM on a uniform grid, $|\Omega_i| = h^d$; for lumped FEM, it equals the lumped mass diagonal entry divided by $\chi C_m$; for FVM, it is the control-volume size.

Step 6: Assemble the Semi-Discrete System

Now we put steps 3, 4, and 5 together. The continuous PDE at node $i$ was:

$$\underbrace{\chi C_m \frac{dV_{m,i}}{dt}}_{\text{mass} \times \text{time deriv}} \;=\; \underbrace{-\sum_j K_{ij} V_{m,j}}_{\text{diffusion}} \;+\; \underbrace{\bigl(-\chi\,I_{\text{ion},i} + I_{\text{stim},i}\bigr)|\Omega_i|^{-1}\cdot|\Omega_i|}_{\text{ionic + stimulus}}$$

Stacking all $N$ nodes into vectors and matrices:

(7.5) $$\boxed{\mathbf{M}\,\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m = -\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}_m + \mathbf{F}(t)}$$

This is it — the semi-discrete monodomain system. "Semi-discrete" because space has been discretized (the matrices $\mathbf{M}$ and $\mathbf{K}$) but time is still continuous (the time derivative $\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m$). Every term has a clear origin:

TermOriginWhat it encodes
$\mathbf{M}$Time-derivative integral (PDE left-hand side)Volume weight per node; $\mathbf{I}$ for FDM, diagonal for lumped FEM/FVM
$\mathbf{K}$Diffusion operator $\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\nabla V_m)$Spatial coupling: stencil (FDM), element assembly (FEM), flux balance (FVM)
$\mathbf{F}(t)$Ionic current + stimulus (PDE right-hand side)Local chemistry from TTP06/ORd at each node; no spatial derivatives

Equation (7.5) says exactly the same thing as the continuous PDE (7.1), but in a language a computer can execute: "at each node, the voltage rate of change equals the diffusive current arriving from neighbors (encoded by the matrix $-\mathbf{K}\mathbf{V}_m$) plus the ionic current produced locally (encoded by $\mathbf{F}$)." The matrices are just bookkeeping — a structured way to store the neighbor weights that sections 7.5–7.7 computed.

One Full Timestep: How It All Fits Together

To make this concrete, here is what happens during a single timestep $\Delta t$ using Godunov operator splitting (Chapter 8), FDM spatial discretization, and TTP06 ionic model, on a mesh with $N$ nodes:

Step 1 — Reaction (at every node independently): For each node $i = 1, \ldots, N$, evaluate TTP06's 12 currents from the current voltage $V_{m,i}^n$ and state variables $\mathbf{w}_i^n$:

$$I_{\text{ion},i} = I_{\text{Na},i} + I_{\text{CaL},i} + I_{\text{to},i} + \cdots \quad \text{(12 terms from eq. 5.1)}$$

Update the voltage: $V_{m,i}^* = V_{m,i}^n + \Delta t\,(-I_{\text{ion},i} / C_m)$. Update the 17 state variables using Rush–Larsen for gates and Forward Euler for concentrations: $\mathbf{w}_i^{n+1} = \text{advance}(\mathbf{w}_i^n, V_{m,i}^n, \Delta t)$. This step is embarrassingly parallel — each node is independent.

Step 2 — Diffusion (the matrix-vector product): Starting from the reaction-updated voltages $\mathbf{V}_m^*$, solve: $\mathbf{M}\,(\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} - \mathbf{V}_m^*)/\Delta t = -\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1}$ (for implicit diffusion) or $\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}_m^* + (\Delta t / \chi C_m)\,(-\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}_m^*)$ (for explicit diffusion with lumped mass). For FDM, this is just the stencil applied to each grid point — 5 multiplies and adds in 2D.

Advance to next timestep: Set $\mathbf{V}_m^n \leftarrow \mathbf{V}_m^{n+1}$ and repeat. On a mesh with 1 million nodes, this means evaluating TTP06 one million times per timestep (Step 1), then performing one sparse matrix-vector product of size $10^6$ (Step 2). With $\Delta t = 0.02$ ms and a simulation length of 500 ms, this is $25{,}000$ timesteps × $10^6$ ionic evaluations = $2.5 \times 10^{10}$ TTP06 calls. This is why efficient ionic model implementations and spatial discretizations matter.

This separation of spatial and temporal concerns is a deliberate design choice in Engine V5.4. All three discretizations (FDM, FEM, FVM) implement the SpatialDiscretization ABC, which exposes apply_diffusion(V) for explicit solvers and get_diffusion_operators(dt, scheme) for implicit solvers. You can switch from FDM to FEM without changing a single line of the solver code. The ionic model plugs in through the IonicModel interface — switching from TTP06 (18 ionic states + V) to ORd (40 ionic states + V) changes only the reaction step, not the diffusion step. Chapters 9 and 10 describe the time integrators; Chapter 14 shows how this framework extends to the bidomain's 2×2 block system.

Chapter 8

Operator Splitting: Divide and Conquer

The monodomain equation couples a stiff, nonlinear ODE system (ion channels) with a linear diffusion PDE. Trying to solve them simultaneously would be nightmarish. Operator splitting breaks the problem into two simpler subproblems — and it works remarkably well.

8.1   Why Split? The Stiffness Argument

Imagine you are cooking a stir-fry. The vegetables need to sizzle at high heat for 30 seconds (fast process), while the rice simmers gently for 20 minutes (slow process). Trying to cook both in the same pot at the same temperature is hopeless — you either burn the rice or undercook the vegetables. The smart approach: cook them in separate pots, each at the right temperature, then combine at the end. Operator splitting does exactly this for the monodomain equation: the ionic channels (fast, local, nonlinear) get their own "pot," and the diffusion (slow, global, linear) gets another.

The fundamental problem is timescale separation. During the upstroke (Phase 0), the sodium gate $m$ changes with a time constant of $\sim 0.1$ ms, while the diffusion length scale requires timesteps of $\sim 0.01$ ms for stability. But during the plateau (Phase 2), everything moves slowly — $\tau \sim 50{-}200$ ms. Any method that tries to handle both regimes in a single system must either use tiny timesteps everywhere (wasting computation during the plateau) or use implicit methods for the full coupled system (requiring the solution of a massive nonlinear system at each step).

Operator splitting exploits the structure of the problem. Write equation (7.1) as:

$$\frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} = \underbrace{\frac{1}{\chi C_m}\nabla \cdot (\mathbf{D}\nabla V_m)}_{\mathcal{D}(V_m)} + \underbrace{\frac{-I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, \mathbf{w})}{ C_m}}_{\mathcal{R}(V_m, \mathbf{w})}$$

Instead of solving $\partial V_m/\partial t = \mathcal{D} + \mathcal{R}$ as one equation, we solve them sequentially: first advance the reaction $\mathcal{R}$ (purely local ODE at each point), then advance the diffusion $\mathcal{D}$ (linear PDE, no ionic coupling).

8.2   Godunov (First-Order) Splitting

The simplest splitting method advances from $t^n$ to $t^{n+1} = t^n + \Delta t$ in two stages:

Stage 1 — Reaction: At every spatial point, solve the ODE system $dV_m/dt = \mathcal{R}(V_m, \mathbf{w})$ and $d\mathbf{w}/dt = \mathbf{g}(V_m, \mathbf{w})$ from $t^n$ to $t^{n+1}$, using $V_m^n$ as the initial condition.

Stage 2 — Diffusion: Starting from the updated $V_m^*$ (output of Stage 1), solve $\partial V_m/\partial t = \mathcal{D}(V_m)$ from $t^n$ to $t^{n+1}$.

Godunov splitting is first-order accurate in time: the splitting error is $O(\Delta t)$. For cardiac simulations with $\Delta t = 0.01{-}0.05$ ms, this error is generally acceptable because the spatial and temporal discretization errors are of similar magnitude.

8.3   Strang (Second-Order) Splitting

For higher accuracy, Strang splitting uses a symmetric sequence:

Half-step reaction ($\Delta t/2$) → Full-step diffusion ($\Delta t$) → Half-step reaction ($\Delta t/2$).

The symmetry cancels the leading-order splitting error, yielding $O(\Delta t^2)$ accuracy. The cost is modestly higher (two reaction solves instead of one, though each is half as long), but the improved accuracy can allow larger timesteps for the same error tolerance.

Strang splitting is second-order because of a symmetry argument. If the error of $\mathcal{R}\to\mathcal{D}$ is $+c\,\Delta t^2$ for some constant $c$, then the error of $\mathcal{D}\to\mathcal{R}$ is $-c\,\Delta t^2$ (the error changes sign when you swap the order). By sandwiching — $\mathcal{R}(\Delta t/2)\to\mathcal{D}(\Delta t)\to\mathcal{R}(\Delta t/2)$ — the first-order errors cancel, leaving $O(\Delta t^3)$ local error and $O(\Delta t^2)$ global error.

8.4   The Rush–Larsen Method for Ionic Variables

During the reaction substep, we must integrate the gating ODEs $dx/dt = (x_\infty - x)/\tau_x$. Forward Euler would require very small substeps for gates with fast kinetics (small $\tau_x$). The Rush–Larsen method sidesteps this entirely by noting that for fixed voltage, the gating ODE has an exact exponential solution:

(8.1) $$x^{n+1} = x_\infty(V_m^n) + \bigl(x^n - x_\infty(V_m^n)\bigr)\,e^{-\Delta t / \tau_x(V_m^n)}$$

This is unconditionally stable for any $\Delta t > 0$: even if $\tau_x = 0.01$ ms (the fastest Na$^+$ gate during the upstroke), the exponential simply drives $x$ to $x_\infty$ without oscillation. For slow gates ($\tau_x \gg \Delta t$), the exponential reduces to Forward Euler, so no accuracy is lost.

Rush–Larsen is like solving the "easy part" of the ODE exactly and only approximating the "hard part" (the voltage dependence of $x_\infty$ and $\tau_x$). Since these parameters change slowly compared to the gate dynamics during an upstroke, the approximation is excellent. It transforms the stiffest part of the problem — fast gating — from a numerical headache into a single exponential evaluation per gate per timestep.

In Engine V5.4, the splitting and ionic integration are cleanly separated. The SplittingStrategy ABC (solver/splitting/base.py) owns an IonicSolver and a DiffusionSolver and calls them in the appropriate order. GodunovSplitting calls ionic_solver.step(state, dt) then diffusion_solver.step(state, dt). StrangSplitting calls ionic at dt/2, diffusion at dt, ionic at dt/2.

The RushLarsenSolver (solver/ionic_time_stepping/rush_larsen.py) is a subclass of the IonicSolver ABC. Its step() method follows a specific order: (1) compute $I_{\text{ion}}$ from the current state, (2) compute $x_\infty$ and $\tau_x$ from the old voltage, (3) Forward Euler on voltage, (4) Rush–Larsen exponential update on gates, (5) Forward Euler on concentrations. A separate ForwardEulerIonicSolver is available for debugging. Concentration variables always use Forward Euler because they do not follow relaxation kinetics.

Chapter 9

Explicit Diffusion Solvers

After the reaction substep updates the ionic variables, the diffusion substep must advance $V_m$ under pure diffusion. Explicit time-stepping methods — from the workhorse Forward Euler to the high-accuracy RK4 — form a family of increasing sophistication. This chapter walks through each one, starting from intuition and building to implementation-ready equations.

9.1   Forward Euler and the CFL Condition

The simplest idea for advancing the diffusion equation is also the most intuitive: look at the current state, compute how fast things are changing, and take a step forward. This is Forward Euler — the computational equivalent of "if I'm heading north at 60 mph, in one hour I'll be 60 miles north." It works beautifully as long as you don't take steps that are too large. Take too big a step, and the solution oscillates wildly and blows up — this limit is called the CFL condition, and it is the fundamental price of simplicity.

Here is the core idea: at each timestep, we evaluate the right-hand side of the diffusion equation (the spatial Laplacian acting on $V_m$), multiply by $\Delta t$, and add the result to the current state. We never look ahead — only at where we are. That is what makes it "explicit": the new state is computed entirely from the old state, with no system of equations to solve.

In the semi-discrete form from equation (7.5), the diffusion substep is the ODE system $\mathbf{M}\,d\mathbf{V}/dt = -\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}$. Forward Euler approximates the time derivative by a forward difference:

(9.1) $$\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}^n + \Delta t\;\mathbf{M}^{-1}\bigl(-\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}^n\bigr)$$

When using FDM with lumped mass ($\mathbf{M} = \mathbf{I}$), this simplifies to $\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}^n - \Delta t\,\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}^n$. Each grid point's update depends only on its neighbors at time level $n$ — no global communication, no matrix solve. This is why Forward Euler is trivially parallelizable and ideal for GPU computation.

The price is the CFL stability condition. For a regular grid with spacing $h$ and diffusivity $d$, Forward Euler is stable only when:

(9.2) $$\Delta t \leq \frac{h^2}{2\,n_{\text{dim}}\,d_{\max}}$$

where $n_{\text{dim}}$ is the spatial dimension and $d_{\max}$ is the largest eigenvalue of the conductivity tensor. For typical cardiac parameters ($h = 0.2$ mm, $d_{\max} = 0.174$ mm$^2$/ms, 2D), this gives $\Delta t \leq 0.057$ ms — tight, but workable for the $\Delta t = 0.01{-}0.05$ ms range that the ionic model already requires.

Worked CFL example. Suppose we want to simulate a 2D cardiac sheet with $h = 0.1$ mm (a fine mesh) and longitudinal conductivity $d_L = 0.174$ mm$^2$/ms. Then $\Delta t_{\max} = (0.1)^2 / (2 \times 2 \times 0.174) = 0.01 / 0.696 = 0.0144$ ms. That is below the typical ionic timestep of 0.02 ms — so for fine meshes, the diffusion stability constraint becomes the bottleneck, not the ionic kinetics. This is exactly why Chapter 10's implicit methods exist.

The CFL condition scales as $h^2$. Halve the mesh spacing and the maximum timestep drops by a factor of four. For fine 3D meshes ($h = 0.1$ mm), this can force $\Delta t$ below what the ionic solver needs, making explicit methods the bottleneck. This is the primary motivation for implicit methods (Chapter 10).

9.2   Why Go Beyond Forward Euler?

Imagine driving from New York to Boston. Forward Euler checks your speedometer once at the start and assumes that speed holds for the entire trip. If the road curves, you end up in the ocean. A smarter driver checks the speedometer multiple times along the way — at the start, at the midpoint, near the end — and uses all those readings to estimate where they actually end up. That is what Runge–Kutta methods do: sample the slope multiple times within each timestep and combine the samples into a much better estimate of the true solution.

The key insight is that Forward Euler's error comes from assuming the derivative is constant across the entire step. In reality, the derivative changes as $V_m$ changes. By evaluating the derivative at intermediate points within the step, we can capture this curvature. More evaluations = better curvature tracking = higher-order accuracy. The family of methods that does this systematically is called Runge–Kutta (RK) methods, named after Carl Runge and Martin Kutta who developed them in the early 1900s.

Every explicit RK method follows the same recipe: (1) evaluate the right-hand side at several carefully chosen points, (2) take a weighted average of those evaluations, (3) use that average as the effective slope for the timestep. The differences between RK1 (Forward Euler), RK2, and RK4 are simply how many sample points they use and how they weight them.

9.3   RK2 — Heun's Method (The Two-Sample Correction)

RK2 is like a rough draft followed by a revision. You start by making the same guess Forward Euler would (the "predictor") — but instead of stopping there, you evaluate the slope at that predicted point too, and then average the two slopes. The result is far more accurate because you have sampled the derivative at both ends of the interval instead of just one.

Figure 9.1 — RK2 (Heun's method) visualized. The red arrow is the Forward Euler predictor ($k_1$ slope). The blue arrow is the slope evaluated at the predicted point ($k_2$). The green arrow is the average of both slopes — which lands much closer to the true solution (dashed gray).

Formally, RK2 (Heun's method) advances from $V^n$ to $V^{n+1}$ in two stages. Let $f(V) = \mathbf{M}^{-1}(-\mathbf{K}\,V)$ denote the right-hand side of the diffusion ODE:

(9.3) $$\boxed{\begin{aligned} k_1 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n\right) & &\text{(slope at start)} \\[4pt] k_2 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n + k_1\right) & &\text{(slope at FE predictor)} \\[4pt] V^{n+1} &= V^n + \tfrac{1}{2}\left(k_1 + k_2\right) & &\text{(average both slopes)} \end{aligned}}$$

That final line is the key: $V^{n+1}$ is not the Forward Euler predictor $V^n + k_1$. Instead, we go back to $V^n$ and use the average of the two slopes, $\frac{1}{2}(k_1 + k_2)$. This is where the name "predictor–corrector" comes from: $k_1$ predicts, and the averaging with $k_2$ corrects.

Expanding $f(\mathbf{V}) = \mathcal{L}\,\mathbf{V}$ where $\mathcal{L}$ is the discrete diffusion operator (i.e., apply_diffusion(V) from Chapter 7), the matrix form computed at each timestep is:

(9.3b) $$\boxed{\begin{aligned} \mathbf{k}_1 &= \mathcal{L}\,\mathbf{V}^n \\[4pt] \mathbf{k}_2 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(\mathbf{V}^n + \Delta t\,\mathbf{k}_1\bigr) \\[4pt] \mathbf{V}^{n+1} &= \mathbf{V}^n + \tfrac{\Delta t}{2}\bigl(\mathbf{k}_1 + \mathbf{k}_2\bigr) \end{aligned}}$$

Note that each $\mathbf{k}_i$ here is a bare rate vector (units of mV/ms) — the factor $\Delta t$ is applied only at the final update. This is the convention used by the solver code and is mathematically equivalent to the standard form (9.3) where $k_i = \Delta t \cdot f(V)$.

Why exactly $\frac{1}{2}$? Heun's method is designed so that the Taylor expansion of $V^{n+1}$ matches the true solution through $O(\Delta t^2)$. Forward Euler matches only through $O(\Delta t)$. By evaluating $f$ at the predicted point and averaging, we capture the second-order term $\frac{1}{2}\Delta t^2 f'(V^n) f(V^n)$ that Forward Euler misses. The coefficient $\frac{1}{2}$ is not arbitrary — it is the unique weight that achieves this cancellation for the two-stage Heun form.

Worked example. Consider $dV/dt = -2V$ with $V^0 = 1.0$ and $\Delta t = 0.1$. The exact solution at $t = 0.1$ is $e^{-0.2} = 0.81873$.

Forward Euler: $V^1 = 1.0 + 0.1 \times (-2 \times 1.0) = 0.800$. Error = $0.01873$.

RK2 (Heun):
$k_1 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times 1.0) = -0.2$
$\tilde{V} = 1.0 + (-0.2) = 0.8$
$k_2 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times 0.8) = -0.16$
$V^1 = 1.0 + \frac{1}{2}(-0.2 + (-0.16)) = 1.0 - 0.18 = 0.820$. Error = $0.00127$.

RK2's error ($0.00127$) is 15× smaller than Forward Euler's ($0.01873$) for the same timestep — and it only cost one extra function evaluation.

9.4   RK4 — The Classical Four-Stage Method

If RK2 is checking your speedometer twice, RK4 checks it four times: at the start, twice at the midpoint (using two different estimates), and once at the end. It then combines all four readings with a specific weighted average that is tuned to capture the curvature of the solution remarkably well. RK4 is so accurate that it has been the default ODE integrator in science and engineering since the 1900s — a century of trust.

The genius of RK4 is its midpoint sampling. Rather than just checking the endpoints (like RK2), it takes two independent samples at $t^n + \Delta t/2$. Why the midpoint? Because the midpoint rule for integration is second-order accurate all by itself, and by doing it twice (with refined estimates), the errors cancel to fourth order. The final weighted average — giving the midpoint samples twice the weight of the endpoints — is not a coincidence. It mirrors Simpson's rule from numerical integration, which achieves fourth-order accuracy by weighting the endpoints and midpoint as $\frac{1}{6}$, $\frac{4}{6}$, $\frac{1}{6}$.

Figure 9.2 — The four stages of RK4. Each colored line shows a slope evaluation; the green result is the weighted average $\frac{1}{6}(k_1 + 2k_2 + 2k_3 + k_4)$. The midpoint slopes ($k_2$, $k_3$) get double weight, mirroring Simpson's rule.

The classical RK4 method evaluates the right-hand side $f(V) = \mathbf{M}^{-1}(-\mathbf{K}\,V)$ at four carefully chosen points:

(9.4) $$\boxed{\begin{aligned} k_1 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n\right) & &\text{(slope at } t^n\text{)} \\[4pt] k_2 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n + \tfrac{1}{2}\,k_1\right) & &\text{(1st midpoint, using } k_1 \text{ to get there)} \\[4pt] k_3 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n + \tfrac{1}{2}\,k_2\right) & &\text{(2nd midpoint, using } k_2 \text{ to get there)} \\[4pt] k_4 &= \Delta t \; f\!\left(V^n + k_3\right) & &\text{(slope at } t^{n+1}\text{, using } k_3 \text{ to get there)} \\[6pt] V^{n+1} &= V^n + \tfrac{1}{6}\bigl(k_1 + 2k_2 + 2k_3 + k_4\bigr) & &\text{(weighted average)} \end{aligned}}$$

Read the stages as a conversation: $k_1$ says "here's the slope at the start." $k_2$ says "okay, I used your slope to walk to the midpoint — here's the slope there." $k_3$ says "let me try the midpoint again using $k_2$'s improved estimate." $k_4$ says "and here's the slope at the far end, using $k_3$." The final weighted average gives the midpoint samples ($k_2, k_3$) double weight because the midpoint carries more information about the interval's curvature — exactly as in Simpson's 1-4-1 quadrature rule.

In discrete matrix form with $\mathcal{L}$ as the spatial operator:
