(9.4b) $$\boxed{\begin{aligned} \mathbf{k}_1 &= \mathcal{L}\,\mathbf{V}^n \\[3pt] \mathbf{k}_2 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(\mathbf{V}^n + \tfrac{\Delta t}{2}\,\mathbf{k}_1\bigr) \\[3pt] \mathbf{k}_3 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(\mathbf{V}^n + \tfrac{\Delta t}{2}\,\mathbf{k}_2\bigr) \\[3pt] \mathbf{k}_4 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(\mathbf{V}^n + \Delta t\,\mathbf{k}_3\bigr) \\[5pt] \mathbf{V}^{n+1} &= \mathbf{V}^n + \frac{\Delta t}{6}\bigl(\mathbf{k}_1 + 2\,\mathbf{k}_2 + 2\,\mathbf{k}_3 + \mathbf{k}_4\bigr) \end{aligned}}$$

Again, each $\mathbf{k}_i$ is a bare rate (mV/ms), not pre-multiplied by $\Delta t$. The factor $\Delta t$ appears only at the final update, which avoids four unnecessary multiplications per step.

Why these specific weights? The weights $\frac{1}{6}(1, 2, 2, 1)$ are not chosen by trial and error. They come from matching the Taylor expansion of $V(t^n + \Delta t)$ through the $\Delta t^4$ term. There are 8 "order conditions" that a 4th-order method must satisfy (corresponding to the 8 rooted trees with up to 4 vertices in Butcher's theory). The classical RK4 tableau — with its midpoint-endpoint structure — is the simplest 4-stage method satisfying all 8 conditions. No 3-stage explicit method can achieve 4th-order accuracy.

Worked example. Same test: $dV/dt = -2V$, $V^0 = 1.0$, $\Delta t = 0.1$. Exact answer: $e^{-0.2} = 0.818731$.

$k_1 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times 1.0) = -0.200$
$k_2 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times (1.0 - 0.100)) = 0.1 \times (-1.800) = -0.180$
$k_3 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times (1.0 - 0.090)) = 0.1 \times (-1.820) = -0.182$
$k_4 = 0.1 \times (-2 \times (1.0 - 0.182)) = 0.1 \times (-1.636) = -0.1636$

$V^1 = 1.0 + \frac{1}{6}(-0.200 + 2(-0.180) + 2(-0.182) + (-0.1636))$
$\phantom{V^1} = 1.0 + \frac{1}{6}(-1.0876) = 1.0 - 0.18127 = 0.81873$

Error = $0.000001$ — five orders of magnitude smaller than Forward Euler, for just 4× the cost. This is the power of fourth-order convergence.

9.5   Stability Regions: How Large Can $\Delta t$ Be?

Every explicit method has a "safe zone" — a region in the complex plane where the timestep is small enough that the solution stays bounded. Step outside this zone, and the solution blows up exponentially. The size and shape of this safe zone determines the practical maximum timestep.

For the test equation $dV/dt = \lambda V$ (where $\lambda$ is an eigenvalue of the diffusion operator), one timestep gives $V^{n+1} = R(z)\,V^n$ where $z = \lambda\,\Delta t$ and $R(z)$ is the stability function. The method is stable when $|R(z)| \leq 1$. For diffusion, $\lambda$ is real and negative (eigenvalues of $-\mathbf{K}$), so we only need the intersection of the stability region with the negative real axis.

The stability functions for each method are:

(9.5) $$\begin{aligned} \text{Forward Euler:} \quad R(z) &= 1 + z & &\text{stable for } z \in [-2, 0] \\[4pt] \text{RK2 (Heun):} \quad R(z) &= 1 + z + \tfrac{1}{2}z^2 & &\text{stable for } z \in [-2, 0] \\[4pt] \text{RK4:} \quad R(z) &= 1 + z + \tfrac{1}{2}z^2 + \tfrac{1}{6}z^3 + \tfrac{1}{24}z^4 & &\text{stable for } z \in [-2.785, 0] \end{aligned}$$

Figure 9.3 — Stability regions in the complex $z = \lambda\,\Delta t$ plane. The method is stable when $z$ lies inside the boundary. Along the negative real axis (which is what matters for diffusion), RK4 allows $\sim$39% larger timesteps than FE/RK2.

A critical observation: RK2 and Forward Euler have the same stability limit along the real axis ($|z| \leq 2$). So RK2 cannot take larger timesteps than Forward Euler for pure diffusion — its advantage is purely in accuracy per step, not stability. RK4, however, extends the real-axis limit to $|z| \leq 2.785$, allowing about 39% larger steps. In terms of the CFL condition:

(9.6) $$\Delta t_{\text{RK4}} \leq \frac{2.785\; h^2}{2\,n_{\text{dim}}\,d_{\max}} \approx 1.39\;\Delta t_{\text{FE}}$$

9.6   Cost vs. Accuracy: Choosing Your Explicit Method

Each RK method trades cost for accuracy. The question is: for a given error tolerance, which method minimizes total work? The answer depends on whether you are stability-limited (the CFL condition forces small $\Delta t$) or accuracy-limited (you could take larger $\Delta t$ but the error would be too large).

Method $f$ evals/step Order Real-axis stability $\Delta t_{\max}$ (relative) Best when...
Forward Euler 1 1 $[-2, 0]$ 1.0× CFL-limited (fine mesh, ionic $\Delta t$ ≫ CFL)
RK2 (Heun) 2 2 $[-2, 0]$ 1.0× Accuracy matters, not quite CFL-limited
RK4 4 4 $[-2.785, 0]$ 1.39× Smooth solution, accuracy-limited regime

The practical verdict for cardiac simulation: Most cardiac simulations are CFL-limited — the sharp wavefront during depolarization demands small $\Delta t$ regardless of accuracy concerns. In this regime, Forward Euler is usually the best choice: the CFL condition forces a small enough $\Delta t$ that first-order accuracy is adequate, and the cost per step is minimal. RK4 shines in accuracy-limited regimes (coarse mesh, smooth solutions) or when coupled with adaptive timestepping, which Chapter 10 does not cover but is an active research direction.

9.7   When Explicit Methods Shine

Despite the CFL constraint, explicit methods remain competitive in several important scenarios:

The mesh is coarse enough that the CFL limit does not bite. For $h = 0.2{-}0.5$ mm (common in 2D studies), the allowed $\Delta t$ is comparable to or larger than what the ionic solver needs anyway.

GPU parallelism is critical. Explicit methods are embarrassingly parallel — each cell's update is independent. No linear system needs to be solved, no global communication is required. On modern GPUs with thousands of cores, this parallelism translates directly to speed.

Memory is limited. Explicit methods store only the current state and the Laplacian evaluation — no preconditioner, no Krylov vectors, no mass matrix factorization.

In Engine V5.4, all three explicit methods inherit from a DiffusionSolver ABC (solver/diffusion_time_stepping/base.py). Each calls self._spatial.apply_diffusion(V) — the $\mathcal{L}$ operator — which encapsulates FDM, FEM, or FVM (Chapter 7). Swapping between spatial methods is a one-line configuration change; the time integrator only sees the operator $\mathcal{L}$. Explicit solvers need no pre-built matrices; only the implicit solvers (Chapter 10) build DiffusionOperators.

Memory consideration for RK4. Each $\mathbf{k}_i$ is a full-length voltage vector (one float per mesh node). RK4 stores 4 of these simultaneously, plus the original $\mathbf{V}^n$. For a 3D mesh with $10^6$ nodes at 8 bytes/float, that is $5 \times 8$ MB $= 40$ MB — trivial on a CPU, but meaningful on a GPU where shared memory is limited. Forward Euler needs only one extra vector ($2 \times 8$ MB). This is another reason why Forward Euler dominates GPU cardiac codes.

Chapter 10

Implicit Diffusion Solvers

When meshes are fine or three-dimensional, the CFL constraint makes explicit methods impractical. Implicit methods trade the simplicity of a matrix-free update for unconditional stability — at the cost of solving a linear system at every timestep.

10.1   Why Implicit? The CFL Wall

Explicit methods (Chapter 9) are like steering a boat by looking only at where you are. If the river is calm, this works fine. But in rapids (fine meshes, fast diffusion), the boat oscillates wildly because you are always reacting to where you were, not where you are going. Implicit methods fix this by peeking ahead: they ask "where will I be at the end of this step?" and solve for that future state directly. This costs more per step (you must solve a system of equations), but the payoff is unconditional stability — you can take any step size without blowing up.

The fundamental issue is that the CFL condition (equation 9.2) scales as $h^2$: halve the mesh spacing and the maximum timestep drops by a factor of four. For a 3D mesh with $h = 0.1$ mm ($d_{\max} = 0.174$ mm$^2$/ms), the CFL limit is $\Delta t \leq 0.004$ ms — far below the $0.01{-}0.05$ ms range the ionic solver prefers. Running 5–12 explicit diffusion substeps per ionic step wastes enormous effort. Implicit methods eliminate this constraint entirely: they are unconditionally stable, meaning $\Delta t$ is limited only by accuracy, not by stability. In practice, this lets us match the diffusion timestep to the ionic timestep, halving or more the total simulation time.

The price is that at each timestep, the new voltage $\mathbf{V}^{n+1}$ appears on both sides of the equation — so we must solve a linear system $\mathbf{A}\,\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{b}$ to find it. This is where the machinery of Chapter 7 pays off: the stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$ and mass matrix $\mathbf{M}$ from the spatial discretization become the building blocks of $\mathbf{A}$ and $\mathbf{b}$.

10.2   Backward Euler (BDF1): The Simplest Implicit Method

If Forward Euler asks "where am I now, and where does the slope say I should go?", Backward Euler asks the reverse: "where do I end up such that the slope there points back to where I started?" This backward-looking logic makes it inherently stable — it cannot overshoot because it is defined by the destination, not the departure.

Formally, Backward Euler evaluates the diffusion operator at the new time level:

(10.1) $$\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}^n + \Delta t\;\mathcal{D}(\mathbf{V}^{n+1})$$

Since $\mathbf{V}^{n+1}$ appears on both sides, this is an implicit equation. To solve it, we substitute the semi-discrete diffusion operator from Chapter 7: $\mathbf{M}\,d\mathbf{V}/dt = -\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}$. Replacing $d\mathbf{V}/dt$ with the backward difference $(\mathbf{V}^{n+1} - \mathbf{V}^n)/\Delta t$ and rearranging:

(10.2) $$\boxed{\underbrace{\bigl(\mathbf{M} + \Delta t\,\mathbf{K}\bigr)}_{\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \underbrace{\mathbf{M}}_{\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^n}$$

This is the exact linear system the code solves. The left-hand side matrix $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}} = \mathbf{M} + \Delta t\,\mathbf{K}$ is symmetric positive definite (SPD) for the standard monodomain problem with Neumann boundary conditions, so the conjugate gradient method converges reliably. The right-hand side is simply $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}} \cdot \mathbf{V}^n$ — a single sparse matrix-vector product.

Backward Euler is first-order accurate ($O(\Delta t)$) and L-stable — meaning it not only stays bounded but actively damps high-frequency modes. This aggressive damping is useful after discontinuous events (stimulus application, wavefront collision) where oscillations would otherwise pollute the solution. It is also the natural first step for multi-step methods like BDF2.

For FDM with lumped mass ($\mathbf{M} = \mathbf{I}$), the system simplifies to $(\mathbf{I} + \Delta t\,\mathbf{K})\,\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}^n$.

10.3   Crank–Nicolson: Second-Order Implicit

Backward Euler looks only at the destination; Forward Euler looks only at the start. Crank–Nicolson averages the two — evaluating the diffusion operator at both the old and new time levels and taking the midpoint. This averaging is what gives it second-order accuracy: by sampling both ends of the interval, it captures the curvature that either method alone would miss.

The Crank–Nicolson method (also called the trapezoidal rule) applies the diffusion operator at the half-way point between old and new:

(10.3) $$\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{V}^n + \frac{\Delta t}{2}\bigl[\mathcal{D}(\mathbf{V}^n) + \mathcal{D}(\mathbf{V}^{n+1})\bigr]$$

Substituting $\mathcal{D}(\mathbf{V}) = -\mathbf{M}^{-1}\mathbf{K}\,\mathbf{V}$, multiplying through by $\mathbf{M}$, and collecting terms:

(10.4) $$\boxed{\underbrace{\bigl(\mathbf{M} + \tfrac{\Delta t}{2}\mathbf{K}\bigr)}_{\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \underbrace{\bigl(\mathbf{M} - \tfrac{\Delta t}{2}\mathbf{K}\bigr)}_{\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^n}$$

Compare with Backward Euler (10.2): the only difference is the $\frac{1}{2}$ on the $\mathbf{K}$ terms and the appearance of $\mathbf{K}$ on the right-hand side. Both sides now involve the stiffness matrix, but the computation is the same: one sparse matrix-vector product for the RHS, then a linear solve with $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$.

Crank–Nicolson is second-order accurate ($O(\Delta t^2)$) and A-stable — unconditionally stable, but not L-stable. The practical consequence: at very sharp wavefronts, CN can produce mild 2$\Delta t$ oscillations (the Crank–Nicolson "ringing" artifact). These oscillations do not grow (A-stability) but they can be aesthetically unpleasant and may trigger spurious ionic model activation. For most cardiac simulations with $\Delta t \leq 0.05$ ms, the oscillations are negligible.

Worked example. Consider a 5-node 1D cable (FDM, lumped mass, $\mathbf{M} = \mathbf{I}$) with stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$ from Chapter 7 and $\Delta t = 0.02$ ms. The CN matrices are:

$\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}} = \mathbf{I} + 0.01\,\mathbf{K}$,   $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}} = \mathbf{I} - 0.01\,\mathbf{K}$

At each timestep: (1) compute $\mathbf{b} = \mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}} \cdot \mathbf{V}^n$ (sparse matvec, $\sim 5N$ flops), then (2) solve $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}} \cdot \mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{b}$ (CG iteration, 10–30 iterations × $\sim 5N$ flops per iteration). Compare with Forward Euler: one matvec per step, but requiring $\Delta t \leq h^2/(2d) \approx 0.003$ ms — meaning CN can take $\sim$7× larger steps for the same total cost per unit simulation time.

Both $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$ and $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}$ are precomputed once at the start of the simulation (or when $\Delta t$ changes) and reused at every timestep. They are sparse matrices with the same sparsity pattern as $\mathbf{K}$. This one-time assembly cost is amortized over thousands of timesteps.

10.4   BDF2: Second-Order with Strong Damping

BDF2 is like Crank–Nicolson's tougher cousin. Both are second-order accurate, but BDF2 achieves this by using two previous time levels — $\mathbf{V}^n$ and $\mathbf{V}^{n-1}$ — rather than averaging the diffusion operator. The payoff is L-stability: BDF2 aggressively damps stiff transients that would cause CN to ring. For the steep cardiac wavefront ($\sim$0.3 mm wide, crossing a grid cell in $\sim$0.01 ms), this damping is usually beneficial.

The BDF2 formula replaces the first-order backward difference with a second-order version using two history levels:

(10.5) $$\frac{3\,\mathbf{V}^{n+1} - 4\,\mathbf{V}^n + \mathbf{V}^{n-1}}{2\,\Delta t} = \mathcal{D}(\mathbf{V}^{n+1})$$

The left side is a second-order backward difference approximation to $d\mathbf{V}/dt$ at time $t^{n+1}$, using the three points $\{t^{n-1}, t^n, t^{n+1}\}$. Multiplying through by $2\,\Delta t\,\mathbf{M}$ and substituting $\mathcal{D} = -\mathbf{M}^{-1}\mathbf{K}$:

(10.6) $$\boxed{\underbrace{\bigl(3\,\mathbf{M} + 2\,\Delta t\,\mathbf{K}\bigr)}_{\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \underbrace{4\,\mathbf{M}}_{\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}}\;\mathbf{V}^n - \mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^{n-1}}$$

The right-hand side now involves two previous voltage vectors: $4\,\mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^n - \mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^{n-1}$. This is the price of being a two-step method: the solver must store the previous timestep's voltage $\mathbf{V}^{n-1}$ in addition to the current $\mathbf{V}^n$. Since $\mathbf{V}^{n-1}$ is not available at $t = 0$, the first step must use BDF1 (Backward Euler) as a bootstrap — a one-step method that requires only $\mathbf{V}^0$.

BDF2 is A-stable but (despite its strong damping) technically not L-stable — that distinction belongs to BDF1. However, BDF2's damping of high-frequency modes is much stronger than CN's. The stability function $R(z) = (4 - 1/(1 + 2z/3))$ satisfies $|R(\infty)| = 1/3$, compared to $|R(\infty)| = 1$ for CN. In practice, this means BDF2 suppresses the "ringing" at sharp wavefronts that CN can exhibit.

10.5   Comparing the Three Methods

Method Order $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$ $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}} \cdot \mathbf{V}^n$ Stability Best when...
BDF1 1 $\mathbf{M} + \Delta t\,\mathbf{K}$ $\mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^n$ L-stable First step; after discontinuities
CN 2 $\mathbf{M} + \frac{\Delta t}{2}\mathbf{K}$ $(\mathbf{M} - \frac{\Delta t}{2}\mathbf{K})\,\mathbf{V}^n$ A-stable Default choice; smooth solutions
BDF2 2 $3\mathbf{M} + 2\Delta t\,\mathbf{K}$ $4\mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^n - \mathbf{M}\,\mathbf{V}^{n-1}$ A-stable, strongly damping Sharp wavefronts; large $\Delta t$

All three methods share the same computational skeleton: precompute $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$ and $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}$ once, then at each timestep perform one sparse matrix-vector product (the RHS) followed by one linear solve (the LHS). The linear solve dominates the cost — typically 60–80% of wall-clock time in an implicit monodomain simulation.

10.6   Linear Solvers: PCG, Chebyshev, and FFT

The linear system $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}\,\mathbf{V}^{n+1} = \mathbf{b}$ must be solved efficiently at every timestep. Since $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$ is SPD (for Neumann boundary conditions), the conjugate gradient method is the natural choice. Engine V5.4 offers three solver strategies, each suited to different hardware and problem configurations:

PCG (Preconditioned Conjugate Gradient) with Jacobi preconditioning is the default. It is robust, well-understood, and converges in $O(\sqrt{\kappa})$ iterations, where $\kappa$ is the condition number of the system. For the monodomain problem, $\kappa \propto h^{-2}$, so finer meshes require more iterations — but typically 10–30 suffice for the tolerance levels needed in cardiac simulation.

Chebyshev iteration is a fixed-polynomial method that requires no inner products — making it ideal for GPU computation, where global reductions (inner products) are expensive. It requires an estimate of the eigenvalue bounds of $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$, but these can be precomputed once. The Chebyshev solver achieves zero-synchronization operation: every thread computes independently for a fixed number of iterations.

FFT/DCT solver applies when the domain is a regular rectangle with uniform spacing. The discrete Laplacian diagonalizes under a cosine transform (for Neumann BC), giving a direct solve: transform → divide by eigenvalues → inverse transform. This is $O(N \log N)$ and exact to machine precision — no iterations needed. It is the fastest option when applicable, but limited to rectangular domains with constant conductivity.

The choice of linear solver often dominates the total simulation cost. In a typical implicit monodomain simulation, 60–80% of wall-clock time is spent in the linear solver. This is why the Chebyshev (GPU-friendly) and FFT (direct) solvers exist in V5.4 alongside PCG — each targets a different performance bottleneck.

In Engine V5.4, the implicit solvers (CrankNicolsonSolver, BDF1Solver, BDF2Solver) each inherit from DiffusionSolver and additionally own a LinearSolver. At construction, each calls spatial.get_diffusion_operators(dt, scheme) with scheme = "CN", "BDF1", or "BDF2" — this returns a DiffusionOperators object containing the precomputed sparse matrices $\mathbf{A}_{\text{lhs}}$ and $\mathbf{B}_{\text{rhs}}$. The step() method is then identical for all three: compute rhs = B_rhs @ V, solve A_lhs @ V_new = rhs. BDF2 additionally stores V_prev and subtracts M @ V_prev from the RHS, with an automatic fallback to BDF1 for the first timestep.

Part III
The Bidomain Extension
When the monodomain assumption breaks down — modeling both intracellular and extracellular domains for defibrillation, unipolar electrograms, and heterogeneous tissue.
Chapter 11

Why Bidomain? — The Physical Picture

Before we write a single equation, let us build a physical picture of what is happening inside a beating heart — and why two "domains" are needed to describe it honestly.

11.1   Currents in a Living Heart

Cardiac tissue is not a simple conducting wire. It is a dense, three-dimensional mesh of individual cells (myocytes), each wrapped in a lipid bilayer membrane that acts as both a capacitor and a nonlinear switch. Between the cells lies a bath of salty fluid — the extracellular space. Inside each cell, the cytoplasm forms another conducting medium — the intracellular space. The two spaces are connected electrically by thousands of gap junctions that stitch neighboring cells together.

When a wave of electrical activation sweeps through the heart, current flows simultaneously in both spaces:

  • Intracellular current $\mathbf{J}_i$ travels from cell to cell through gap junctions, following the fiber direction preferentially (anisotropy ratio $\approx$ 10:1).
  • Extracellular current $\mathbf{J}_e$ flows through the interstitial fluid, also anisotropic but with a milder ratio ($\approx$ 2.5:1).
  • Transmembrane current $I_m$ crosses the cell membrane, carried by ion channels, pumps, and membrane capacitance.

The crucial observation is that these anisotropy ratios are not equal. Current takes different preferred paths in the two spaces. This mismatch is what the monodomain model cannot capture — and what the bidomain model was invented to describe.

11.2   Divergence and Conservation: The Language of Current Flow

To build the bidomain equations, we need one fundamental physical law: conservation of charge. In a region of tissue, charge cannot appear or disappear — it can only flow in or out. The mathematical way to say "what flows out must equal what is produced inside" is the divergence.

What is divergence, intuitively? Imagine placing a tiny balloon at a point in a fluid. If the fluid is flowing outward from that point, the balloon inflates — the divergence is positive. If the fluid converges inward, the balloon deflates — the divergence is negative. For electric current, $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{J}$ measures how much current is "created" at each point (positive) or "absorbed" (negative).

Now apply this to our two domains. In the intracellular space, current $\mathbf{J}_i$ flows from cell to cell. At any point, some of that current may leak out of the intracellular space across the membrane into the extracellular space. That leaking current is the transmembrane current $I_m$. Conservation says:

(11.1) $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf{J}_i = -\chi \, I_m$$

The negative sign tells us: if transmembrane current flows outward (from inside the cell to outside), it is a sink for intracellular current — current is leaving the intracellular space.

Similarly, in the extracellular space, that same transmembrane current is now a source — current arriving from inside:

(11.2) $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf{J}_e = +\chi \, I_m$$

Here $\chi$ is the surface-to-volume ratio of the cells (how much membrane area per unit volume of tissue — typically 1000–1500 cm$^{-1}$). It converts a current per unit membrane area ($I_m$ in $\mu$A/cm$^2$) into a current per unit volume.

The conservation identity. If you add equations (11.1) and (11.2), the transmembrane currents cancel exactly: $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf{J}_i + \nabla \cdot \mathbf{J}_e = 0$$ This is simply the statement that total current is conserved: every charge that leaves the intracellular space enters the extracellular space, and vice versa. No charge is created or destroyed inside the tissue. This identity is the backbone of the entire bidomain framework.

11.3   From One Domain to Two

In the monodomain model, we make a simplifying assumption: the ratio of intracellular to extracellular conductivity is the same in every direction. Under this assumption, the extracellular potential $\phi_e$ becomes spatially uniform (or can be set to zero), and we need to track only one unknown — the transmembrane potential $V_m$. One PDE, one unknown. Simple and fast.

But real cardiac tissue violates this assumption. The intracellular space is strongly anisotropic (current flows about 10 times faster along fibers than across them), while the extracellular space is only mildly anisotropic ($\approx$ 2.5:1). This unequal anisotropy ratio means the extracellular potential is not uniform — it develops complex spatial patterns of its own.

This matters in practice whenever we need to model:

  • Defibrillation — an external electric field interacts with the tissue through the extracellular space.
  • Virtual electrode polarization — field-induced regions of depolarization and hyperpolarization that cannot exist in the monodomain picture.
  • ECG generation — body-surface potentials are driven by extracellular currents.
  • External stimulation — electrode stimuli enter through $\phi_e$.

To capture these phenomena, we must solve for both $V_m$ and $\phi_e$. We need the bidomain model: two coupled equations, two unknowns, one beautiful (and computationally expensive) system.

Figure 12.1. Comparison of monodomain and bidomain representations. The bidomain model introduces a second unknown ($\phi_e$) and a second equation (elliptic), coupled through the intracellular conductivity tensor.
Chapter 12

The Bidomain Equations

We now derive the bidomain equations step by step, building on the conservation laws of Chapter 12 and the monodomain equation familiar from Engine V5.4.

12.1   The Monodomain Equation (Review)

The monodomain equation, which Engine V5.4 solves, describes how the transmembrane potential $V_m$ evolves in time:

(12.1) $$\chi \, C_m \frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} = -\chi \, I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, \mathbf{w}) + \nabla \cdot \bigl(\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m\bigr) + I_{\text{stim}}$$

Reading each term from left to right:

  • Left-hand side: rate of change of membrane charge ($\chi C_m\, \partial V_m/\partial t$). The membrane is a capacitor.
  • $-\chi\,I_{\text{ion}}$: ionic currents through membrane channels — these are nonlinear functions of $V_m$ and gating variables $\mathbf{w}$.
  • $\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}\,\nabla V_m)$: diffusion of voltage through the tissue. The tensor $\mathbf{D}$ encodes the effective conductivity — a blend of intracellular and extracellular contributions.
  • $I_{\text{stim}}$: externally applied stimulus current.

This is a single parabolic PDE (it has a time derivative and a second-order spatial derivative). It is well-suited to standard diffusion solvers, LBM, or finite-element methods.

12.2   Building the Bidomain System

Let us now be more precise. In each domain, Ohm's law gives us the current density:

$$\mathbf{J}_i = -\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla \phi_i, \qquad \mathbf{J}_e = -\mathbf{D}_e\,\nabla \phi_e$$

where $\phi_i$ and $\phi_e$ are the intracellular and extracellular potentials, and $\mathbf{D}_i$, $\mathbf{D}_e$ are their respective conductivity tensors.

The transmembrane potential is defined as their difference:

$$V_m = \phi_i - \phi_e$$

The transmembrane current per unit volume is the sum of capacitive and ionic components:

$$\chi\, I_m = \chi\,C_m\,\frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} + \chi\, I_{\text{ion}}(V_m, \mathbf{w})$$

Now substitute into the conservation laws from Chapter 12. From the intracellular conservation $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{J}_i = -\chi I_m$:

$$\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla\phi_i) = \chi\,C_m\,\frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} + \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}$$

Since $\phi_i = V_m + \phi_e$, we can rewrite $\nabla\phi_i = \nabla V_m + \nabla\phi_e$, giving us:

(12.2) $$\chi\,C_m\,\frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t} = -\chi\,I_{\text{ion}}(V_m,\mathbf{w}) + \nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla V_m) + \nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla\phi_e) + I_{\text{stim}}^i$$

This is the parabolic equation of the bidomain system. Compare it to the monodomain equation (12.1): the key difference is the extra coupling term $\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla\phi_e)$, which accounts for how the extracellular potential gradient drives intracellular current.

For the second equation, add both conservation laws: $\nabla\cdot\mathbf{J}_i + \nabla\cdot\mathbf{J}_e = 0$. Expanding:

(12.3) $$0 = \nabla\cdot\bigl(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla V_m\bigr) + \nabla\cdot\bigl[(\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e)\,\nabla\phi_e\bigr] + I_{\text{stim}}^e$$

This is the elliptic equation. It has no time derivative — it is a constraint that must hold at every instant. Think of it as saying: "at each moment, the total current flowing into and out of every point in the tissue must balance."

Why is the second equation elliptic? Because the extracellular space has no "memory" — it equilibrates instantaneously (on the timescale of cardiac propagation). There is no capacitor in the extracellular space storing charge over time. The extracellular potential adjusts immediately to satisfy current conservation. This makes $\phi_e$ a slave variable: it is completely determined by the current state of $V_m$ at each instant.

12.3   The Parabolic–Elliptic Couple

Equations (12.2) and (12.3) together form the bidomain system. Let us highlight their structure:

Equation Type Unknown Character
(12.2) — Membrane dynamics Parabolic $V_m$ (primary) Evolves in time; has $\partial V_m/\partial t$
(12.3) — Current conservation Elliptic $\phi_e$ (constraint) No time derivative; instantaneous balance

The two equations are bidirectionally coupled: the parabolic equation depends on $\phi_e$ (through $\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla\phi_e)$), and the elliptic equation depends on $V_m$ (through $\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla V_m)$). Neither can be solved independently.

Additionally, the gating variables $\mathbf{w}$ evolve according to a system of ODEs:

(12.4) $$\frac{d\mathbf{w}}{dt} = \mathbf{h}(V_m, \mathbf{w})$$

These ODEs are local — they depend only on $V_m$ and $\mathbf{w}$ at each point, not on spatial derivatives. This is good news: the ionic solver from Engine V5.4 (e.g., Rush–Larsen exponential integrator) can be reused without modification.

12.4   When Bidomain Reduces to Monodomain

Under the equal anisotropy ratio assumption — meaning $\mathbf{D}_e = \lambda\,\mathbf{D}_i$ for some positive scalar $\lambda$ — the elliptic equation (12.3) degenerates. With this assumption, the combined conductivity $(\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e) = (1+\lambda)\,\mathbf{D}_i$, and the elliptic equation becomes $\nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla[V_m + (1+\lambda)\phi_e]) = 0$, which implies $\phi_e$ is essentially constant (or zero with appropriate boundary conditions). The parabolic equation then reduces to the monodomain form with an effective conductivity:

$$\mathbf{D}_{\text{eff}} = \frac{\mathbf{D}_i \, \mathbf{D}_e}{\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e}$$

In real cardiac tissue, however, the anisotropy ratios are markedly different:

Domain$\sigma_L$ (S/cm)$\sigma_T$ (S/cm)Ratio $\sigma_L/\sigma_T$
Intracellular0.3 – 0.50.03 – 0.05$\approx$ 10:1
Extracellular0.3 – 0.60.15 – 0.3$\approx$ 2.5:1

Because 10:1 $\neq$ 2.5:1, the equal anisotropy assumption fails, and the full bidomain system is needed.

12.5   Conductivity Tensors and Fiber Architecture

In each domain, the conductivity tensor is expressed in local fiber coordinates as a diagonal matrix:

$$\mathbf{D}_d^{\text{local}} = \begin{pmatrix}\sigma_d^L & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & \sigma_d^T & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & \sigma_d^T\end{pmatrix}, \quad d \in \{i, e\}$$

where $\sigma_d^L$ and $\sigma_d^T$ are the longitudinal (along-fiber) and transverse (cross-fiber) conductivities. In an anatomically realistic heart model, the fibers rotate transmurally (about 120° from epicardium to endocardium), so the local tensor must be rotated into global coordinates via:

$$\mathbf{D}_d^{\text{global}}(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{R}(\theta(\mathbf{x}))\;\mathbf{D}_d^{\text{local}}\;\mathbf{R}(\theta(\mathbf{x}))^T$$

This makes the conductivity a spatially varying tensor field — one of the major implementation challenges for any bidomain solver.

Engine V5.4 already handles fiber-aligned conductivity tensors for the monodomain model. For bidomain, the same rotation machinery can be reused — we simply need to maintain two tensor fields ($\mathbf{D}_i$ and $\mathbf{D}_e$) instead of one effective $\mathbf{D}$.

Chapter 13

Spatial Discretization

Chapter 7 developed the spatial discretization toolkit — FDM, FEM, FVM — for a single diffusion operator. Here we apply those same methods to the bidomain system, where two coupled PDEs produce a fundamentally different algebraic structure: a 2×2 block matrix system with $2N$ unknowns.

13.1   From One Stiffness Matrix to Two

In the monodomain model (Chapter 7), spatial discretization produces a single stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$ assembled from the effective conductivity tensor $\mathbf{D}$. The bidomain model has two conducting domains — intracellular and extracellular — each with its own conductivity tensor. Applying the same discretization machinery (whether FDM, FEM, or FVM) to each domain separately produces two stiffness matrices:

Intracellular stiffness $\mathbf{K}_i$: assembled from $\mathbf{D}_i$ using exactly the same procedure as the monodomain $\mathbf{K}$, with entries $(K_i)_{jk} = \int_\Omega \nabla\varphi_j \cdot (\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla\varphi_k)\,dx$ for FEM, or the corresponding stencil matrix $\mathbf{L}_i$ for FDM.

Extracellular stiffness $\mathbf{K}_e$: assembled identically but with $\mathbf{D}_e$. The combined stiffness $\mathbf{K}_i + \mathbf{K}_e$ corresponds to the bulk conductivity tensor $\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e$.

Engine V5.4's spatial discretization infrastructure already assembles a single stiffness matrix $\mathbf{K}$ (or Laplacian $\mathbf{L}$) from a conductivity tensor field. Extending to bidomain requires calling this same assembly routine twice — once with $\mathbf{D}_i$ and once with $\mathbf{D}_e$ — then combining the results into the 2×2 block format described below.

13.2   The Bidomain Weak Form

For FEM specifically, the bidomain weak form involves both unknowns ($V_m$ and $\phi_e$) simultaneously. Multiplying the parabolic equation by test function $v \in H^1(\Omega)$ and integrating by parts:

$$\begin{aligned} \int_\Omega \chi C_m \frac{\partial V_m}{\partial t}\,v\,dx &+ \int_\Omega \nabla V_m \cdot (\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla v)\,dx + \int_\Omega \nabla\phi_e\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla v)\,dx \\[4pt] &= -\int_\Omega \chi\,I_{\text{ion}}\,v\,dx + \int_\Omega I_{\text{stim}}^i\,v\,dx \end{aligned}$$

For the elliptic equation, multiply by $q \in H^1(\Omega)$:

$$\begin{aligned} \int_\Omega \nabla\phi_e \cdot \bigl[(\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e)\,\nabla q\bigr]\,dx &+ \int_\Omega \nabla V_m \cdot (\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla q)\,dx \\[4pt] &= -\int_\Omega I_{\text{stim}}^e\,q\,dx \end{aligned}$$

Notice the critical structural feature: the intracellular conductivity $\mathbf{D}_i$ appears in both equations — it is the coupling agent between $V_m$ and $\phi_e$. The extracellular conductivity $\mathbf{D}_e$ appears only in the elliptic equation. Expanding in the finite element basis produces the semi-discrete system:

(13.1) $$\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{M} & \mathbf{0} \\ \mathbf{0} & \mathbf{0}\end{pmatrix}\!\begin{pmatrix}\dot{\mathbf{V}}_m \\ \dot{\boldsymbol{\phi}}_e\end{pmatrix} + \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{K}_i & \mathbf{K}_i \\ \mathbf{K}_i & \mathbf{K}_i\!+\!\mathbf{K}_e\end{pmatrix}\!\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{V}_m \\ \boldsymbol{\phi}_e\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{F}_V \\ \mathbf{F}_\phi\end{pmatrix}$$

Notice the elegant structure: $\mathbf{K}_i$ appears in every block of the 2×2 stiffness matrix — it is the "glue" coupling $V_m$ and $\phi_e$. The extracellular stiffness $\mathbf{K}_e$ appears only in the (2,2) block, strengthening the elliptic equation. The mass matrix $\mathbf{M}$ (from Chapter 7) appears only in the (1,1) position because only $V_m$ has a time derivative.

13.3   The 2×2 Block System

Regardless of the spatial discretization method (FDM, FEM, or FVM — all described in Chapter 7), the final algebraic system at each time step has the same form:

(13.2) $$\underbrace{\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{A}_{11} & \mathbf{A}_{12} \\ \mathbf{A}_{21} & \mathbf{A}_{22}\end{pmatrix}}_{\mathbf{A}}\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{V}_m \\ \boldsymbol{\phi}_e\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{b}_1 \\ \mathbf{b}_2\end{pmatrix}$$

With the typical identification (after applying a semi-implicit time stepper like Crank–Nicolson):

BlockDefinitionProperties
$\mathbf{A}_{11}$$\mathbf{M}/\Delta t + \mathbf{K}_i$Symmetric positive definite (SPD)
$\mathbf{A}_{12}$$\mathbf{K}_i$Coupling: intracellular stiffness
$\mathbf{A}_{21}$$\mathbf{K}_i$$= \mathbf{A}_{12}^T$ (symmetric)
$\mathbf{A}_{22}$$\mathbf{K}_i + \mathbf{K}_e$SPD (combined stiffness)

The total system has $2N$ unknowns (where $N$ is the number of spatial nodes) — double the monodomain system's $N$ unknowns. This is the fundamental object that all subsequent solver strategies (Chapter 16) must handle efficiently. The system is symmetric but indefinite — a saddle-point problem that requires specialized linear algebra. This is a qualitative departure from the monodomain, where the implicit system is SPD and solvable by conjugate gradient (Chapter 10).

Figure 14.1. The 2×2 block structure of the bidomain linear system. The diagonal blocks are SPD; the off-diagonal coupling blocks are the intracellular stiffness $\mathbf{K}_i$. The full system is symmetric but indefinite.
Chapter 14

Time Integration and Operator Splitting

Chapter 8 introduced operator splitting for the monodomain equation — separating the local reaction (ionic ODEs) from the global diffusion (PDE). The same splitting framework applies to the bidomain model, but the diffusion sub-step is fundamentally different: instead of a single SPD system, it requires solving the 2×2 block system from Chapter 14.

14.1   Operator Splitting Applied to Bidomain

The splitting strategy from Chapter 8 carries over directly. In Godunov (first-order) splitting, each time step has two stages: (1) advance the reaction locally using Rush–Larsen (Section 8.4), then (2) solve the diffusion sub-step. The difference is entirely in step 2. For the monodomain, the diffusion sub-step solves a single SPD linear system. For the bidomain, it solves the full 2×2 block system:

$$\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{A}_{11} & \mathbf{A}_{12} \\ \mathbf{A}_{21} & \mathbf{A}_{22}\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}V_m^{n+1} \\ \phi_e^{n+1}\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{b}_1(V_m^*) \\ \mathbf{b}_2\end{pmatrix}$$

where $V_m^*$ comes from the reaction sub-step. Strang (second-order) splitting uses the same symmetric sandwich — half-reaction, full-diffusion, half-reaction — as described in Section 8.3. Research has shown that Strang splitting has better stability properties than Godunov for bidomain, because the symmetric arrangement prevents splitting error from accumulating unidirectionally. The recommendation is to use Strang splitting for production bidomain simulations.

The critical new question for bidomain is: what time stepper do we use for the diffusion sub-step? The monodomain's implicit solvers (Crank–Nicolson, BDF — Chapter 10) are starting points, but the bidomain's saddle-point structure and sharp wavefronts demand more careful choices.

14.2   Time Steppers: Crank–Nicolson vs. SDIRK2

Within the diffusion sub-step, we need an implicit time integrator for the 2×2 block system. Two standard choices are:

Crank–Nicolson (CN)

The trapezoidal rule applied to the diffusion step:

$$\frac{\mathbf{M}}{\Delta t}\bigl(V_m^{n+1} - V_m^*\bigr) = \tfrac{1}{2}\Bigl[\mathcal{L}(V_m^{n+1},\phi_e^{n+1}) + \mathcal{L}(V_m^*,\phi_e^n)\Bigr]$$

where $\mathcal{L}$ represents the spatial diffusion operator. CN is second-order and A-stable, but it can produce spurious oscillations near sharp wave fronts — a serious concern in cardiac electrophysiology, where the action potential upstroke is extremely steep ($dV/dt \sim 300$ V/s).

SDIRK2 (Singly Diagonally Implicit Runge–Kutta, order 2)

The SDIRK2 method uses two implicit stages with the same diagonal coefficient $\gamma = 1 - \sqrt{2}/2 \approx 0.2929$:

$$\begin{aligned} \text{Stage 1:}\quad \mathbf{k}_1 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(V_m^n + \gamma\,\Delta t\,\mathbf{k}_1\bigr) & &\text{(implicit solve)} \\[6pt] \text{Stage 2:}\quad \mathbf{k}_2 &= \mathcal{L}\bigl(V_m^n + (1{-}\gamma)\,\Delta t\,\mathbf{k}_1 + \gamma\,\Delta t\,\mathbf{k}_2\bigr) & &\text{(implicit solve)} \\[6pt] V_m^{n+1} &= V_m^n + \Delta t\bigl[(1{-}\gamma)\,\mathbf{k}_1 + \gamma\,\mathbf{k}_2\bigr] \end{aligned}$$

SDIRK2 is second-order, L-stable (meaning it damps spurious high-frequency oscillations rather than preserving them), and requires two linear solves per step — each of the same size and structure as the CN solve, but with a smaller effective time step ($\gamma\,\Delta t$ instead of $\Delta t$).

Recommendation: SDIRK2 is preferred over Crank–Nicolson for bidomain. The extra linear solve per step (two instead of one) is a modest cost increase ($\approx$1.5×), but L-stability eliminates the spurious oscillations that plague CN near sharp wave fronts. This is especially important for bidomain, where the coupling to $\phi_e$ can amplify such oscillations.

14.3   IMEX Methods

An alternative to operator splitting is the Implicit-Explicit (IMEX) approach, which treats different terms with different discretizations within a single unified step:

  • Explicit: ionic current $I_{\text{ion}}$ (nonlinear, local, less stiff)
  • Implicit: diffusion operator (linear, global, stiff)

IMEX avoids the splitting error entirely. The general form writes the semi-discrete system as $d\mathbf{u}/dt = \mathbf{f}_E(\mathbf{u}) + \mathbf{f}_I(\mathbf{u})$, where $\mathbf{f}_E$ is treated explicitly (ionic currents) and $\mathbf{f}_I$ implicitly (diffusion + elliptic coupling). Because the diffusion operator is linear, the implicit part requires only a linear solve — no Newton iteration.

IMEX Euler (SBDF1) — first-order, L-stable, self-starting:

$$\frac{\chi C_m}{\Delta t}\,\mathbf{M}\,(\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} - \mathbf{V}_m^n) + \mathbf{K}_i\,\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} + \mathbf{K}_i\,\boldsymbol{\phi}_e^{n+1} = -\chi\,\mathbf{M}\,I_{\text{ion}}^n$$

SBDF2 — second-order, strong damping, recommended for production:

$$\tfrac{\chi C_m}{\Delta t}\,\mathbf{M}\bigl(\tfrac{3}{2}\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} - 2\mathbf{V}_m^n + \tfrac{1}{2}\mathbf{V}_m^{n-1}\bigr) + \mathbf{K}_i\,\mathbf{V}_m^{n+1} + \mathbf{K}_i\,\boldsymbol{\phi}_e^{n+1} = -\chi\,\mathbf{M}\bigl(2\,I_{\text{ion}}^n - I_{\text{ion}}^{n-1}\bigr)$$

Other second-order IMEX multistep variants include CN-FE (Crank–Nicolson for diffusion, forward Euler for reaction) and CN-AB (Crank–Nicolson with Adams–Bashforth extrapolation for ionic terms). Ethier & Bourgault (2008) conducted a systematic stability analysis and found that SBDF2 provides the strongest damping of high-frequency error — making it preferred for cardiac applications where sharp wave fronts produce high-frequency error components. CN-FE should generally be avoided for bidomain because it can produce spurious oscillations near the action potential upstroke, similar to the Crank–Nicolson issue discussed in Section 15.2.

IMEX Runge–Kutta methods offer a single-step (self-starting) alternative. An $s$-stage IMEX-RK method uses an explicit tableau for ionic terms and a DIRK (diagonally implicit Runge–Kutta) tableau for diffusion, requiring $s$ linear solves per step. The stability regions are generally larger than IMEX multistep methods, but the extra linear solves per step make them more expensive for bidomain.

IMEX vs. operator splitting: IMEX eliminates splitting error entirely, while operator splitting (Godunov/Strang) introduces $O(\Delta t)$/$O(\Delta t^2)$ splitting error. However, operator splitting is simpler to implement, allows specialized ionic solvers (Rush–Larsen), and permits different time steps for reaction and diffusion. For production codes, operator splitting with SDIRK2 remains the most common choice. IMEX (especially SBDF2) is a strong alternative when splitting error is a concern.

14.4   Why Fully Explicit Methods Fail

A natural question arises: can we avoid implicit solves entirely and use fully explicit time integration for the bidomain model? The answer is no, for a fundamental structural reason: the elliptic equation for $\phi_e$ has no time derivative.

Recall the bidomain system. The parabolic equation (for $V_m$) has a $\partial V_m/\partial t$ term that can in principle be marched forward explicitly. But the elliptic equation — $0 = \nabla\cdot((\mathbf{D}_i + \mathbf{D}_e)\,\nabla\phi_e) + \nabla\cdot(\mathbf{D}_i\,\nabla V_m)$ — is a constraint that must be satisfied at every instant. There is no "marching forward" possible for a constraint equation. It always requires solving a linear system:

$$(\mathbf{K}_i + \mathbf{K}_e)\,\boldsymbol{\phi}_e = -\mathbf{K}_i\,\mathbf{V}_m \tag{15.1}$$

This is the single most important distinction between bidomain and monodomain. In the monodomain model, there is only a single parabolic PDE, and fully explicit methods (including LBM) can handle the entire system. In the bidomain model, at least one implicit linear solve per time step is unavoidable.

Even if we accept the mandatory elliptic solve, we can ask: can the parabolic part be treated explicitly? Yes — but at a severe cost. Puwal & Roth (2007) derived the forward Euler stability condition for the bidomain parabolic equation. The CFL condition takes the standard parabolic form:

$$\Delta t \leq \frac{\chi\,C_m\,\Delta x^2}{2\,d\,D_{\max}} \tag{15.2}$$

where $d$ is the spatial dimension and $D_{\max}$ is the maximum effective diffusion coefficient derived from the bidomain conductivity tensors. For typical cardiac parameters with $\Delta x = 0.1$ mm in 3D, this gives $\Delta t_{\text{explicit}} \lesssim 0.5$ μs. By contrast, semi-implicit methods allow $\Delta t \approx 50$ μs — a factor of 100× larger. Weber dos Santos et al. (2004) numerically verified this: for $\Delta x = 3.3$ μm, forward Euler required $\Delta t \leq 0.07$ μs while a semi-implicit scheme permitted $\Delta t > 100\times$ larger.

Because $\Delta t \propto \Delta x^2$, refining the mesh makes the penalty worse quadratically. For production-quality meshes, the explicit CFL restriction is 25–100× more severe than what accuracy alone requires.

Engine V5.4's explicit diffusion solvers (RK2, RK4) work well for monodomain because the CFL restriction for a single parabolic PDE is manageable. For bidomain, these same explicit methods would require prohibitively small time steps for the parabolic equation, and they cannot handle the elliptic equation at all. The implicit diffusion solvers (CN, BDF1, BDF2) are the correct starting point for bidomain extension.

14.5   Explicit Stabilized Methods (RKC)

Runge–Kutta–Chebyshev (RKC) methods offer a middle ground between fully explicit and implicit approaches. An RKC method with $s$ stages is fully explicit (no linear solves for the parabolic part), but its stability domain grows quadratically with $s$:

$$\beta_s \approx 0.65\,s^2$$

where $\beta_s$ is the stability interval length. Standard forward Euler has $\beta_1 = 2$; an RKC method with $s = 20$ stages achieves $\beta_{20} \approx 260$ — effectively relaxing the CFL restriction by a factor of 130×. Each stage requires only a matrix-vector product (cheap, parallelizable), and the total number of stages adapts automatically to the stiffness.

For the bidomain parabolic equation, RKC would work as follows: (1) solve the elliptic constraint for $\phi_e$ (implicit, unavoidable), (2) advance $V_m$ with $s$ RKC stages (explicit), (3) update ionic variables with Rush–Larsen. The elliptic solve is still required, but the parabolic part avoids any implicit linear solve.

Rosilho de Souza et al. (2024) recently proposed the emRKC (explicit exponential multirate stabilized) method for cardiac electrophysiology, combining multirate RKC for diffusion with Rush–Larsen exponential integration for gating variables. This method outperforms the standard IMEX baseline for the monodomain model and is inherently more parallel. However, it has been demonstrated only for the monodomain model so far. Extending emRKC to the bidomain parabolic equation is conceptually straightforward, though the elliptic solve will still dominate the cost (82–90% of total runtime in typical bidomain simulations).

The spectrum of explicitness for bidomain: At one extreme, fully implicit methods (backward Euler, CBDF2) treat everything implicitly, requiring nonlinear Newton solves. At the other, "maximally explicit" methods treat the parabolic PDE with RKC and only solve the elliptic constraint implicitly. In between, IMEX methods (SBDF2, operator splitting) treat ionic currents explicitly and diffusion implicitly. For most production bidomain codes, IMEX/operator-splitting represents the best trade-off — but RKC-based approaches are an active area of research with potential GPU advantages.

Chapter 15

Linear Solvers for the Block System

The linear solve is the computational bottleneck of the bidomain model, consuming 70–85% of total compute time. Choosing the right solver and preconditioner is critical for practical performance.

15.1   The Saddle-Point Structure

The 2×2 block system (15.2) is symmetric but indefinite. This means its eigenvalues include both positive and negative values — a hallmark of saddle-point problems that arise throughout computational physics (Stokes flow, constrained optimization, mixed FEM). The key implications are:

  • Conjugate Gradient (CG) cannot be used on the full system — CG requires positive definiteness.
  • MINRES (Minimum Residual) is the natural Krylov solver for symmetric indefinite systems.
  • GMRES works for any system but requires more memory (stores the full Krylov basis).
  • The condition number of the bidomain system ranges from $10^8$ to $10^{10}$ for realistic meshes — preconditioning is absolutely essential.

15.2   Block Preconditioners

The 2×2 block structure suggests a natural family of preconditioners that exploit the block decomposition rather than treating the system as a monolithic matrix.

Block Diagonal Preconditioner

$$\mathbf{P}_{\text{diag}} = \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{A}_{11} & \mathbf{0} \\ \mathbf{0} & \mathbf{A}_{22}\end{pmatrix}$$

Application: solve two independent SPD systems ($\mathbf{A}_{11}^{-1}\mathbf{x}_1$ and $\mathbf{A}_{22}^{-1}\mathbf{x}_2$) in each preconditioning step. Simple to implement but ignores the off-diagonal coupling, leading to 50–200 outer iterations.

Block LDU (Triangular) Preconditioner

A more sophisticated choice uses the Schur complement:

$$\mathbf{S} = \mathbf{A}_{22} - \mathbf{A}_{21}\,\mathbf{A}_{11}^{-1}\,\mathbf{A}_{12}$$

The lower-triangular preconditioner is:

$$\mathbf{P}_{\text{LDU}} = \begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{A}_{11} & \mathbf{0} \\ \mathbf{A}_{21} & \mathbf{S}\end{pmatrix}$$

With exact inverses of $\mathbf{A}_{11}$ and $\mathbf{S}$, the preconditioned system converges in at most 2–3 Krylov iterations. In practice, we approximate:

  • $\mathbf{A}_{11}^{-1}$ using one AMG V-cycle for the mass-shifted stiffness matrix.
  • $\mathbf{S}^{-1}$ using one AMG V-cycle for an approximation of the Schur complement (often $\tilde{\mathbf{S}} \approx \mathbf{K}_e$ or $\tilde{\mathbf{S}} \approx \mathbf{K}_i + \mathbf{K}_e$).

With these approximations, convergence typically requires 10–30 outer iterations — a dramatic improvement over unpreconditioned solves.

The Schur complement has a physical interpretation. It represents the "effective" equation for $\phi_e$ after eliminating $V_m$. Computing it exactly is as expensive as solving the original system, but good approximations are cheap: for instance, $\tilde{\mathbf{S}} \approx \mathbf{K}_e$ works well because the mass-matrix contribution in $\mathbf{A}_{11}$ makes $\mathbf{A}_{11}^{-1}\approx (\Delta t/\mathbf{M})$ for small $\Delta t$, and the correction term $\mathbf{K}_i\,(\Delta t/\mathbf{M})\,\mathbf{K}_i$ is small.

15.3   Algebraic Multigrid (AMG)

AMG is the workhorse preconditioner for elliptic and elliptic-like systems in cardiac simulations. It constructs a hierarchy of progressively coarser "meshes" (algebraically, not geometrically) and uses smoothing + coarse-grid correction cycles to damp errors at all spatial frequencies simultaneously.

For the bidomain system, AMG is applied at the sub-block level:

  • $\mathbf{A}_{11}$ block: AMG handles the mass-shifted intracellular stiffness efficiently. Convergence in 1–2 V-cycles.
  • $\mathbf{A}_{22}$ or $\mathbf{S}$ block: AMG handles the combined stiffness (elliptic problem for $\phi_e$). This is the bottleneck — the elliptic solve typically consumes 60–80% of total linear solver time.

Available AMG implementations for GPU-accelerated bidomain:

LibraryGPU SupportBest For
AMGX (NVIDIA)Native CUDAGPU-only workloads; best performance on NVIDIA hardware
Hypre (LLNL)CUDA, HIP, SYCLLarge-scale CPU/GPU clusters; mature and robust
PyAMG (Python)CPU onlyPrototyping; integrates well with PyTorch via scipy
Trilinos ML/MueLuPartial (Kokkos)Full solver ecosystem; advanced smoothers

15.4   Krylov Methods: MINRES, GMRES, and Friends

The outer Krylov solver iterates on the preconditioned system until the residual falls below a tolerance (typically $10^{-6}$ to $10^{-8}$).

MINRES

The mathematically optimal choice for symmetric indefinite systems. It minimizes the residual norm over the Krylov subspace at each iteration. Requires a symmetric preconditioner (block diagonal qualifies; block LDU does not, unless symmetrized). Memory-efficient: stores only 3 vectors.

GMRES

Works for any system (no symmetry requirement). Allows non-symmetric preconditioners like block LDU. However, memory grows linearly with iteration count (each iteration stores one vector). A restart parameter (typically 30–50) limits memory at the cost of slower convergence.

Flexible GMRES (FGMRES)

A variant that allows the preconditioner to change at each iteration — useful when AMG V-cycles produce slightly different results each time (as happens with some adaptive smoothers). Memory cost is 2× GMRES.

Recommended solver stack for bidomain:
Outer solver: FGMRES(30) or MINRES
Preconditioner: Block LDU with AMG V-cycles for sub-blocks
Expected iterations: 10–30 per time step
Expected speedup over unpreconditioned: 50–100×

15.5   The Null-Space Problem

The elliptic equation for $\phi_e$ has homogeneous Neumann boundary conditions everywhere (no current leaves the tissue). This means $\phi_e$ is determined only up to an additive constant — the matrix $\mathbf{A}_{22}$ has a one-dimensional null space spanned by the constant vector $\mathbf{1}$.

This must be handled explicitly. Common approaches:

  1. Pin one node: set $(\phi_e)_k = 0$ for some node $k$. Computationally simplest; physically reasonable (choose a "ground" reference).
  2. Integral constraint: enforce $\int_\Omega \phi_e\,dx = 0$ via a Lagrange multiplier. More elegant but adds one extra equation.
  3. Deflation: project the constant mode out of the Krylov subspace at each iteration. Algebraically clean; no modification to the matrix.

For Engine V5.4 extension, we recommend the simplest approach: pin one node to zero. This is easy to implement (replace one row/column of $\mathbf{A}_{22}$ with the identity) and has negligible impact on solution accuracy for cardiac-sized domains.

Chapter 16

Implementation Roadmap for Engine V5.4

This chapter maps the mathematical framework from Chapters 12–15 onto the concrete software architecture of Engine V5.4, identifying what can be reused, what must be extended, and what must be built new.

16.1   Architecture Extensions

Engine V5.4 is built around four abstract interfaces: SpatialDiscretization, DiffusionSolver, LinearSolver, and IonicSolver. The bidomain extension requires changes primarily in the first three:

16.2   Recommended Solver Stack

Based on the analysis in Chapters 13–15, the recommended solver configuration for a production bidomain engine is:

Time Integration: Strang splitting (reaction–diffusion–reaction)

Reaction Solver: Rush–Larsen (reuse from V5.4)

Diffusion Time Stepper: SDIRK2 (L-stable, second-order)

Outer Linear Solver: FGMRES(30) with tolerance $10^{-6}$

Preconditioner: Block LDU with Schur complement approximation

Sub-block Solver: AMG V-cycle (AMGX for GPU, Hypre for CPU)

Null-Space Handling: Pin one node of $\phi_e$ to zero

Expected Performance: 10–30 FGMRES iterations per time step; 10–20× slower than monodomain

Part IV
Lattice-Boltzmann Methods
A radically different paradigm for solving diffusion equations — no matrices, no linear solves, just fictitious particles streaming and colliding on a lattice. From the heat equation to cardiac monodomain and bidomain, LBM trades algebraic machinery for embarrassingly parallel data movement.
Chapter 17

The Lattice-Boltzmann Method: From Kinetic Theory to Computation

In Chapters 13–16, we discretized partial differential equations. We started with physics at the macroscopic scale — fluid flow, heat diffusion, electrical signal propagation — and asked: how do we approximate the derivatives on a computer? Those methods (FDM, FEM, FVM) work brilliantly and are the industrial standard. But there is another path, one that begins not with a PDE, but with something more fundamental: the motion of individual particles, governed by kinetic theory. This chapter introduces the Lattice-Boltzmann Method (LBM). Unlike classical discretizations that approximate a PDE, LBM simulates a discrete analogue of the Boltzmann equation — a statement about how particles move and collide. The miracle is that when you correctly simulate the microscopic collisions, the macroscopic behavior (flow, diffusion, electrical conduction) emerges automatically. For cardiac problems, especially when spatial resolution is tight or parallelization is critical, LBM offers a compelling alternative to classical methods. We will build this from first principles: the Boltzmann equation, its simplification to the BGK collision operator, the discretization of velocity space onto a lattice, and the final algorithm. By chapter's end, you will understand not just what LBM computes, but why the method works — and how to tune it for cardiac electrophysiology.

17.1   The Thermodynamic Foundation: Phase Space and the Equilibrium State

Core Insight: Every numerical method we have studied so far — FDM, FEM, FVM — starts from a macroscopic PDE and discretizes it. LBM takes a radically different route. It starts from a much deeper level: the statistical mechanics of particles. If you correctly model how particles distribute themselves in velocity space and how that distribution evolves, the macroscopic behavior — diffusion, fluid flow, heat conduction — emerges automatically. To understand LBM, we must first understand the space it lives in and the equilibrium it relaxes toward.

Position Space: What You Already Know

In every method we have encountered so far, the unknown is a scalar field: $V(\mathbf{x}, t)$ for voltage, $T(\mathbf{x}, t)$ for temperature, $\phi(\mathbf{x}, t)$ for a generic concentration. At each point $\mathbf{x}$ in the domain, we store one number — the local value of the field. This is position space: a map from location to field value. Position space is intuitive. You can picture a heat map of the heart where each pixel has a color representing voltage. FDM puts a number on each grid point. FEM interpolates between nodes. FVM averages over cells. In all cases, the fundamental object is simple: one value per spatial location. But this simplicity comes at a cost. The macroscopic PDE — the diffusion equation, the monodomain equation — is a coarse-grained description. It tells you how the average behavior evolves, but it has already thrown away information about what individual particles are doing. To build LBM, we need to recover some of that microscopic information. That means working in a richer space.

Velocity Space: What Are the Particles Doing?

Imagine standing at a single point in cardiac tissue and watching every ion that passes through. Some ions are moving fast to the right. Some are drifting slowly upward. Some are nearly stationary, vibrating in place. If you could catalog every ion and record its velocity, you would not get a single number — you would get a distribution. This distribution is the velocity-space view at a single point. It answers the question: "Of all the particles at position $\mathbf{x}$, how many have velocity $\mathbf{c}$?" In a hot gas, the distribution is wide — particles are moving at all speeds in all directions. In a cold gas, the distribution is narrow — most particles are clustered near zero velocity. In a gas with bulk flow, the distribution is shifted — its peak is no longer at zero but at the flow velocity $\mathbf{u}$. The crucial point: at every single location in physical space, there is an entire distribution of velocities. Not one velocity, not an average velocity — a full spread. The shape of this spread encodes the thermodynamic state at that point: temperature (width of the spread), bulk flow (shift of the peak), pressure (area under the curve).

Phase Space: Position and Velocity Together

Phase space is the combined space where you track both where a particle is AND how fast it is going. In two spatial dimensions, a particle's full state is four numbers: $(x, y, c_x, c_y)$ — two for position, two for velocity. In three dimensions, it is six numbers: $(x, y, z, c_x, c_y, c_z)$. The distribution function $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t)$ lives in this combined space. It is a function of position, velocity, and time simultaneously. Its value tells you: at position $\mathbf{x}$, at time $t$, the density of particles with velocity $\mathbf{c}$.
(17.1)$$f : (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t) \;\longrightarrow\; \text{particle density at } (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}) \text{ at time } t$$
This is the fundamental object of kinetic theory. Compare it to the macroscopic field:
Macroscopic (FDM/FEM) Kinetic (LBM)
Unknown $\phi(\mathbf{x}, t)$ — one number per point $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t)$ — a distribution per point
Lives in Position space ($D$ dimensions) Phase space ($2D$ dimensions)
Information Average field value Full velocity distribution
Storage per node 1 value $Q$ values (one per velocity direction)
The extra information in the velocity distribution is not wasted. It is exactly what allows LBM to avoid assembling and solving matrix systems — the transport physics is encoded in the distribution itself, and the collision-streaming algorithm updates it locally.

The Equilibrium State: Where Does the Distribution Settle?

Here is the central question of this section — and arguably the most important idea in all of LBM: When a system of particles reaches thermal equilibrium, what shape does the velocity distribution take? The answer has been known since the 19th century, and it is one of the great results of statistical mechanics. James Clerk Maxwell and Ludwig Boltzmann proved that the equilibrium velocity distribution of a gas is a Gaussian (a bell curve) in velocity space:
(17.2)$$f^{\text{eq}}(\mathbf{c}) = \frac{\rho}{(2\pi c_s^2)^{D/2}} \exp\!\left(-\frac{|\mathbf{c} - \mathbf{u}|^2}{2c_s^2}\right)$$
This is the Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution. Every symbol has a physical meaning:
  • $\rho$ — the total density of particles at this location. This is the macroscopic quantity we are ultimately tracking (voltage, temperature, concentration).
  • $\mathbf{u}$ — the bulk (average) velocity of the particles. For diffusion problems, $\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}$ — there is no net flow, and the bell curve is centered at the origin.
  • $c_s$ — a parameter that controls the width of the bell curve. Physically, it is related to temperature: a higher $c_s$ means a wider spread (hotter gas, faster particles on average). In LBM, $c_s$ will become the lattice speed of sound, connecting the microscopic model to macroscopic transport properties.
  • $D$ — the spatial dimension (2 for 2D, 3 for 3D). The normalization $(2\pi c_s^2)^{D/2}$ ensures the total probability integrates to $\rho$.

What the Gaussian Tells Us: Three Physical Claims

The Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution (17.2) is not just a formula — it makes three specific physical claims about what equilibrium looks like. Each claim corresponds to a different "order" of the distribution, and each will turn out to be critically important when we build the lattice method.

Zeroth Order — Conservation

Integrate the distribution over all velocities:
(17.3)$$\int f^{\text{eq}}(\mathbf{c}) \, d\mathbf{c} = \rho$$
The total amount of "stuff" is $\rho$. This is conservation of mass (or charge, or energy, depending on what $\rho$ represents). The equilibrium does not create or destroy anything — it only redistributes velocities. No matter how the particles rearrange their speeds and directions, the total count stays the same.

First Order — Symmetry of Flux

Compute the average velocity by weighting with $\mathbf{c}$:
(17.4)$$\int \mathbf{c} \, f^{\text{eq}}(\mathbf{c}) \, d\mathbf{c} = \rho \, \mathbf{u}$$
For a gas at rest ($\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}$), the mean velocity is zero. The bell curve is symmetric — as many particles move left as right, up as down. There is no net flux. For diffusion problems in cardiac EP, this is exactly the equilibrium condition: at rest, the distribution is centered and symmetric.

Second Order — Isotropic Variance

Compute the velocity covariance (the "spread" of the bell curve):
(17.5)$$\int (c_\alpha - u_\alpha)(c_\beta - u_\beta) \, f^{\text{eq}}(\mathbf{c}) \, d\mathbf{c} = \rho \, c_s^2 \, \delta_{\alpha\beta}$$
The variance is isotropic — the bell curve has the same width in every direction. The $\delta_{\alpha\beta}$ (Kronecker delta: 1 if $\alpha = \beta$, 0 otherwise) means there is no preferred axis. The parameter $c_s^2$ is the variance of the Gaussian, and it is the same along $x$, $y$, and $z$. This isotropy is a physical claim: at thermodynamic equilibrium, no direction is special. The random thermal motion of particles is equally vigorous in all directions. This will later impose constraints on the lattice weights and will be the reason why the standard LBM lattice produces isotropic diffusion by default.
Three orders, three physics: The Gaussian equilibrium encodes conservation (zeroth order), zero net flux (first order), and isotropic variance (second order). These are not abstract mathematical properties — they are the defining characteristics of thermodynamic equilibrium. Every lattice-Boltzmann scheme must respect all three, because they are the physical laws that the scheme must recover at the macroscopic level. In Section 17.4, we will see that these three conditions are exactly the constraints that determine the lattice weights $w_i$ and velocities $\mathbf{c}_i$.

Two Cases: Rest and Flow

The Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution (17.2) handles two physically distinct situations: Case 1: System at rest ($\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}$). The bell curve is centered at the origin of velocity space. No bulk motion — particles jiggle randomly in all directions with equal probability. This is the relevant case for diffusion: cardiac tissue conducts electrical signals by ion diffusion, not bulk fluid flow. The equilibrium distribution simplifies to:
(17.6)$$f^{\text{eq}}(\mathbf{c}) = \frac{\rho}{(2\pi c_s^2)^{D/2}} \exp\!\left(-\frac{|\mathbf{c}|^2}{2c_s^2}\right)$$
a symmetric Gaussian centered at the origin. This is the equilibrium that our cardiac LBM will use. Case 2: System with bulk flow ($\mathbf{u} \neq \mathbf{0}$). The bell curve is shifted — its peak is at $\mathbf{u}$ instead of at the origin. More particles are moving in the flow direction than against it. This is the relevant case for fluid dynamics (Navier-Stokes). The distribution is still a Gaussian, but the center has moved. The shift breaks the first-order symmetry: $\int \mathbf{c} \, f^{\text{eq}} \, d\mathbf{c} = \rho\mathbf{u} \neq \mathbf{0}$.
Why $c_s$ is the bridge between micro and macro: The parameter $c_s$ plays a dual role. In the continuous Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution, it is the thermal velocity — the standard deviation of the velocity Gaussian, set by the temperature of the gas ($c_s^2 = k_B T / m$). When we discretize onto a lattice, $c_s$ becomes the lattice speed of sound ($c_s^2 = c^2/3$ where $c = \Delta x / \Delta t$). This is not a coincidence: the Chapman-Enskog analysis (Section 17.2) shows that the lattice speed of sound must equal the Gaussian's variance parameter for the discrete scheme to recover the correct macroscopic transport equations. The connection $c_s^2 = c^2/3$ is a fixed consequence of the lattice geometry and the quadrature exactness conditions (Section 17.4).

Why the Equilibrium Is the Heart of LBM

In FDM, FEM, and FVM, the physics enters through the PDE — you discretize the diffusion equation or the monodomain equation directly. In LBM, the physics enters through the equilibrium distribution. The entire method can be summarized in one sentence: Relax the velocity distribution toward the Maxwell-Boltzmann equilibrium, and the correct macroscopic PDE emerges automatically. This is why we have spent this section building the equilibrium from physical principles rather than just writing down a formula. The Gaussian is not an arbitrary choice — it is the unique distribution that satisfies the constraints of thermodynamic equilibrium (maximum entropy subject to conservation of mass, momentum, and energy). Every property of LBM — the lattice weights, the velocity sets, the collision operator, the transport coefficients — traces back to this Gaussian. In the sections that follow, we will:
  • Section 17.2: Write down the equation that governs how $f$ evolves toward this equilibrium (the Boltzmann equation), and simplify it for practical computation (the BGK collision operator).
  • Section 17.3: Discretize velocity space onto a lattice, turning the continuous $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t)$ into a finite set of distributions $f_i(\mathbf{x}, t)$, and write the lattice-Boltzmann equation.
  • Section 17.4: Return to the Gaussian equilibrium and ask: what constraints do equations (17.3)–(17.5) impose on the discrete lattice? This will determine the weights $w_i$, velocity vectors $\mathbf{c}_i$, and the discrete equilibrium $f_i^{\text{eq}}$.
  • Section 17.5: Introduce moment space — a representation that reveals which physical laws the collision operator is actually enforcing, and how to control them independently.

17.2   The Boltzmann Equation and Kinetic Theory

The Boltzmann Equation: Fundamental Form

The Boltzmann equation describes how the distribution function $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t)$ — introduced in Section 17.1 — evolves in time. In the Eulerian frame (you stand at a fixed point in phase space and watch the distribution change around you), it reads:
(17.7)$$\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \nabla f + \mathbf{F} \cdot \nabla_{\mathbf{c}} f = \Omega(f)$$
Let us parse each term on the left-hand side:
  • $\frac{\partial f}{\partial t}$: How the distribution changes in time at a fixed point in phase space.
  • $\mathbf{c} \cdot \nabla f$: Particles streaming through physical space at velocity $\mathbf{c}$. This is advection: a particle at $\mathbf{x}$ with velocity $\mathbf{c}$ will be at $\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{c}\Delta t$ after time $\Delta t$.
  • $\mathbf{F} \cdot \nabla_{\mathbf{c}} f$: External forces accelerating particles, changing their velocities. The $\nabla_{\mathbf{c}}$ means the derivative is in velocity space, not physical space. This term accounts for gravity, electric fields, or any body force that shifts the velocity distribution.
The first two terms together form the material (total) derivative familiar from fluid mechanics — the rate of change following a particle that streams at velocity $\mathbf{c}$. The third term is separate: it represents forces that change the particle's velocity, pushing it through velocity space. If we define the streaming derivative as
$$\frac{Df}{Dt} \;\equiv\; \frac{\partial f}{\partial t} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \nabla f$$
then equation (17.7) can be written compactly as
(17.7a)$$\frac{Df}{Dt} + \mathbf{F} \cdot \nabla_{\mathbf{c}} f = \Omega(f)$$
This Lagrangian perspective is often more intuitive: ride along with a particle that streams at constant velocity $\mathbf{c}$. From its frame, the distribution $f$ changes for only two reasons — external forces push particles to different velocities, and collisions reshuffle the velocity population. If there were no forces and no collisions, $f$ would be carried unchanged along the streaming trajectory, like a ball on a frictionless surface. For LBM applications (diffusion, monodomain), we will drop the external force term entirely (no body forces accelerate particles through velocity space). This makes the Eulerian form (17.7) the natural starting point for deriving the lattice-Boltzmann equation. The right-hand side is the collision operator: The symbol $\Omega(f)$ represents collisions. When particles collide, their velocities change. The full collision integral is:
(17.8)$$\Omega(f) = \int \int (f'f'_1 - f f_1) \, g \, B(g, \theta) \, d\Omega \, d\mathbf{c}_1$$
The notation is intimidating. Here is what it means:
  • $f$ and $f_1$: the distribution functions of two particles BEFORE collision.
  • $f'$ and $f'_1$: the distributions of those same two particles AFTER collision.
  • $g = |\mathbf{c} - \mathbf{c}_1|$: the relative speed of the two particles.
  • $B(g, \theta)$: the scattering cross-section, which depends on the relative velocity and scattering angle $\theta$.
  • $d\Omega$: integration over all possible scattering angles.
  • $d\mathbf{c}_1$: integration over all velocities of the second particle.
The expression $(f'f'_1 - ff_1)$ is a balance: the gain term $f'f'_1$ counts particles scattered INTO the state $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c})$ by collisions, while the loss term $ff_1$ counts particles scattered OUT.

The BGK Simplification

The collision integral (17.8) is a 5-dimensional integral — computationally intractable. For a coarse 3D grid with $100^3$ points and even a modest $10^3$ velocities per point, evaluating it requires $\sim 10^{15}$ operations per timestep. For most of the 20th century, the Boltzmann equation remained a theoretical tool, not a practical one. The breakthrough came from Bhatnagar, Gross, and Krook in 1954. They asked: Can we replace the complex collision integral with something simple that still produces the correct macroscopic physics? Their answer was to replace the collision integral with a single relaxation toward the equilibrium distribution we met in Section 17.1:
(17.11)$$\Omega(f) \approx -\frac{1}{\tau}(f - f^{\text{eq}})$$
where $\tau$ is a single relaxation time and $f^{\text{eq}}$ is the Maxwell-Boltzmann equilibrium (equation 17.2). The idea is elegantly simple: the actual distribution $f$ is nudged toward its local equilibrium at a rate determined by $\tau$. No detailed calculation of scattering angles or cross-sections is needed. This is the BGK approximation. Notice the direct connection to Section 17.1: the $f^{\text{eq}}$ that appears here is exactly the Gaussian equilibrium (17.2) — the bell curve in velocity space that encodes conservation, zero flux, and isotropic variance. The BGK operator says: whatever the current distribution looks like, push it toward that Gaussian. The three physical claims we established in Section 17.1 — equations (17.3), (17.4), and (17.5) — are the properties that $f^{\text{eq}}$ carries into the collision step. Substituting (17.11) into (17.7), we get the BGK Boltzmann equation:
(17.12)$$\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \nabla f = -\frac{1}{\tau}(f - f^{\text{eq}})$$
where we have dropped the external force term $\mathbf{F} \cdot \nabla_{\mathbf{c}} f$ (in LBM for diffusion, there are no external forces accelerating particles through velocity space).

Why BGK Works: Chapman-Enskog Analysis

Why should such a drastic simplification — replacing a 5-dimensional integral with a simple relaxation — give the right macroscopic physics? The answer comes from Chapman-Enskog analysis, one of the deepest results in statistical mechanics. The idea: assume the distribution function is close to equilibrium, with small corrections: $$f = f^{\text{eq}} + \epsilon f^{(1)} + \epsilon^2 f^{(2)} + \cdots$$ where $\epsilon$ is a small parameter measuring how far the system is from equilibrium (technically, the Knudsen number $\text{Kn} = \lambda / L$, the ratio of mean free path to system size). Then expand the Boltzmann equation order by order. What emerges at each order is a specific piece of the macroscopic equations: Zeroth order ($\epsilon^0$): the distribution equals the equilibrium $f = f^{\text{eq}}$. Taking the zeroth and first moments of $f^{\text{eq}}$ (exactly the integrals from equations 17.3 and 17.4) gives the conservation laws: $$\frac{\partial \rho}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\rho \mathbf{u}) = 0 \qquad \text{(mass conservation)}$$ These are the Euler equations — ideal fluid dynamics with no dissipation. First order ($\epsilon^1$): the correction $f^{(1)}$ introduces gradients of velocity and temperature. Taking moments of $f^{(1)}$ produces the dissipative terms — this is where viscosity and diffusivity enter:
(17.9)$$\rho \frac{D\mathbf{u}}{Dt} = -\nabla p + \underbrace{\eta \nabla^2 \mathbf{u}}_{\text{viscous stress}} + \mathbf{f}$$
for fluid flow (Navier-Stokes), and
(17.10)$$\frac{\partial T}{\partial t} + \mathbf{u} \cdot \nabla T = \underbrace{D \nabla^2 T}_{\text{diffusion}}$$
for heat or mass diffusion. The underbraced terms are the ones that the first-order correction adds — they are absent in the Euler (zeroth-order) equations. The transport coefficients (viscosity $\eta$, diffusivity $D$) are determined by the relaxation time $\tau$. Large $\tau$ (slow relaxation toward equilibrium) gives high viscosity or diffusivity; small $\tau$ gives low viscosity or diffusivity. The relationship is precise and will be derived explicitly in Section 17.4 as the $\tau$-$D$ relationship.
The key insight: The macroscopic PDEs we have been solving throughout this textbook — the diffusion equation, the monodomain equation, even Navier-Stokes — are not fundamental. They are emergent. They arise as the zeroth- and first-order limits of the Boltzmann equation in the collision-dominated regime. The zeroth-order (equilibrium) contribution gives conservation laws. The first-order (near-equilibrium) correction gives the dissipative transport terms. The equilibrium $f^{\text{eq}}$ carries the conservation physics (17.3)–(17.5); the relaxation rate $1/\tau$ sets the transport coefficients. Together, they recover the full macroscopic PDE.
Why Chapman-Enskog Works: The expansion assumes the distribution is locally near equilibrium, with small non-hydrodynamic corrections. The collision operator acts to restore equilibrium. By expanding around this equilibrium and using the fact that the collision operator has specific zero eigenvalues (corresponding to conserved quantities like mass and momentum), one can systematically extract the macroscopic equations order by order in the Knudsen number.

17.3   The Lattice-Boltzmann Equation: Single Relaxation Time (SRT/BGK)

The Core Strategy of LBM
  1. Start from the kinetic level: the Boltzmann equation governs how $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{c}, t)$ evolves in phase space (Section 17.2).
  2. Simplify the collision: replace the intractable collision integral with BGK relaxation toward $f^{\text{eq}}$ — the Maxwell-Boltzmann Gaussian from Section 17.1 (Section 17.2).
  3. Discretize velocity space: replace the continuous $\mathbf{c}$ with a small finite set of lattice velocities $\mathbf{c}_i$ and write the discrete update rule (this section).
  4. Derive the lattice from the Gaussian: the weights $w_i$, velocity sets $\mathbf{c}_i$, and discrete equilibrium $f_i^{\text{eq}}$ all follow from quadrature on the Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution (Section 17.4).
